# Configuration & Environment

> The 11 designs (environment, property-sources, profiles, property-resolution, origin-tracking, value-injection, config-data, relaxed-binding, binder, config-properties, config-metadata) cover the spine of Spring's config model with strong intent-fidelity and unusually careful boundary-drawing. Coverage of the core mechanics — first-source-wins/never-blend, the read/mutate seam, the profile expression grammar + three levers + reserved-default, the single-pass Config Data engine, relaxed binding's UNIFORM-form equivalence, the Bindable/BindResult/BindHandler trio, two-altitude origin tracking, and compile-time metadata — is essentially complete. The designs are also internally consistent on the BIG cross-cutting questions: they CONSISTENTLY defer (not contradict) the seven Phase-2 keystones (scope/lifetime/ownership handle shape, the read/mutate seam mechanism, the cross-crate discovery substrate, the async pre-materialization pipeline, the metadata/codegen boundary, the strict-vs-lenient default, and the handler-substrate unification). The genuine gaps are (a) several concrete Spring SOURCE TYPES and bootstrap mechanics that are not generic Maps and have their own grammar/behavior (command-line args, random.*, SPRING_APPLICATION_JSON, the ConfigurationPropertySource adapter, @PropertySource, spring.main self-binding + refresh re-ordering), and (b) a handful of boundary concepts (the #{SpEL} dispatch owner, placeholder escaping, on-cloud-platform) that are named as neighbours but have no owner inside this area. The coherence issues are mostly unifications the designs already flag plus a few latent boundary ambiguities (enumerable-facet shape duplicated across three features, the include-per-source transport-vs-algebra seam, collection-replacement needing a contract not yet pinned).

_21 feature designs. Implementation-agnostic — candidate realizations for Phase 2/3, not decisions._

## @Value Injection  `[value-injection]`

Spring's `@Value` is the SCATTERED-SCALAR injection point: a per-field/per-parameter annotation that pulls one externalized value into a bean, with three composable value sources inside one string and a conversion at the end. The doc (§"`@Value` injection" + the lenient-resolver hidden concept) pins six load-bearing intents to replicate as INTENT, not mechanism: (1) THREE SOURCE SHAPES in one annotation string — `${property}` placeholder resolution (delegated to the property-resolution neighbour), `#{SpEL}` expression evaluation (delegated to expression-language), and a literal/default value; these COMPOSE (`#{...}` can wrap a `${...}`, and Spring resolves `${...}` first then `#{...}`). (2) DEFAULTS via the `${prop:default}` colon form — owned by property-resolution's placeholder grammar, but `@Value` is the consumer that exposes it at the injection point. (3) CONVERSION-AT-THE-INJECTION-POINT — the resolved raw string is coerced to the *declared field/param type* (`String`→`int`, comma-separated→`String[]`/`List`) via the type-conversion neighbour; the target type is the field's type, which in Rust is STATICALLY KNOWN at the injection site — the crux of the rust-native opportunity. (4) IT IS AN INJECTION-POINT VARIANT, not a separate subsystem — in Spring's `doResolveDependency` it is *step 2* (`getSuggestedValue`), sitting alongside by-type autowiring (autowiring-resolution / injection-styles neighbours) on the SAME constructor/setter/field seam, processed by a `BeanPostProcessor` (post-processor-spi). So `@Value` must ride leaf's ordinary injection mechanism (constructor-preferred, setter, field), not a magic side channel. (5) THE LENIENT-VS-STRICT FOOT-GUN — Spring's default embedded resolver is LENIENT (an unresolved `${catalog.name}` is injected as the literal text, silently wrong); registering a `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer` makes it strict. leaf must surface this as an explicit, first-class policy with a rich diagnostic (charter #9) and decide whether the DEFAULT flips to strict. (6) `@Value` IS container-expression-capable and EAGER/per-field, deliberately DISTINCT from `@ConfigurationProperties` (config-properties neighbour), which is inert data with NO expression evaluation, whole-object, validated. The philosophical split — "`@Value` is code-ish container expression, config-properties is inert data" — must survive into leaf. SCOPE BOUNDARY: this feature owns ONLY the injection-point seam — the `@Value`-equivalent attribute, the ORCHESTRATION of placeholder→expression→conversion at one point, the default/required/optional semantics at that point, where strict/lenient policy is carried, and how the resolved value lands in a constructor arg / field. It does NOT own the `${...}` walk (property-resolution), the `#{...}` engine (expression-language), string→T coercion (type-conversion), the env read seam (environment), or whole-object binding (config-properties/binder). The genuinely divergent design axis for THIS feature is HOW the injection point is represented in Rust given the target type is statically known: a thin macro that LOWERS each `#[value(...)]` site to a typed expression at compile time, vs a runtime metadata descriptor the container interprets during wiring, vs a hybrid where the literal is parsed/typed at compile time but resolved against the runtime environment.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Macro-lowered typed injection: #[value("...")] compiles to a hand-writable resolve-and-convert expression
Treat each `@Value`-equivalent site as a COMPILE-TIME-LOWERED Rust expression whose output type is the declared field/param type (statically known). A thin attribute macro on a constructor param / struct field / setter emits exactly the code a user could hand-write against public APIs (charter #10): split the literal into its `${...}` / `#{...}` / literal segments at MACRO TIME (the grammar split, not the resolution — resolution is runtime against the live env), then emit `let __v: <FieldTy> = leaf_core::value::resolve_into::<FieldTy>(&env, ValueSpec { segments, policy }, &convert_ctx)?;`. Because `<FieldTy>` is known, conversion is monomorphized (`FieldTy: FromConfigValue` from the type-conversion neighbour's trait-native path) — zero registry lookup, compile error if the field type has no conversion. The `ValueSpec` carries the pre-split segment list (Literal | Placeholder{raw} | Expr{raw}) so the runtime call only does the resolution walk, never re-parses the grammar shape. Example: `#[value("${server.port:8080}")] port: u16` lowers to a placeholder-resolve (property-resolution) → `u16::from_config_value`; `#[value("#{T(num_cpus)::get() * 2}")]` lowers to an expression-language closure → convert. Strict/lenient is a per-site attribute knob (`#[value("...", strict)]`) defaulting to the framework policy, baked into `ValueSpec.policy`. Malformed grammar (`${unbalanced`) is a COMPILE error at the attribute span. The site participates in ordinary injection: the macro registers a constructor-arg/field descriptor exactly like a by-type dependency (injection-styles neighbour), but tagged as a value-source rather than a bean-source — so `@Value` is literally 'step 2' on the same seam.

**Pros:** Best low-overhead + earliest-diagnostic combo (charter #2/#9): target type static → monomorphized conversion, no erased Value, no TypeId lookup; malformed `${...}`/`#{...}` and a field type lacking conversion both fail at COMPILE time at the exact attribute span; Thin macro (charter #10): emits only a `resolve_into::<T>(...)` call a user could write by hand; `cargo expand` shows the plain segment list + typed convert; the programmatic escape hatch is just calling `resolve_into` directly; Rides ordinary injection (injection-styles / autowiring-resolution): a `@Value` site is a constructor-arg/field descriptor flavored 'value-source', not a parallel subsystem — matches Spring's 'step 2 on the same seam' intent exactly; Single-source-of-truth segment split at macro time means runtime never re-parses the `${}`/`#{}` shape — only resolves — keeping the wiring path lean and the grammar boundary (placeholder vs expression) decided once, legibly; Strict/lenient is a visible per-site knob over a framework default, surfacing Spring's hidden foot-gun as an explicit choice (charter #9), with a rich UnresolvedValue diagnostic naming the field, the spec, and sources searched

**Cons:** Diverges from Spring's runtime-BeanPostProcessor mental model: there is no single inspectable `@Value` processor bean a user can replace; 'register a placeholder configurer to go strict' becomes a macro/config knob — a different mechanism (intent-preserving but shape-diverging); The macro must know the field/param TYPE to emit `resolve_into::<FieldTy>` — fine for concrete types, but a generic field (`#[value] x: T`) where `T` is a type parameter forces the bound `T: FromConfigValue` onto the bean, leaking the conversion trait into user generics; Runtime-authored `@Value` strings (a value spec loaded from config at runtime, rare but real in Spring via programmatic `@Value`) are impossible in the lowered form — must fall back to the programmatic `resolve_into` API with a runtime-parsed spec (R2 territory); Default-segment semantics (`${prop:default}` where the default itself may contain placeholders/expressions) must be faithfully handed to property-resolution's recursive walk; the macro must NOT try to evaluate the default itself, only mark it — a boundary that's easy to get subtly wrong; The `env` handle the emitted code reads is the environment neighbour's read view (`&dyn PropertyResolver` / `Env`); this feature inherits whatever reachability/handle-shape decision environment makes (borrow vs Arc-clone), coupling the emitted code's signature to a deferred decision

**Mechanisms considered:** Thin attribute macro lowering each site to `resolve_into::<FieldTy>(&env, ValueSpec, &ctx)` (primary; grammar SPLIT at macro time, resolution at runtime); `ValueSpec` = pre-split `&'static [Segment]` (Literal/Placeholder/Expr) emitted as a const so no runtime re-parse; type-conversion's trait-native `FromConfigValue` for monomorphized convert at the known target type (no registry); Per-site `strict`/`lenient` attribute over a framework-default policy carried in `ValueSpec.policy`; Compile-time malformed-grammar detection (unbalanced `${`/`#{`) at the attribute span; Value-source descriptor registered on the SAME injection seam as by-type deps (injection-styles), flavored value-not-bean; stable-only: attribute macro emitting a const segment array + a monomorphized generic call; no nightly

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The resolution+convert logic lives in `leaf-core` (`resolve_into`, `ValueSpec`, `Segment`, `ValueError`), named by absolute path so emitted code hard-codes `::leaf_core::value::...` (proc-macro hygiene per the cross-crate doc). The macro itself holds ZERO logic (only grammar-split + emit), in `leaf-macros`. CRITICALLY: a `@Value` site needs NO cross-crate registry for the value-resolution path — it compiles and links wherever the bean is defined, with zero DCE/linker exposure (a major win, and full wasm support), because the env is consumed, not a discovered set. Cross-crate enters ONLY transitively: (a) the `env` read handle comes from the container (whatever registry substrate stores it — deferred); (b) a `#{...}` segment referencing a bean (`@beanName`) defers to expression-language's BeanResolver which rides whatever scoped discovery substrate leaf picks (linkme/inventory/build-codegen, with the Layer-0/DCE silent-empty hazard — that crate's problem, not this feature's); (c) IF leaf ever lets custom placeholder/expression syntax be contributed cross-crate, that rides the same link-time-registry-vs-build-codegen duality (default link-time slice + optional dynamic directory-scanned/WASM source) as component scanning — a CANDIDATE, not a decision. The value-source DESCRIPTOR (that a given constructor arg is a `@Value` not a by-type dep) is part of the bean's own injection metadata, registered with the bean's definition — so it shares the bean-discovery substrate but adds no new cross-crate surface of its own. Origin-agnostic: the env read handle is `&dyn PropertyResolver`, so a native, file, or future WASM-module property source feeds resolution identically.

**T2 — execution context:** Value resolution runs on the COLD wiring path — during bean instantiation (constructor/setter/field population), which is itself during refresh/eager-singleton init or prototype creation. It is SYNCHRONOUS, CPU-only, non-blocking: the `${...}` walk is an in-memory stack scan (property-resolution), `#{...}` is a sync pure closure/eval (expression-language contract), conversion is pure CPU (type-conversion). NOTHING here `.await`s or blocks (charter #3/#4) — any property source whose backing is remote/async must have been snapshotted into the env BEFORE wiring (the BootstrapContext discipline inherited from property-sources/environment). Errors are rich synchronous Results folded into the unified startup-diagnostics chain (charter #9): `UnresolvedValue { field, spec, sources_searched }` (strict mode), `ConvertError { raw, target_type, origin, field }`, `ExprError { ... }` — each naming the exact injection point. In LENIENT mode an unresolved placeholder degrades per Spring (literal/default), a policy carried on the spec. No cancellation concern (bounded pure work during a bounded cold step). Because resolution happens at construction time, a `@Value` on a prototype bean re-resolves on every instantiation (re-reading the possibly-mutated-but-sealed env) — semantically matching Spring's per-instance eager evaluation.

#### R2 — Runtime value-descriptor interpreted by the container (Spring-faithful BeanPostProcessor analogue)
Mirror Spring directly: a `@Value` site emits (at macro time) only a small RUNTIME DESCRIPTOR — `ValueInjectionPoint { target: FieldOrArgRef, spec_source: &'static str, target_type: TypeId, convert: fn(&ResolvedRaw, &ConvertCtx) -> Result<ErasedValue, ConvertError> }` — and a container post-processor (the leaf analogue of the `@Value`-handling `AutowiredAnnotationBeanPostProcessor`, post-processor-spi neighbour) INTERPRETS it during bean population. The post-processor parses the `spec_source` string at runtime (or once-and-caches), runs the placeholder walk (property-resolution) → expression eval (expression-language) → conversion (type-conversion's runtime ConversionService path), then writes the erased value into the target slot via a generated setter fn pointer. The `target_type: TypeId` keys the conversion; the per-site `convert` fn pointer is a monomorphized closure the macro emits so conversion is still typed at the boundary even though the descriptor is erased. Strict/lenient is a property of the post-processor / a container-level policy bean (faithfully mirroring 'register a PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer to go strict'). This makes `@Value` processing a single, inspectable, replaceable infrastructure step — a user can swap the value post-processor, observe every resolution, or add a custom value-source — exactly Spring's extensibility.

**Pros:** Highest intent-parity with Spring's actual mechanism (charter #2 says intent-not-mechanism, but this gives the familiar 'a single inspectable/replaceable `@Value` post-processor' model and the literal 'register-a-configurer-to-go-strict' DX for migrants); Only realization that cleanly supports RUNTIME-AUTHORED value specs (a `@Value` string composed/loaded at runtime) and uniform runtime observation of every value resolution — a natural hook for the condition-report/diagnostics lineage to log 'field X resolved to Y from source Z'; One ordered infrastructure step (post-processor-spi): `@Value` is dogfooded as a BPP-analogue exactly like Spring, proving leaf's post-processor SPI is powerful enough for its own features — strong unification signal (charter #11); Custom value-sources / a `random.*`-style synthetic resolver slot in as additional steps in the one post-processor's chain, rather than each `@Value` site baking its own logic — centralizes the resolution policy; Strict/lenient as a container-level policy bean is a single switch for the whole app, matching Spring's global PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer behavior rather than per-site knobs

**Cons:** Reintroduces exactly the erasure/boxing/TypeId-lookup that R1 eliminates (charter #2/#5): `ErasedValue = Box<dyn Any>`, runtime spec parsing, dyn dispatch in the post-processor — overhead on the wiring path the macro approach makes free, and against stack-over-heap discipline; Failure moves to RUNTIME: a malformed `${...}` or a field type lacking a converter is only caught during the startup wiring pass, not at compile time — weaker than R1 on the charter #9 timing ladder (still a fail-fast startup pass, acceptable per the tiered preference, but strictly later); The post-processor consults a shared conversion registry + env during parallel/eager bean init — a read-mostly shared structure that must be frozen-after-refresh to avoid a lock (charter #5); the per-site `convert` fn pointer mitigates but the spec-parse + registry path remains dynamic; Runtime string-parsing of every `@Value` spec at startup is wasted work for the 99% literal-attribute case that R1 splits at compile time — and re-parsing on every prototype instantiation unless cached on the descriptor; Carries the type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept INTO this feature (how is `target_type`/erased value stored/keyed/written), pre-coupling it to a deferred Phase-2 decision more than R1 does

**Mechanisms considered:** Macro emits a runtime `ValueInjectionPoint` descriptor (target ref + spec string + TypeId + monomorphized convert fn pointer); container post-processor interprets it; Post-processor as the leaf `@Value`-BPP analogue (post-processor-spi), runtime-replaceable + observable; `Box<dyn Any + Send + Sync>` erased value channel written into the target slot via a generated setter fn pointer; Runtime spec parse cached on the descriptor (parse-once) to avoid re-parsing per prototype instantiation; Strict/lenient as a container-level policy bean (the PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer analogue), not per-site; `const TypeId::of` (stable 1.91) for the descriptor's target_type key; Value-source post-processor step chained with synthetic-resolver steps (random.*) for centralized resolution policy

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The value post-processor + descriptor types live in `leaf-core`. This realization is MORE cross-crate-exposed than R1 because the value-handling post-processor is itself an infrastructure bean that must be DISCOVERED and auto-registered (the AnnotationConfigUtils analogue) — riding whatever post-processor discovery substrate leaf picks (deferred), with the Layer-0/DCE silent-empty hazard (if the value post-processor's crate isn't force-linked, `@Value` silently does nothing — a catastrophic silent failure, exactly the worst trait the cross-crate doc warns about). The per-site `ValueInjectionPoint` descriptor must also be discovered as part of the bean's injection metadata across crates. Custom value-sources contributed cross-crate ride the static-link-registry-vs-build-codegen duality (default link-time slice of `const ValueSourceDescriptor` + optional dynamic directory-scanned/WASM source) with the full DCE guardrails (const descriptors, runtime eval, force-link, zero-contribution self-check, order from descriptor DATA not link order). Origin-agnostic: erased values flow identically regardless of source origin. The strong T1 caveat: because the WHOLE feature hinges on a discovered post-processor being present, this realization most needs the self-check ('the value post-processor registered N sites; assert N matches expected') to avoid silently-broken `@Value`.

**T2 — execution context:** The value post-processor runs on the COLD wiring path during bean population (refresh/eager-init for singletons, per-call for prototypes), synchronous + non-blocking. Spec parse (if not pre-cached) is cold CPU; resolution walk + expression eval + conversion are sync non-blocking exactly as R1 (any remote/async source pre-snapshotted before wiring). The shared conversion registry + env are frozen-after-refresh so concurrent eager/background bean init reads them lock-free (charter #5). Errors propagate as rich synchronous Results into the unified startup-diagnostics chain (charter #9), surfaced at the startup validation pass (later than R1's compile-time catch for malformed grammar). If the post-processor itself is discovered via inventory (life-before-main), the SUBMISSION must be a trivial non-panicking descriptor (per the cross-crate doc) with all real work deferred to the runtime post-processing pass. No cancellation concern (bounded). The erased-value write into the target slot must be a synchronous, panic-free fn-pointer call; a wrong-type downcast is a fail-fast diagnostic, not UB.

#### R3 — Typed Value<T> deferral primitive: @Value as an injectable handle, eager-by-default with explicit laziness
Reframe `@Value` not as 'write a string into a field' but as 'inject a typed value handle resolved from the environment', unifying with the deferral-primitives neighbour. The macro lowers `#[value("...")] port: u16` to EAGER resolution by default (resolve-and-store the `u16` at construction, as R1), BUT the SAME machinery exposes injectable handle types for the non-eager cases Spring covers awkwardly: `Value<u16>` (eager-resolved newtype carrying the typed value + its origin for diagnostics), `LazyValue<u16>` (resolves on first `.get()`, for an expensive `#{...}` expression or to break an init-order issue — the deferral-primitives `@Lazy`/ObjectProvider analogue at the value level), and `RequiredValue<u16>` vs `Option<T>` field (encoding Spring's required-vs-optional at the TYPE level — a missing `${x}` with no default makes `Option<u16>` = `None` but `RequiredValue<u16>` a fail-fast error, mapping Spring's null-never-overrides + required semantics into Rust's type system). All handle types share ONE primitive: a `ResolvedValue<T>` produced by the same `resolve_into::<T>` core as R1, differing only in WHEN it resolves and HOW absence is encoded. The default (bare `T` field) stays eager-at-construction for Spring parity; the handle types are the explicit escape hatches (charter #10). Strict/lenient: `RequiredValue<T>`/bare-`T` are effectively strict (absence = error), `Option<T>`/`LazyValue<T>` carry a lenient default — encoding the policy in the TARGET TYPE rather than a separate knob, the most rust-native expression of the foot-gun.

**Pros:** Encodes Spring's required/optional/lazy value semantics in the TYPE SYSTEM (charter #1 DX + #9): `Option<T>` = optional, `RequiredValue<T>`/bare `T` = mandatory-fail-fast, `LazyValue<T>` = deferred — the strict/lenient foot-gun becomes a visible type choice, not a hidden global mode; Unifies with deferral-primitives (charter #11): `LazyValue<T>` is the value-level analogue of `@Lazy`/ObjectProvider, sharing one deferral mechanism rather than a bespoke per-feature lazy path — and `Value<T>` carrying origin serves origin-tracking diagnostics for free; Keeps the zero-cost eager default (bare `T`, monomorphized convert as R1) while giving genuine escape hatches for the expensive-expression / break-init-order cases Spring handles with `@Lazy` on a `@Value` (which Spring's own docs call 'rather limited'); `LazyValue<T>` is the clean home for an EXPENSIVE `#{...}` expression that shouldn't run at construction — deferring the expression-language eval (and any cold async pre-materialization of its inputs) to first use, off the eager wiring path; Origin-carrying `Value<T>` handles make 'where did this value come from' answerable at the injection point (origin-tracking neighbour) without a separate query — strengthening diagnostics (charter #9)

**Cons:** Most NOVEL surface vs Spring: Spring has no `Value<T>`/`LazyValue<T>`/`RequiredValue<T>` handle family — a Spring migrant must learn leaf's type-encoded semantics; risks over-proliferating handle types for a feature whose 95% case is 'inject a scalar' (tension with charter #2 intent-shape and #5 don't-over-build); `LazyValue<T>` reintroduces an execution-context question (charter #3): if its deferred resolution can run on a hot path at first `.get()`, and the `#{...}` expression's inputs need cold async pre-materialization, the lazy handle must NOT do blocking/async resolution at `.get()` on a hot path — forcing either a strictly-sync-resolvable contract or a deferred-but-pre-materialized model, more complex than eager; The handle types are newtypes around `ResolvedValue<T>` — small heap/indirection for `LazyValue` (needs a OnceCell/OnceLock + the unresolved spec) vs R1's bare value; justified only where laziness is genuinely wanted, so the default must stay bare-`T` to honor charter #5; Encoding strict/lenient in the type collides with a possible framework-global policy (R2's container-level switch): if both exist, which wins for a bare `T` field — the global default or the type? A policy-precedence question this realization creates; Requires the deferral-primitives neighbour's model to be settled enough to share (`LazyValue<T>` ≈ value-level ObjectProvider) — a coupling that's really a Phase-2 unification call, presented here as a candidate shape not a decision

**Mechanisms considered:** One `resolve_into::<T>` core (shared with R1) producing `ResolvedValue<T>`, wrapped by handle types differing in WHEN/HOW; Eager bare-`T` default (zero-cost, monomorphized) + `Value<T>` (eager + origin) + `LazyValue<T>` (OnceCell-backed first-use resolve) + `RequiredValue<T>`/`Option<T>` for required-vs-optional encoding; strict/lenient encoded in the TARGET TYPE (absence-as-error vs absence-as-None) rather than a separate knob; `LazyValue<T>` as the value-level analogue of deferral-primitives' `@Lazy`/ObjectProvider (shared deferral mechanism); `OnceLock`/`OnceCell` (stable) for lazy memoized resolution; `Option<T>` for natural optionality; origin-carrying `Value<T>` for origin-tracking diagnostics at the injection point; stable-only: newtypes, OnceLock, generics; no nightly

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same kernel-residency as R1 (`resolve_into`, `ResolvedValue`, the handle types `Value`/`LazyValue`/`RequiredValue` in `leaf-core`, absolute-path-emitted by the macro). T1 profile is essentially R1's — the eager path needs NO cross-crate registry and has zero DCE/linker exposure (full wasm support); the value-source descriptor rides the bean's own injection metadata. The handle types add no new cross-crate surface: they are ordinary generic types resolved at the call site by the compiler (like type-conversion's trait-native path), not a discovered set — so `LazyValue<T>` works wherever its `T: FromConfigValue` is coherent, same orphan-rule considerations as conversion. The `LazyValue<T>` first-use resolution still borrows/clones the env read handle (environment neighbour's reachability decision) and, if its `#{...}` references a bean, defers to the expression-language BeanResolver's scoped discovery substrate (with the Layer-0/DCE hazard) exactly as R1 — no new exposure. Origin-agnostic: `Value<T>` carries an `Origin` regardless of whether the backing source is native/file/WASM. If laziness ever needs cross-crate-contributed value-sources, same link-time-registry/duality + guardrails as R1/R2 apply (candidate, not decision).

**T2 — execution context:** Eager handles (bare `T`, `Value<T>`, `RequiredValue<T>`, `Option<T>`) resolve on the COLD wiring path exactly as R1 — synchronous, non-blocking, rich Results into the startup-diagnostics chain. The distinctive T2 concern is `LazyValue<T>`: its `.get()` may fire on a HOT path (steady-state, inside request handling), so its deferred resolution contract MUST be synchronous + non-blocking (placeholder walk + sync expression eval + convert) — it may NOT `.await` or block at `.get()` (charter #3/#4). Any `#{...}` expression whose inputs need remote/async data must have those inputs cold-async-pre-materialized into the env BEFORE the lazy handle is first touched (BootstrapContext discipline); the lazy handle memoizes via `OnceLock` so the resolution+convert runs at most once (cold-ish first-touch), and subsequent `.get()`s are a lock-free read (charter #4). A lazy resolution FAILURE at first `.get()` is a rich Result/Error at the call site (later than startup — a deliberate trade for genuinely deferred values, acceptable per charter #9's tiered timing). No cancellation concern for the bounded sync resolve; cross-thread reach of a `LazyValue<T>` requires the captured env handle be `Send+Sync` (environment's reachability decision), and the `OnceLock` makes the memoized value safely publishable across `.await`/threads.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's `@Value` conversion exists partly because the JVM only knows the target type as a runtime `Class<?>`; the `ConversionService` routes around erasure. In Rust the injection-point target type is ALWAYS statically known (the field/param type), so a runtime conversion-registry lookup (R2) solves a problem Rust doesn't have — faithfully porting it reintroduces erasure/boxing the type system would eliminate (charter #2/#5). The trait-native lowered path (R1/R3) is the rust-native fit; whether a runtime path is needed at all is entangled with the deferred type-erasure & registry-substrate meta-concept.
- Spring's lenient-by-default resolver SILENTLY injects an unresolved `${catalog.name}` as the literal string — the doc flags this as a classic bug source. This is in direct TENSION with charter #9 (fail with rich actionable context, as early as the richest diagnostic allows) and with Rust's typed fail-fast ethos. leaf can model both modes for intent parity but must DECIDE whether the default flips to strict — and Rust makes the choice sharper: an unresolved placeholder in Rust is naturally an `Err`/`None`, so silently producing a literal string requires deliberately suppressing that — a foot-gun leaf would have to opt INTO rather than fall into.
- Spring processes `@Value` via a reflective `BeanPostProcessor` that writes into a field by reflection. Rust has no reflective field write; the value must land via a constructor argument, a generated setter, or a typed field initializer the macro emits — so 'field injection' for `@Value` cannot be a magic side channel (charter #10) and is most naturally constructor/initializer-shaped, nudging the same direction Spring's docs already prefer (constructor over field injection).
- `@Value` composes `${...}` then `#{...}` in one string, with Spring resolving placeholders FIRST then the expression. In Rust the clean factoring separates the placeholder engine (property-resolution), the expression engine (expression-language), and the type-conversion seam — so the composition that Spring fuses into one resolver must be an explicit ORCHESTRATION across three neighbour features, and WHO owns the `${}`-vs-`#{}` split/ordering must be decided (this feature orchestrates, but the dispatch grammar boundary is shared with expression-language).
- Spring's `null`/absent-property fall-through and the required-vs-optional `@Value` distinction are runtime/reflective. Rust models them sharply with `Option<T>` (absent = None) vs a required type (absent = error) — a clarity win (R3 encodes this in the type), but it forces leaf to be explicit that a source returning `Some("")` is present-and-wins, NOT absence (preserving Spring's monotonic-override invariant) — the type system makes the distinction crisper than Java's null but demands the contract be stated.
- Spring's `@Value` is processed eagerly at bean construction with the env reachable globally via the context. In Rust, reading the environment at construction requires the bean to hold/borrow the env read handle (`&dyn PropertyResolver` / `Env`) — 'reachable everywhere' collides with ownership: a `LazyValue<T>` that resolves later must capture a `Send+Sync` env handle (likely an `Arc`-backed clone), reintroducing the Arc the memory-discipline charter (#5) wants to avoid — the reachability question is the environment neighbour's deferred keystone applied to value injection.
- Spring lets `@Value` evaluate a `#{...}` expression that may call bean methods or do non-trivial work at construction time on the JVM where blocking is tolerated. leaf's async-first + hot-path-no-block discipline (charter #3/#4) FORBIDS a `@Value` resolution (placeholder walk, expression eval, or a `LazyValue.get()` on a hot path) that blocks or awaits — so any value whose backing is remote/async must be pre-materialized into the env during a cold async pre-step BEFORE wiring, narrowing what a `@Value` expression can do at resolve time versus Spring.

### Interactions
- **property-resolution** — HARD DEPENDENCY and primary delegate. The `${property}` / `${prop:default}` portion of a `@Value` string is resolved by THIS feature handing the placeholder segment to property-resolution's `resolve_placeholders` / `resolve_required_placeholders` walk (defaults via `:`, recursion, cycle detection, strict/lenient). This feature owns the INJECTION-POINT orchestration and the default exposure; property-resolution owns the actual `${...}` walk. The strict/lenient policy decided here is the same policy that selects property-resolution's lenient-leave-literal vs strict-fail tail.
- **expression-language** — HARD DEPENDENCY and primary delegate for the `#{SpEL}` portion. A `#{...}` segment is evaluated by expression-language (R1 lowers it to a typed closure; R2 calls the engine). Spring resolves `${...}` FIRST then `#{...}` within one string — so this feature owns the split/ordering of the two engines inside a `@Value` string (the `${}`-before-`#{}` dispatch), while expression-language owns `#{...}` evaluation. The evaluate-then-convert chain (expression result → type-conversion) lands at this injection point. Boundary: who owns the `${}`/`#{}` dispatch grammar is shared and flagged.
- **type-conversion** — HARD DEPENDENCY and final step. The resolved raw value (after placeholder + expression) is coerced to the DECLARED field/param type at this injection point — Spring's 'conversion at the injection point'. This feature owns the resolve-to-raw + the call site where the target type is known; type-conversion owns String→T (numbers, comma-separated→Vec, Duration/DataSize/enums). R1/R3 use the trait-native monomorphized `FromConfigValue` (target type static); R2 uses the runtime ConversionService. ConvertError carries the field, raw value, target type, and origin (charter #9).
- **environment** — Upstream supplier of the read view. `@Value` resolution reads through environment's `PropertyResolver`/`Env` read handle (the consume-only seam). This feature is a PRIMARY consumer of that handle (environment explicitly lists value-injection as the `${property}` reader). The handle SHAPE (borrowed `&dyn PropertyResolver` vs Arc-clone `Env`) and its cross-`.await`/thread reachability are environment's deferred decision; this feature's emitted code (R1) and `LazyValue` capture (R3) inherit it.
- **injection-styles** — This feature IS an injection-point variant riding the same constructor/setter/field seam. A `@Value` site is a constructor-arg/field/setter descriptor flavored 'value-source' rather than 'bean-source' — in Spring's `doResolveDependency` it is step 2 alongside by-type autowiring. leaf must treat `@Value` as ordinary injection (constructor-preferred, the rust-native nudge), NOT a parallel subsystem; injection-styles owns the seam mechanics, this feature contributes the value-source flavor.
- **autowiring-resolution** — Sibling on the same resolution pipeline. Spring's `doResolveDependency` tries a `@Value` suggested-value (step 2) BEFORE by-type candidate finding. leaf's resolution must check for a value-source flavor on the injection point and resolve it via this feature, short-circuiting the by-type candidate search. This feature defines what 'a value-source injection point' means; autowiring-resolution owns the surrounding by-type policy.
- **post-processor-spi** — In Spring `@Value` is processed by a `BeanPostProcessor` (the dogfooding intent — features built on the public extension points). R2 realizes this literally (a discoverable value post-processor). leaf must decide whether `@Value` is dogfooded as a post-processor-analogue (R2, runtime, inspectable, replaceable) or lowered at compile time (R1/R3, no post-processor bean). Either way the conceptual 'decorate the product during population' tier is where value resolution sits if runtime.
- **config-properties** — Deliberate philosophical CONTRAST, not a dependency. `@Value` is per-field, eager, expression-capable (code-ish container expression); `@ConfigurationProperties` is whole-object, validated, documented, and NOTABLY does NOT evaluate `#{...}` (config is inert data). leaf must preserve this split: this feature's expression-capability is exactly what config-properties refuses. Guidance mirrors Spring's: prefer config-properties for related setting groups, reserve `@Value` for isolated scalars or genuine expression needs.
- **deferral-primitives** — R3 unifies here: `LazyValue<T>` is the value-level analogue of `@Lazy`/ObjectProvider, sharing one deferral mechanism. The required-vs-optional `@Value` encoding (`Option<T>` vs `RequiredValue<T>`) parallels deferral-primitives' optional (0..1) / required modeling in the type signature. Whether value-injection shares deferral-primitives' machinery (vs a bespoke lazy value) is a Phase-2 unification call; flagged.
- **origin-tracking** — Diagnostics dependency. A resolved value should carry provenance (which source/file:line) so a `@Value` conversion or strict-resolution failure reports 'cannot resolve ${server.port} for field X (application.yaml:12)'. R3's `Value<T>` handle carries origin at the injection point explicitly. This feature surfaces origin alongside the resolved value to satisfy charter #9; origin-tracking owns the provenance wrapping.
- **bean-instantiation** — Execution-context host. `@Value` resolution runs DURING bean instantiation/population (constructor args, field init) on the cold wiring path — eager-singleton init at refresh, or per-call for prototypes (re-resolving each time, matching Spring's per-instance eager evaluation). bean-instantiation owns the construction lock/three-level-cache and the timing; this feature contributes the value-resolution step that runs within construction.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- DEFAULT mode — lenient or strict for unresolved `@Value` placeholders? Spring defaults to lenient (inject the literal string, a documented foot-gun) in direct tension with charter #9. Should leaf flip to strict by default, keep Spring's lenient for parity, or run strict as a startup validation pass that collects EVERY unresolved `@Value` at once? This must be ONE framework-wide policy coordinated with property-resolution's identical strict/lenient question and environment's read-view, not decided per-feature.
- Is `@Value` lowered at compile time to a typed monomorphized resolve-and-convert (R1/R3, no runtime processor, target type static — the rust-native fit) or interpreted at runtime by a discoverable post-processor (R2, Spring-faithful, inspectable/replaceable, runtime-authored specs possible)? This is the keystone for THIS feature and is entangled with the metadata/codegen-boundary and type-erasure/registry-substrate meta-concepts — adopting either in Phase 1 would pre-bias Phase 2.
- Who owns the `${...}`-vs-`#{...}` split and ordering inside a single `@Value` string (Spring resolves `${}` then `#{}`)? This boundary spans this feature (orchestration), property-resolution (placeholders), and expression-language (`#{}`), and needs ONE owner for the dispatch grammar — left unresolved.
- Should required/optional/lazy `@Value` semantics be encoded in the TARGET TYPE (R3's `Option<T>` / `RequiredValue<T>` / `LazyValue<T>`) — unifying with deferral-primitives — or via per-site attribute knobs / a global policy bean (R2)? If both a type-encoded and a global strict/lenient policy exist, which wins for a bare-`T` field? A policy-placement + precedence question spanning deferral-primitives, environment, and property-resolution.
- How does the bean read the environment at `@Value` resolution time, and how does a `LazyValue<T>` capture it for later resolution without forcing an `Arc<dyn PropertyResolver + Send + Sync>` that violates memory discipline (charter #5)? This is environment's reachability/handle-shape keystone (the scope/lifetime/ownership model) applied to value injection — deferred there, inherited here.
- Where on the fail-fast timing ladder (charter #9) does each `@Value` failure land — malformed grammar (compile-time in R1, startup in R2), missing strict placeholder (startup validation pass), conversion error (compile-time-absence vs startup), and a `LazyValue.get()` failure (necessarily runtime)? Is that boundary consistent across `@Value` vs `@ConfigurationProperties`, and shared with the unified error/diagnostics meta-concept?
- If the value post-processor (R2) or custom value-sources are discovered cross-crate, do they ride the SAME scoped discovery substrate as component-scanning/auto-config (linkme vs inventory vs build-codegen), and how is the Layer-0/DCE silent-empty hazard handled — given a missing value post-processor would make `@Value` silently do NOTHING (a catastrophic silent failure)? Couples to the cross-crate composition keystone and the 'scope discovery, not global' guardrail.
- Where does the async pre-materialization pipeline live for a `@Value` whose `${...}` source or `#{...}` expression inputs are remote/async, given resolution at wiring time (and `LazyValue.get()` on hot paths) MUST be synchronous non-blocking? This is the same BootstrapContext-analogue async-fetch→snapshot→sync-use pipeline property-sources/environment/property-resolution already flagged — must align framework-wide.

### Notes
Scope discipline: I treated this feature strictly as the INJECTION-POINT SEAM and ORCHESTRATOR — the `@Value`-equivalent attribute, the composition of placeholder→expression→conversion at one statically-typed injection point, the default/required/optional/strict-lenient semantics AS SURFACED at that point, and how the resolved value lands in a constructor arg / field. I deliberately did NOT redesign its delegates, all of which were handed to me as upstream/neighbour designs and which I build on concretely: the `${...}` walk + defaults + recursion + cycle detection + strict/lenient tail (property-resolution), the `#{...}` engine (expression-language), string→T coercion (type-conversion, whose trait-native `FromConfigValue` path R1/R3 lean on directly), the env read seam + reachability (environment), provenance (origin-tracking), and whole-object inert binding (config-properties/binder, the deliberate contrast). The genuinely divergent axis for THIS feature is HOW the injection point is represented given the target type is statically known in Rust: R1 = thin macro lowering each site to a monomorphized `resolve_into::<FieldTy>` call (best low-overhead + earliest-diagnostic, grammar split at compile time, zero cross-crate/DCE exposure, but no runtime-authored specs and lowest Spring-mechanism parity); R2 = runtime value-descriptor interpreted by a discoverable BeanPostProcessor-analogue (highest Spring parity, inspectable/replaceable, runtime-authored specs, but reintroduces erasure/boxing/registry-lookup and is the realization MOST exposed to the catastrophic silent-empty-registry hazard — a missing value post-processor silently breaks ALL `@Value`); R3 = typed handle family (`Value<T>`/`LazyValue<T>`/`RequiredValue<T>`/`Option<T>`) over R1's core that encodes required/optional/lazy/strict in the TYPE SYSTEM and unifies the value-level lazy case with deferral-primitives (most rust-native semantics + best diagnostics via origin-carrying handles, but most novel surface vs Spring and a `LazyValue` hot-path execution-context concern). All three keep resolution SYNCHRONOUS + non-blocking on the cold wiring path (charter #3/#4 — placeholder walk is in-memory, expression eval is sync-pure, conversion is pure CPU; any remote/async source pre-snapshotted before wiring), ride ordinary injection rather than a magic side channel (charter #10 — `@Value` is 'step 2' on the same constructor/setter/field seam as by-type autowiring), keep the macro thin (at most emitting a `resolve_into::<T>` call + a const segment array), encode absent-vs-present-empty distinctly via Option/Result, and surface rich injection-point-naming diagnostics (UnresolvedValue / ConvertError / ExprError, plus R1's compile-time malformed-grammar catch) per charter #9. Recurring structural facts across all three: (a) the lenient-vs-strict foot-gun is a framework-wide policy decision shared with property-resolution, NOT this feature's alone; (b) the `${}`-before-`#{}` dispatch ordering inside one string needs one owner spanning this feature + property-resolution + expression-language; (c) `@Value` vs `@ConfigurationProperties` is a deliberate code-ish-expression vs inert-data split that must survive; (d) the env reachability/handle-shape is environment's deferred keystone inherited here. Charter alignment: hot-path-free (resolution is cold wiring; only R3's `LazyValue.get()` can touch a hot path and is contractually sync-non-blocking); macros thin; intent parity is literal in the three source shapes + the default semantics + the strict/lenient fork, while the representation is rust-native (charter #2/#11). Nightly review: nothing needs nightly — attribute macros emitting const segment arrays + monomorphized generic calls, `FromConfigValue` trait dispatch, `OnceLock` for R3 laziness, `Option`/`Result`, and (only if R2) `const TypeId::of` (stable 1.91) all on stable; resolution stays synchronous so no AFIT/RPITIT boxing tax. The cross-crate touchpoints are minimal for R1/R3 (the value path is cross-crate-inert; only transitive env-handle + `#{@bean}` BeanResolver exposure) and significant for R2 (the value post-processor must be discovered, with the silent-empty self-check mandatory); where present they share the SAME link-time-registry/DCE substrate as component scanning. Whether `@Value` is lowered (R1/R3) or runtime-processed (R2), whether it unifies with deferral-primitives (R3), and the strict/lenient default are all flagged UNRESOLVED for Phase 2, consistent with Phase 1 staying implementation-agnostic.

## Profiles (environment-varying wiring)  `[profiles]`

Spring's `@Profile` is NOT a special-cased container feature — it is meta-annotated `@Conditional(ProfileCondition.class)`: ordinary condition evaluation whose predicate reads the triggering annotation's profile-expression attribute and asks the Environment `acceptsProfiles(...)`. The intent leaf must replicate (intent, not the JVM mechanism):

(1) PROFILES ARE A CONDITION PRESET, NOT A PARALLEL ENGINE. The conceptual economy is the whole point: profiles predate the generic condition SPI; Spring retrofitted them onto it rather than maintaining two engines. leaf's `profiles` feature must be the most-ergonomically-named preset over the already-designed `conditions` SPI (a `Leaf(ON_PROFILE, expr)` condition reading `environment.active_profiles()`), NOT a bespoke registration path. It owns only what the SPI delegates to it: the EXPRESSION GRAMMAR and the ACTIVATION LEVERS.

(2) EXPRESSION GRAMMAR with fail-fast-on-ambiguity. A profile value is a simple name (`prod`) or an expression over `!` (NOT), `&` (AND), `|` (OR). The hard rule: `&` and `|` may NOT be mixed without parentheses (`a & b | c` is REFUSED — must be `(a & b) | c`). This is a deliberate Spring value: refuse to guess intent rather than bake in memorized precedence. Two more buried rules: the ARRAY/multi-value form is implicit OR (`["p1","p2"]` registers if p1 OR p2 — practitioners wrongly expect AND); and NEGATION MATCHES ABSENCE (`!prod` is true whenever `prod` is simply not in the active set, including the all-default case — there is no "unknown" tri-state).

(3) THREE DISTINCT ACTIVATION LEVERS, deliberately not overloaded into one knob: `active` = the externally-chosen environment (ops-replaceable); `include` = profiles the app INSISTS ON regardless of environment (cross-cutting, e.g. `common`; added BEFORE the active ones; processed PER-PROPERTY-SOURCE not list-merged — relaxed-binding collection-merge does NOT apply); `group` = a logical alias that FANS OUT (`group.production=proddb,prodmq` → activating `production` also activates proddb+prodmq) so ops toggle one coarse name while devs keep fine-grained ones.

(4) THE RESERVED `default` FALLBACK with its notorious gotcha. If NO profile is explicitly active, a fallback profile literally named `default` activates and `@Profile("default")` beans register. It is a FALLBACK, not an always-on baseline: the moment ANY explicit profile is active, the default set is ignored ENTIRELY — activating one unrelated profile silently disables every default-profile bean. The fallback exists to avoid the zero-profiles-active dead state (guarantee a baseline) while preventing double-wiring. Name configurable (`spring.profiles.default`, settable to disable).

(5) STRUCTURAL build-once-configure-per-environment. The deeper philosophy: the SAME artifact assembles a STRUCTURALLY DIFFERENT object graph (embedded DB in dev, real datasource in prod) with zero rebuild — environment-varying WIRING, not just key/value tuning. A profile-excluded bean's definition never exists (invisible to autowiring/post-processors, per the conditions contract — graceful no-bean, not lazy/uninstantiated). Class-level profile short-circuits all nested beans; a method-level profile cannot re-enable a bean inside a profile-excluded enclosure.

(6) ACTIVATION IS ITSELF A PROPERTY (`spring.profiles.active`), so the same property-source stack and ordering drive it (a CLI override beats a file) — this is the `environment` unification. leaf OWNS: the grammar parser/evaluator, the active/include/group/default lever semantics, and the `ON_PROFILE` condition preset. leaf does NOT own: the condition SPI two-phase/AND-fold/no-error-on-miss (`conditions`), the active-profile-set source-of-truth and read view (`environment`), the property stack/ordering (`property-sources`/`property-resolution`), the match/no-match accounting (`condition-report`), or relaxed-binding of the activation keys (`relaxed-binding`).

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Profiles as a canned condition: `ON_PROFILE` preset over the conditions SPI + a const-parsed ProfileExpr
Treat profiles as exactly what Spring proved them to be — the most-ergonomically-named preset over the already-designed `conditions` SPI — and add NOTHING bespoke to the registration path. The profile attribute macro `#[profile("prod & (us-east | eu-central)")]` (and the array sugar `#[profile("p1", "p2")]`) is THIN: it emits exactly one `ConditionEntry { id: leaf::cond::ON_PROFILE, attrs }` (R1 of conditions) / one `Leaf(ON_PROFILE, attrs)` node (R3 of conditions) onto the element's annotation-model metadata descriptor — the same channel any condition uses. The attrs carry the PARSED expression as const data: the macro parses the grammar at compile time into a `const ProfileExpr` tree (`enum ProfileExpr { Name(&'static str), Not(&'static ProfileExpr), And(&'static [ProfileExpr]), Or(&'static [ProfileExpr]) }`), so the macro can `compile_error!` with a span on the mixed-`&`/`|`-without-parens ambiguity, on empty names, and (if validation is on) on illegal characters — the fail-fast-on-ambiguity intent realized as a COMPILE error (charter #9, earliest legible). The array form lowers to `Or([...])`. The `OnProfile` condition impl (in `leaf-conditions`/the profiles crate, a normal hand-writable type registered by `ConditionId`) reads that const `ProfileExpr` from the metadata and evaluates it against `ctx.environment.active_profiles()` via a free fn `profiles::matches(expr, &ActiveProfiles)` that recurses the tree (And=all, Or=any, Not=absence-of). Negation-matches-absence falls out of `Not` evaluating `!active.contains(name)`. Phase is PARSE (order-insensitive: depends only on the sealed environment, not on what beans are defined-so-far), so profiles prune whole config trees in the cheap early pass. The `default`-fallback, include-fan-in, and group-fan-out are NOT in this matcher — they are resolved upstream when the environment seals `ActiveProfiles` (see T2), so by the time `OnProfile` evaluates, `active_profiles()` already reflects includes+groups+default. Profiles thus add: a grammar parser (compile-time), a tree evaluator (runtime free fn), and a registered `ConditionId` — three small pieces, zero new registration machinery.

**Pros:** Maximal unification (charter #11): profiles are literally a condition preset — no parallel engine, no new discovery channel, no new phase model. Inherits the conditions SPI's two-phase ordering, AND-fold (a `#[profile]` stacked with another condition just ANDs), graceful-no-bean, and condition-report wiring for free — exactly Spring's conceptual economy.; Strongest grammar diagnostics (charter #9): the ambiguous-mixed-operator rule, empty-name, and char-validation become COMPILE errors with a precise span at the `#[profile("...")]` site, beating Spring's runtime throw — the expression is rejected before it can ever mis-wire.; Thin macro (charter #10): emits one const `ConditionEntry`/`Leaf` carrying a const `ProfileExpr`; ALL evaluation logic lives in a normal testable crate; `cargo expand` shows a legible const tree. The parser+evaluator are independently unit-testable without the container.; Origin-agnostic (guardrail): the profile condition rides the SAME shared definition-descriptor substrate as every other condition/component, so a profile-gated bean from a statically-linked crate OR a future dynamic/WASM-module source is gated identically — profiles invent no bespoke channel.; Clean separation of concerns matching the neighbour designs: grammar+lever-semantics live here; the active-set source-of-truth lives in `environment`; the predicate substrate lives in `conditions`. Each neighbour's open question (where `accepts_profiles` lives) is answered by this free-fn factoring.

**Cons:** Does NOT by itself realize the include/group/default LEVER semantics — it only consumes the resolved `ActiveProfiles`. Those levers must be resolved during environment sealing (a `refresh-lifecycle` ordering dependency on `environment`/`config-data`), so this realization is correct only if that resolution is specified elsewhere; profiles owns the lever ALGEBRA but borrows the place it runs.; Compile-time grammar parsing means a profile expression cannot be assembled from runtime data — fine for `@Profile` (always a literal) but means there is no runtime `accepts_profiles("<dynamic string>")` unless a separate runtime parser exists (the same parser, invoked at runtime, is the natural answer — see mechanismsConsidered).; The `default`-fallback gotcha (any explicit profile wipes the default set) is a SEMANTIC the environment seal must implement; if the seal logic and the `Not`-matches-absence logic disagree about what 'active' means in the all-default case, the notorious surprise reappears — needs one canonical `ActiveProfiles` definition shared by both.; Because profiles are PARSE-phase, a `#[profile]` cannot depend on what-other-beans-exist (no profile-on-bean-presence) — correct per Spring (profiles read only the environment) but means profiles can never be the OnBean-style late lever; that is by design but worth stating.

**Mechanisms considered:** thin `#[profile("expr"|"p1","p2")]` attribute macro emitting one `ConditionEntry{ON_PROFILE, attrs}` / `Leaf(ON_PROFILE, attrs)` onto the annotation-model descriptor; compile-time grammar parser in `leaf-macros` lowering to a `const ProfileExpr` tree (Name/Not/And/Or); array form → `Or`; `compile_error!` on mixed `&`/`|` without parens, empty names, and (gated) illegal profile chars; registered `OnProfile` Condition impl resolving via `ConditionId` (R1-conditions) reading the const `ProfileExpr` from metadata; free fn `profiles::matches(&ProfileExpr, &ActiveProfiles) -> bool` (And=all/Or=any/Not=absence), reused at compile-validation and runtime; SAME parser exposed as a runtime fn for a programmatic `accepts_profiles("<str>") -> Result<bool, ProfileParseError>` escape hatch; PARSE-phase classification (depends only on sealed environment, order-insensitive) so profiles prune in the cheap early pass

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No new cross-crate substrate: the `ProfileExpr` ABI and the `ON_PROFILE` `ConditionId` live in the stable kernel (`leaf-core`/`leaf-conditions`); emitted const trees hard-code absolute paths to them (proc-macro hygiene). Profile-gated definitions travel as const `ConditionEntry`/`Leaf` data on the SAME definition-descriptor discovery table that components/conditions ride (link-time `linkme` slice default + optional dynamic directory-scanned/WASM source) — origin-agnostic, never a bespoke channel. Inherits the conditions SPI's two hazard surfaces (the gated definitions; the `ConditionId`→impl registry that must resolve `ON_PROFILE` or a startup self-check fails loudly rather than silently passing/dropping every profiled bean). Generics limitation is inherited verbatim: a `#[profile]` on a generic `Foo<T>` has no monomorphized symbol → the macro hard-errors or demands per-instantiation registration, same as component discovery. CROSS-CRATE PROFILE-ACTIVATION CONTRIBUTION is the one genuinely profile-specific T1 wrinkle: a crate that INSISTS on a profile (the `spring.profiles.include`/library-insists analogue) contributes an activation, not a gated definition — that contribution must also ride a const descriptor evaluated at runtime (never pre-main, never link order), and is naturally an `environment`/`config-data` customizer (an EnvironmentPostProcessor-analogue) rather than something profiles owns. Ordering of activations is DATA (include-before-active, group fan-out) resolved at runtime in the env seal, never section/constructor order.

**T2 — execution context:** Strictly COLD-PATH and SYNCHRONOUS, inherited from conditions: `OnProfile::matches` runs in the registration planner during `refresh-lifecycle` bring-up, before any eager singleton or bean future exists; it is a pure read over the SEALED `ActiveProfiles` (a snapshot derived from the property stack), must not `.await`, must not block. Two happens-before edges this feature imposes on the lifecycle: (a) the Environment must be SEALED — and `ActiveProfiles` fully computed (active resolved from the property stack, includes prepended, groups fanned out, default-fallback applied iff no explicit profile) — BEFORE the profile condition pass runs; (b) any async pre-step that a profile-activation contributor needs (e.g. a remote config source that decides activation) must complete and produce an in-memory property source BEFORE the synchronous seal, mirroring the env design's async-pre-step→in-memory-source→sync-customize pipeline. The grammar parse is compile-time (no runtime cost) in the common case; the runtime parser (escape hatch) is a bounded synchronous string parse. Errors: a malformed expression is a compile error (common path) or a `ProfileParseError` aggregated into the startup validation diagnostic (runtime escape hatch) — NEVER a panic, NEVER a no-match disguised as an error; an unsatisfied profile is a graceful no-bean recorded in condition-report, distinct and time-separated from a later unsatisfied-dependency failure. No cancellation surface on the read; the seal+profile-pass is a bounded non-cancellable cold step.

#### R2 — Profile as a first-class named bean GROUP (a registration scope), with the grammar as the group's activation guard
Push on the OTHER half of the intent the catalog name emphasizes — 'named GROUPS of beans registered only when active' — and make the GROUP a first-class structural unit rather than dissolving everything into per-element conditions. A `#[profile_group("prod")] mod prod_wiring { ... }` (or a builder `ProfileGroup::named("prod").contains(...)`) declares a named cluster of bean definitions whose ENTIRE membership is gated by one shared `ProfileExpr` guard. The macro still lowers each contained definition's descriptor to carry a `group: Option<GroupId>` tag plus the shared guard, but the design surfaces the group as a NAMED, INTROSPECTABLE entity: the container holds `ProfileGroups: Map<GroupId, { expr: ProfileExpr, members: &[DefId] }>`, evaluates each group's guard ONCE against `ActiveProfiles`, and admits/drops the whole membership atomically — directly modeling Spring's class-level-`@Profile`-short-circuits-nested-beans semantics (a group is the analogue of a `@Configuration` class) as a structural fact rather than re-deriving it per bean. Method-level profiles inside an active group are STILL per-element conditions (R1) layered ON TOP (AND-folded) so the 'cannot re-enable inside an excluded enclosure' rule is just: an excluded group never evaluates its members' inner conditions. The group itself is the natural home for the three levers' OBSERVABLE effect: a group can be activated directly (its name in `active`), via `include` (force-insisted), or via `group`-fan-out (a coarse name expands to several `GroupId`s) — so the lever model maps onto a first-class object you can list/diagnose ('group prod: active via group-alias production; 4 members admitted'). Grammar parsing and the `matches` evaluator are identical to R1 (shared crate); the divergence is the REPRESENTATION: profiles get a named-scope identity, not just a scattered set of per-element guards.

**Pros:** Models the 'named group of beans' intent literally and gives a strong DIAGNOSTIC/INTROSPECTION surface (charter #9 spirit + condition-report): you can list groups, their guard, their activation reason, and admitted/dropped membership as a coherent unit — richer than a flat per-bean match list; directly explains the class-level-short-circuit semantics.; Encodes the class-level-`@Profile`-gates-all-nested-beans rule STRUCTURALLY (group admitted/dropped atomically) instead of re-deriving it from condition stacking, eliminating a class of 'why did this nested bean survive its excluded enclosure' bugs by construction.; Natural, auditable home for the three levers' effect: activation-by-name / by-include / by-group-fan-out all resolve to 'which GroupIds are on', a small introspectable mapping that maps cleanly onto Spring's three-knobs intent and onto `container-hierarchy` (a child context can layer additional active groups).; Group-level guard evaluation is O(groups) not O(beans) on the cold path — slightly cheaper than per-bean condition evaluation when a group has many members (low-overhead #2), and the membership admit/drop is a single decision.

**Cons:** Risks becoming a PARALLEL ENGINE — the exact anti-pattern Spring avoided by making `@Profile` just a `@Conditional` (charter #11). A first-class 'group scope' that the container special-cases is conceptual debt unless it is carefully expressed as sugar that STILL lowers to condition descriptors; the design must prove the group is a VIEW over conditions, not a second registration path.; 'Group' the structural unit collides namewise and conceptually with `spring.profiles.GROUP` the lever (a logical alias). Two different 'group' meanings in one feature is a documentation/foot-gun hazard; must be disambiguated (e.g. 'profile-scope' vs 'profile-alias').; A named group as a Rust `mod`/builder constrains WHERE profiled beans can live (inside the group declaration), which is less flexible than Spring's any-element `@Profile` and clashes with auto-discovery across crates — a group spanning multiple crates is awkward (the GroupId membership is then itself a cross-crate collected set, re-importing the discovery hazard at group granularity).; Mixing group-level guards with per-method inner conditions creates two evaluation altitudes (group then member), doubling the 'where was this decided' surface that condition-report must reconcile — partially undoing the unification win.; Does not, by itself, simplify the include/group-lever resolution (still an environment-seal concern) — it only gives the RESULT a nicer shape.

**Mechanisms considered:** first-class `ProfileGroups: Map<GroupId, {expr, members}>` evaluated once per group against `ActiveProfiles`; `#[profile_group("name")] mod ...` / `ProfileGroup` builder lowering members to descriptors tagged with `group: Option<GroupId>` + shared guard; group = sugar that STILL emits condition descriptors (a VIEW over conditions, not a parallel path) to preserve unification; per-member inner conditions AND-folded under an admitted group (class-level-short-circuit modeled as: excluded group never evaluates members); group-as-introspection-unit feeding condition-report ('group X: activated via <lever>; N admitted, M dropped'); disambiguation of 'profile-scope group' (this) vs 'profile-alias group' (the `group` lever); cross-crate GroupId membership as a collected set on the shared substrate (with the attendant discovery hazard at group granularity)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same kernel residency for `ProfileExpr`/`ON_PROFILE`/`GroupId` as R1. The added cross-crate wrinkle is GROUP MEMBERSHIP that spans crates: if a named profile-group can collect members from multiple crates, the `GroupId → [DefId]` mapping is itself a cross-crate collected set riding the shared link-time slice (+ optional dynamic/WASM source), inheriting the DCE/Layer-0 hazards at group granularity (a member in an unreferenced crate silently vanishes from the group — the self-check must assert expected membership counts, not just expected groups). If instead a group is confined to one `mod` in one crate (the conservative form), there is no new cross-crate surface beyond R1's — the group is purely local sugar over per-element descriptors. Ordering of group guard evaluation is DATA (depends on the sealed `ActiveProfiles`), never link/section order. The group's activation-reason (active vs include vs group-alias) is computed at runtime in the env seal and attached to the group for diagnostics; it is not a link-time fact.

**T2 — execution context:** Cold-path and synchronous like R1: group-guard evaluation runs in the registration planner during `refresh-lifecycle`, after the Environment seals `ActiveProfiles`, before any instantiation. Each group's guard is evaluated ONCE (a pure read over the sealed snapshot, no `.await`, no block); an admitted group then proceeds to evaluate its members' inner conditions, a dropped group short-circuits its entire membership (the structural realization of class-level short-circuit) — both bounded, non-cancellable cold steps. The same happens-before edges as R1 apply (environment sealed with includes+groups+default resolved before the pass; async activation pre-steps complete before seal). Errors: a malformed group guard is a compile error (literal expr) or aggregated startup-validation error; a dropped group is a graceful no-beans for its whole membership, recorded as one coherent group-level entry in condition-report (distinct from a later unsatisfied-dependency failure). No hot-path cost; group introspection (listing activation reasons/membership) is a cold diagnostic read.

#### R3 — Activation algebra as a sealed `ActiveProfiles` value object owned by profiles, consumed by a trivial matcher
Invert the center of gravity: the genuinely hard, intent-rich part of profiles is NOT the per-bean gate (which R1 already minimizes) but the ACTIVATION ALGEBRA — how `active`/`include`/`group`/`default` combine into the final active set, with all its buried rules. Make profiles OWN that algebra as a first-class, tested value object `ActiveProfiles`, and make the matcher trivially thin. `ActiveProfiles` is built by a pure `fn resolve_active(levers: ProfileLevers, validate: bool) -> Result<ActiveProfiles, ProfileError>` where `ProfileLevers { active: Vec<Name>, include: Vec<Name>, groups: Map<Name, Vec<Name>>, default: Name }` is harvested (by the environment/config-data layer) from the property stack. `resolve_active` encodes EVERY buried rule explicitly and testably: (a) if `active` is empty AND `include` is empty → the set is exactly `{default}` (the reserved fallback); (b) the moment `active` OR `include` is non-empty → `default` is dropped ENTIRELY (the notorious gotcha, made a single explicit branch with a clear name `default_suppressed_by_explicit`); (c) `include` names are prepended BEFORE `active` (order preserved for diagnostics, though membership is a set for matching); (d) group fan-out is applied TRANSITIVELY with cycle detection (activating `production` pulls in `proddb,prodmq`, and if those are themselves group keys they fan out too — a fixpoint with a loud error on a cycle, a hazard Spring's flat model risks silently); (e) name validation (letters/digits/`-_.+@`, must start/end alphanumeric) gated by `validate`. The result is a sealed, immutable `ActiveProfiles` (a `Set<Name>` + ordered provenance for diagnostics) that is `Send+Sync` and reachable as the environment's `active_profiles()`. The matcher is then dead-simple: `OnProfile` (R1's preset) just calls `profiles::matches(&expr, &active)`. This realization's divergence is SCOPE: profiles owns a substantial, independently-testable activation engine (the levers + fallback + transitive group fan-out + cycle detection + validation), and exposes `ActiveProfiles` as the canonical shared definition of 'what is active' that BOTH the matcher and the env seal agree on — eliminating the R1-noted risk of two disagreeing notions of 'active'.

**Pros:** Puts the hard intent where it belongs: the include/group/default LEVER semantics — the part most likely to surprise (default-suppression, include-prepend, per-source-not-merged, group fan-out) — become one explicitly-named, exhaustively-tested pure function, the strongest guard against the notorious 'my default DataSource vanished' gotcha (DX #1, diagnostics #9).; Single canonical `ActiveProfiles` shared by the matcher AND the env read view removes the R1 ambiguity (two notions of 'active'); the `default`-fallback and negation-matches-absence are defined ONCE against one set, so they cannot drift.; Adds TRANSITIVE group fan-out with explicit CYCLE DETECTION — a correctness improvement over Spring's flat one-level fan-out framing, surfacing a `group production includes proddb which includes production` cycle as a loud startup error rather than a silent infinite/partial expansion.; Pure, runtime-data function (`resolve_active`) is trivially unit-testable across the full matrix of lever combinations without any container, linker, or async — high confidence on the buried rules; provenance retained for rich diagnostics (charter #9).; Keeps the per-bean path maximally thin (delegates matching to R1) AND honors the cross-crate directive verbatim: ALL activation/feature/profile logic is RUNTIME evaluation over harvested lever DATA, never pre-main, never link order.

**Cons:** The lever-HARVESTING (reading `active`/`include`/`group`/`default` from the property stack, with relaxed-binding of the keys and the per-property-source-not-list-merged `include` rule) is a `property-sources`/`config-data`/`relaxed-binding` concern, not profiles' — so this realization must draw a crisp boundary: profiles owns `resolve_active(ProfileLevers)` (the ALGEBRA) but NOT how `ProfileLevers` is gathered (the TRANSPORT). Getting that boundary wrong re-imports config-data into profiles.; The per-property-source-not-list-merged semantics of `include` is genuinely a property-stack concern (how the levers are read per source before being h- handed to `resolve_active`); a pure `resolve_active` over already-flattened `Vec<Name>` cannot enforce it — so either `ProfileLevers` must carry per-source structure (leaking transport into the algebra) or that rule lives upstream (cleaner, but then profiles doesn't fully own the lever intent).; Transitive group fan-out with cycle detection is more machinery than Spring's documented one-level fan-out; if Spring's intent is strictly one level, the transitive version is a (defensible) divergence that must be a conscious choice, not an accident.; `ActiveProfiles` as a sealed shared value object couples profiles tightly to the environment seal timing — it MUST be produced exactly at the env-seal step and never mutated after (matching Spring's before-refresh activation), so a late `addActiveProfile` escape hatch becomes a 'rebuild a child ActiveProfiles' operation rather than an in-place mutate (interacts with the env design's builder-consumed/seal model).

**Mechanisms considered:** pure `fn resolve_active(ProfileLevers, validate) -> Result<ActiveProfiles, ProfileError>` encoding every buried rule as named branches; sealed immutable `ActiveProfiles { set: Set<Name>, ordered_provenance: Vec<(Name, ActivationReason)> }`, Send+Sync, = env `active_profiles()`; explicit `default_suppressed_by_explicit` branch for the fallback gotcha; `include`-prepend; one-set membership for matching; transitive group fan-out as a fixpoint with cycle detection (loud `ProfileGroupCycle` error); gated name validation (letters/digits/`-_.+@`, start/end alphanumeric) as the Boot `spring.profiles.validate` analogue; clear boundary: profiles owns the ALGEBRA (`resolve_active`); `config-data`/`property-sources`/`relaxed-binding` own harvesting `ProfileLevers` from the stack; trivial `OnProfile` matcher (R1 preset) consuming the canonical `ActiveProfiles`; child-`ActiveProfiles` derivation for hierarchical contexts / late additive activation (no in-place mutation)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** `ActiveProfiles`, `ProfileLevers`, `ProfileExpr`, `ProfileError`, and `ON_PROFILE` live in the stable kernel; `resolve_active` is a pure function in the profiles crate (no cross-crate registration of its own — it is called once at env seal). The cross-crate surface is entirely INHERITED: (a) profile-GATED definitions ride the shared definition-descriptor substrate via the R1 `OnProfile` condition (link-time slice default + optional dynamic/WASM source, same DCE/Layer-0/generics hazards); (b) cross-crate profile-ACTIVATION contributions (a library insisting on an `include`d profile) are EnvironmentPostProcessor-analogue customizers that contribute to `ProfileLevers` before `resolve_active` runs — they ride the same env-customizer discovery the environment design specifies (const descriptors, runtime-evaluated, topo-ordered by data, never link order), NOT a profiles-specific channel. Because `resolve_active` is pure and runs at the final binary's env-seal step (the only point that sees the whole resolved feature/crate set), the active set is deterministic given the binary's resolved features+property sources — and must be documented to differ between `-p` and `--workspace` builds (the conditions/env feature-unification caveat). Group fan-out and include-prepend are runtime DATA computations, never link/section order.

**T2 — execution context:** `resolve_active` runs EXACTLY ONCE, synchronously, on the cold bootstrap path at the environment-seal step of `refresh-lifecycle` — after all property sources (incl. any async-pre-step-produced in-memory sources and all env customizers contributing levers) are in place, before the condition pass, before any instantiation. It is a pure, bounded, non-blocking computation (no `.await`); the transitive group fan-out is a bounded fixpoint over a finite lever set with cycle detection (terminates loudly, never spins — satisfies charter #4: this is cold-path, and it is NOT a poll loop). The produced `ActiveProfiles` is then immutable/`Send+Sync` and read lock-free by every subsequent `OnProfile` evaluation and by the env read view across `.await`/thread hops. Errors: a malformed expression (compile-time for literals), an invalid profile name under validation, or a group cycle are returned as a rich `ProfileError` carrying the offending name/expr and its provenance, aggregated into the startup validation diagnostic gate (charter #9) BEFORE wiring proceeds — never a panic, never silently. Late additive activation (the `addActiveProfile`/Boot-additional-profiles analogue) is modeled as deriving a NEW child `ActiveProfiles` before seal (or a child context), not as mutation — preserving the immutable-after-seal contract. No cancellation surface on the seal/evaluate path; the async work that may be cancelled lives strictly upstream in the source-loading pre-steps.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's `@Profile` is runtime condition evaluation over a reflectively-readable annotation (`ProfileCondition` reads `Profile.value` off `AnnotatedTypeMetadata`). Rust has no annotation reflection — the profile EXPRESSION must be materialized into const metadata at compile time by `annotation-model` and read as a const `ProfileExpr`. The 'one reusable condition parameterized by the annotation's attributes' intent survives only because the attribute is deliberately frozen into data, not read off a type at runtime. (Inherited from the conditions clash, specialized to profiles.)
- Spring throws at RUNTIME on an ambiguous mixed-`&`/`|` expression. Rust can do markedly better: because the expression is a literal known to the macro, the ambiguity (and empty-name, and illegal-char) become COMPILE errors with a span — the fail-fast-on-ambiguity intent realized earlier and more legibly (charter #9). The clash is only that this is IMPOSSIBLE for a runtime-constructed expression string (a programmatic `accepts_profiles("<dynamic>")`), which must fall back to a runtime `Result<_, ProfileParseError>` — so the SAME grammar has two error timings depending on whether the expression is literal or dynamic.
- The reserved-`default` FALLBACK and its 'any explicit profile wipes the default set entirely' gotcha is a stateful resolution rule Spring computes while mutating a live environment. In Rust, with an immutable-after-seal `ActiveProfiles` (driven by the environment design's seal/builder model), this must be a single pure branch computed ONCE at seal — and crucially there can be only ONE canonical notion of 'the active set' shared by both the negation-matches-absence matcher and the fallback logic, or the gotcha mutates into an even subtler drift bug. Rust's immutability is an asset here (one sealed value) but forbids Spring's 'mutate active profiles late' flexibility without an explicit child-derivation escape hatch.
- Negation-matches-ABSENCE with no tri-state ('`!prod` is true whenever prod is simply not active, including all-default') maps cleanly to `!set.contains(name)` over a `Set<Name>` — Rust's lack of a null/unknown is actually a CLARITY win, but it pins down that 'absent' must be defined against the single sealed active set, including correctly treating the all-default case as 'prod absent' (so `!prod` registers under the default profile). A subtle interaction with the fallback rule that one shared `ActiveProfiles` must get right.
- Spring activates profiles by MUTATING the live `Environment` before refresh (`setActiveProfiles`/`addActiveProfile`, Boot's additive `setAdditionalProfiles`). Rust ownership (per the environment design) pushes toward levers harvested into an immutable `ActiveProfiles` at a single seal point; 'augment rather than replace' (Boot-additional) and 'add a profile late' become explicit lever-merge / child-derivation operations, not in-place mutation — an intent-preserving but mechanism-diverging realization tied to the env builder-consumed/token seam decision.
