The link-time-registry (linkme default 'classpath') vs build-time-codegen duality PLUS the optional dynamic directory-scanned (WASM 'classpath') source-provider set, with the DCE/zero-contribution-self-check guardrails, must be decided uniformly across ALL discovery features — left unresolved here on purpose.
- How is source-contributor ORDERING decided deterministically given that link/section/constructor order is unspecified? Each contributed source carries a position-hint (first/last/before(name)/after(name)) as descriptor DATA applied in a runtime sort — but the global precedence POLICY (the analogue of Boot's fixed externalized-config precedence order, and Boot's PropertySourceOrderingBeanFactoryPostProcessor re-ordering) is a cross-feature concern spanning config-data, profiles, and auto-config that needs one owner.
- Where does the async pre-step live that materializes a remote/async-backed source into an in-memory `PropertySource` BEFORE it joins the (synchronous-read) stack — i.e. the async-fetch -> in-memory-snapshot -> sync-add pipeline (the BootstrapContext analogue)? This is the §3 async-execution-model deferral applied to config sources and must align with the same pipeline `environment` flagged for its customizers.
- Should provenance (`origin-tracking`) be attached to EVERY returned value (R3's index-row model, hot-path-friendly but memory-heavier) or be queryable on-demand by walking the stack (lighter, but a second pass)? This decides whether the read path always carries origin or only the diagnostic path does, and trades hot-path memory against diagnostic latency — coordinated with origin-tracking and the binder's error reporting.
- Does the `PropertySource` trait expose a first-class enumerable facet (R3's `EnumerablePropertySource` sub-trait, needed by relaxed-binding/binder for key listing) or an optional downcast facet (R1's `as_enumerable`), and how do non-enumerable sources (random.*-style, JNDI-like) coexist with enumerable ones in first-source-wins ordering? The enumerable/non-enumerable correctness rule spans this feature, relaxed-binding, and binder.

### Notes
Scope discipline: I treated this feature strictly as the HELD STACK MECHANICS — the named, ordered, first-source-wins data structure; the `PropertySource` source trait (origin-agnostic); the explicit ordering-mutation vocabulary (addFirst/addLast/addBefore/addAfter/replace/remove); and the four load-bearing invariants (first-source-wins, never-blend, null-never-overrides, name-as-identity + child-wins merge). I deliberately did NOT redesign the `environment` façade/seam/reachability (upstream, complete), the `${...}` placeholder/resolution engine (`property-resolution`), the profile grammar/levers (`profiles`), the file-format loading + precedence policy (`config-data`), or value provenance internals (`origin-tracking`) — those are distinct neighbours. The genuinely divergent axis for THIS feature is HOW the ordered named stack is represented in Rust: R1 = literal in-place `Vec<Box<dyn PropertySource>>` + name index (max intent fidelity, simplest, linear-scan reads); R2 = persistent `Arc`-layered cons-chain (zero-copy hierarchical child-wins, lock-free immutable reads, more Arc churn on mid-stack mutation); R3 = mutable Vec frozen at seal into a precomputed effective-key index with enumerable/non-enumerable source split (O(1) provenance-carrying reads, more machinery + a cross-path first-wins correctness rule). All three keep the source as a `dyn PropertySource` trait object (Box or Arc) to stay origin-agnostic so native/file/future-WASM sources slot in identically (guardrail), and all three encode null-never-overrides as `Option<PropertyValue>` (None=absent) and never-blend as Option short-circuit / record-first-occurrence-only. Charter alignment: read path is synchronous in-memory and lock-free in all three (charter #4); the only Arc is deliberate (R2's sharing handle, R3's index share via env) and justified per charter #5; the single PropertySource trait + thin descriptor for cross-crate contributors keeps macros thin (charter #10); intent parity is literal in the mutation vocabulary while the representation is rust-native (charter #2/#11). Nightly review: nothing needs nightly — object-safe traits, `Option`/`Cow`/`Arc`, `HashMap`, and (R3) a precomputed `HashMap` index cover everything on stable; reads stay synchronous so no AFIT/RPITIT boxing tax on the source trait. The ONE cross-crate touchpoint (source-contributor discovery, the config-data/EnvironmentPostProcessor analogue) shares the SAME link-time-registry/DCE substrate as component scanning and should NOT invent a bespoke mechanism — flagged UNRESOLVED for Phase 2, consistent with what the `environment` neighbour flagged.

## Property Resolution & Placeholders  `[property-resolution]`

This feature owns the READ PATH the whole config model depends on: the resolver that turns a key (and any embedded `${...}` placeholders) into an effective value over the ordered, first-source-wins source stack supplied by the upstream `property-sources` neighbour. Spring's intent (docs §"Read vs. mutate: PropertyResolver vs. ConfigurableEnvironment" + §"@Value / the lenient-by-default resolver" + the null-never-overrides rule) has five load-bearing parts to replicate as INTENT, not mechanism:\n\n(1) **A least-privilege READ interface** (`PropertyResolver`): `get_property(key)`, `get_property_or(key, default)`, `get_property_as::<T>(key)` (typed), `get_required_property(key)`, `contains_property(key)`, and the two placeholder entry points `resolve_placeholders(text)` / `resolve_required_placeholders(text)`. This is the consume-only seam handed to application code; it deliberately CANNOT reshape source ordering (that is the `environment` mutate seam). The resolver is `PropertySourcesPropertyResolver` — it walks the stack's first-source-wins primitive and adds the placeholder/typing/required logic on top.\n\n(2) **`${...}` placeholder expansion with three sub-behaviours**: (a) **defaults** — `${key:fallback}` uses `fallback` (which itself may contain placeholders) when `key` is unresolved; the value-separator is `:` and is itself configurable in Spring; (b) **recursive / nested resolution** — both the placeholder body and the resolved value are re-scanned, so `${${meta}}` (placeholder-in-key) and a resolved value that itself contains `${...}` both expand, with **circular-reference detection** (Spring's `PropertyPlaceholderHelper` tracks a visited set and throws on a cycle like `a=${b}, b=${a}`); (c) **partial/literal text** — placeholders are expanded in-place inside surrounding literal text (`jdbc:${db.host}:5432`), not only whole-string.\n\n(3) **Strict vs. lenient modes** — THE signature Spring gotcha. The default embedded value resolver is **lenient**: an unresolved `${catalog.name}` with no default is left as the literal text `${catalog.name}` (Spring) / passed through, silently producing wrong config. The **strict** mode (Spring's `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer` / `resolveRequiredPlaceholders`) FAILS on any unresolved placeholder. leaf must make this an explicit, first-class mode choice with a rich diagnostic in strict mode (charter #9), and must NOT silently default to the foot-gun without making the lenient choice visible.\n\n(4) **null-never-overrides / never-blend inherited from the stack** — resolution returns the FIRST source that *contains* the key; a source returning absent (`None`) falls through; a present value (even empty) wins and is NEVER blended. The resolver must preserve this: it returns exactly one source's value with one traceable origin, and placeholder expansion must not accidentally merge across sources.\n\n(5) **Typed reads + the conversion seam** — `get_property_as::<T>()` coerces the resolved raw string via the downstream `type-conversion` neighbour; this feature owns the resolve-to-raw-string + placeholder walk and hands the raw winning value (with origin) to conversion. Required/typed/default are orthogonal modifiers layered over the same core walk.\n\nSCOPE BOUNDARY: `property-sources` owns the stack data structure, ordering-mutation vocabulary, and first-source-wins LOOKUP PRIMITIVE; `environment` owns the read/mutate seam and reachability; `relaxed-binding` owns canonical-form key mapping; `type-conversion` owns string→T coercion; `origin-tracking` owns provenance wrapping. THIS feature owns only: the read-interface shape, the `${...}` placeholder parser/expander (defaults + recursion + cycle detection + partial-text), the strict-vs-lenient policy, and the required/typed/contains read modifiers — all layered ON TOP OF the stack's `get(key)`. The genuinely divergent design axis is HOW the placeholder walk, the strict/lenient mode, and the recursive-expansion-with-cycle-detection are represented in Rust (an eager char-scanner expander vs a parse-to-AST-then-evaluate engine vs a lazy/streaming `Cow`-returning resolver), and where the mode/diagnostic policy is carried.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Eager recursive char-scanner expander over a borrowed source view (the literal PropertySourcesPropertyResolver transcription)
Model the resolver as the most direct Rust transcription of `PropertySourcesPropertyResolver` + `PropertyPlaceholderHelper`. The resolver is a thin, stack-borrowing reader; placeholder expansion is a single hand-rolled recursive char scanner — the literal Spring algorithm.

```rust
pub trait PropertyResolver {
    fn get_property(&self, key: &str) -> Option<ResolvedValue>;           // raw, after placeholder walk
    fn get_property_or<'a>(&self, key: &str, default: &'a str) -> Cow<'a, str>;
    fn get_property_as<T: FromConfigValue>(&self, key: &str) -> Result<Option<T>, ConvertError>;
    fn get_required_property(&self, key: &str) -> Result<ResolvedValue, MissingProperty>;
    fn contains_property(&self, key: &str) -> bool;                       // raw stack hit, NO expansion
    fn resolve_placeholders(&self, text: &str) -> Cow<'_, str>;           // lenient: leave unresolved
    fn resolve_required_placeholders(&self, text: &str) -> Result<String, UnresolvedPlaceholder>;
}
pub struct ResolvedValue { raw: String, origin: Origin }   // single source, single origin (never blended)
```
The concrete resolver borrows the sealed stack: `struct SourcesResolver<'s> { stack: &'s PropertySourceStack, mode: ResolveMode, prefix: &'static str /* "${" */, suffix: &'static str /* "}" */, sep: char /* ':' */, escape: Option<char> }`. **Placeholder walk** is the literal Spring `parseStringValue` recursion: scan for the next `prefix`, find its matching `suffix` accounting for NESTED `${`, recurse on the inner body FIRST (so `${${meta}}` resolves the key expression), then `stack.get(resolved_key)`; on hit, recurse on the RESULT (so a value containing `${...}` re-expands); on miss, split the body on the FIRST `sep` to get `key:default` and recurse on the default; on total miss, lenient mode leaves the literal `${key}` in place and strict mode returns `UnresolvedPlaceholder { placeholder, surrounding_text, sources_searched }`. **Cycle detection** is Spring's exact mechanism: a `visited: &mut SmallVec<[&str; 8]>` set threaded through the recursion; re-entering an in-progress key returns `CircularPlaceholder { chain: [a, b, a] }`. **Strict/lenient** is the `mode` field consulted at the single 'unresolved' branch. `get_property(key)` = `stack.get(key)` then expand the raw value; `contains_property` is a pure stack hit with no expansion (matching Spring); typed reads expand-then-convert via the `type-conversion` seam.

**Pros:** Most literal intent parity: `parseStringValue`'s nested-scan + recurse-on-key + recurse-on-value + `:`-default + visited-set cycle check maps line-for-line; a Spring developer recognizes the exact algorithm, and the lenient-leaves-literal vs strict-fails fork is the recognizable `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer` knob (charter #1 DX, #2 intent-shape).; Borrows the sealed stack (`&'s PropertySourceStack`) — zero ownership duplication, no Arc, the resolver is a stack-local cheap view created per read-batch; reads are a synchronous in-memory scan with early return, lock-free, no allocation when no placeholder is present (`Cow::Borrowed` fast path) (charter #4 hot-path, #5 memory).; `contains_property` = raw stack hit (no expansion) faithfully mirrors Spring's subtlety that containment is about the key existing, not about a resolvable value — avoids a class of surprising bugs.; Cycle detection with a `SmallVec` visited set is stack-allocated for the common shallow case and yields a rich `CircularPlaceholder { chain }` diagnostic naming the full cycle (charter #9), strictly better than Spring's terse message.; Single hand-rolled scanner = thin: the macro layer never touches this (it is plain runtime code in `leaf-core`), and the whole expander is hand-writable and testable in isolation (charter #10).

**Cons:** A hand-rolled char scanner that must correctly handle nested `${`, the `:` separator (only the FIRST colon splits, so a default value may itself contain colons/placeholders), and an optional escape char is fiddly and a classic bug magnet — the very correctness Spring already debugged over years (off-by-one on matching-brace depth, separator-inside-nested-placeholder).; Eager full-string expansion allocates a `String` whenever ANY placeholder is present, even if the caller only needed a prefix — no laziness; for a hot path that resolves the same template repeatedly there is no memoization (each call re-walks).; The `mode: ResolveMode` is a per-resolver field, so strict-vs-lenient is fixed at resolver construction; Spring's reality is more nuanced (the `@Value` embedded resolver is lenient while a `resolveRequiredPlaceholders` call is strict on the SAME environment) — R1 must expose both `resolve_placeholders` (lenient) and `resolve_required_placeholders` (strict) methods rather than a single mode, slightly duplicating the walk's tail.; Recursion depth is bounded only by the cycle-detection set and the actual nesting; a pathological deeply-nested template recurses on the call stack (mitigated by cycle detection but not depth-capped unless an explicit limit is added).; Borrow lifetime `'s` ties the resolver to the sealed stack's lifetime; handing a `PropertyResolver` across an `.await`/thread requires the stack be reachable as `&'s` for that span — fine when the env hands out an `Arc<SealedEnv>`-rooted borrow, but couples this feature's handle shape to the `environment` neighbour's reachability decision.

**Mechanisms considered:** hand-rolled recursive char scanner replicating Spring `PropertyPlaceholderHelper.parseStringValue` (nested-brace matching, first-`:`-splits-default, recurse-on-key-then-on-value); `&'s PropertySourceStack` borrow (no owned copy, no Arc) for the resolver view; `Cow<'_, str>` return to keep the no-placeholder fast path allocation-free; `SmallVec<[&str; N]>` stack-allocated visited set for cycle detection (rich `CircularPlaceholder{chain}` diagnostic); two methods (`resolve_placeholders` lenient / `resolve_required_placeholders` strict) rather than a single `mode` field, mirroring Spring's two entry points; OR a `ResolveMode` enum field — both surfaced as a tension; typed reads delegate to the `type-conversion` neighbour via a `FromConfigValue` seam (no conversion logic here); stable-only: `Cow`, `Option`, `Result`, `SmallVec`/`Vec`; reads synchronous so no AFIT/RPITIT on the read trait

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The `PropertyResolver` trait + `SourcesResolver` are kernel types in `leaf-core`, named by absolute path. The resolver itself is constructed from a stack the container already owns, so it needs NO cross-crate registration on the read path — it is a pure consumer of the `property-sources` primitive. Cross-crate enters ONLY through two narrow, OPTIONAL extension points, each carrying the static-link-registry-plus-optional-dynamic-directory duality as CANDIDATES (not decisions): (a) a pluggable **placeholder-syntax/escape policy** (prefix/suffix/separator/escape) if leaf ever lets an integration override the `${...}` grammar — contributed as a `const PlaceholderSyntax` descriptor; (b) custom **resolution hooks** analogous to Spring's `StringValueResolver` chain (e.g. a `random.*`-style synthetic resolver, the RandomValuePropertySource analogue) — these would be discovered as a link-time `linkme` distributed-slice of `const ResolverHookDescriptor { matches_prefix, resolve_fn }` (default 'classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned hook set (the WASM-module 'classpath'). DCE guardrails apply exactly as for component scanning: const descriptors, runtime evaluation, force-link of opted-in crates, and a zero-contribution self-check that warns when a hook-providing crate yields nothing (silent-empty is the worst failure). CRITICAL: hook ORDER must come from descriptor DATA (a declared priority/prefix), never link/section order. By default leaf needs NEITHER extension — the core resolver is fully self-contained — so this feature is cross-crate-inert unless an integration opts into a syntax override or a synthetic resolver.

**T2 — execution context:** The resolver runs entirely on the **synchronous, non-blocking read path** — it is created (cheap, stack-borrowing) and called during steady-state operation AND during cold bootstrap (e.g. when `config-data` or `profiles` resolve `${...}` in locations / active-profile values). Reads MUST NOT `.await` or block (charter #4): the underlying `stack.get(key)` is an in-memory scan, and placeholder expansion is pure CPU recursion over already-materialized strings — any source whose backing is remote/async must have been snapshotted into the stack BEFORE the resolver sees it (the discipline inherited from `property-sources`). Errors are local, rich, and synchronous values (never panics): `MissingProperty { key, sources_searched }`, `UnresolvedPlaceholder { placeholder, surrounding_text, sources_searched }`, `CircularPlaceholder { chain }`, `ConvertError { raw, target_type, origin, cause }` — each carries the causal chain (charter #9). No cancellation concern (pure CPU, bounded by template size + cycle-detection). The resolver is `Send + Sync` when the borrowed stack is, so a resolver view can cross `.await`/threads as long as the stack outlives it — cross-task reachability of the stack itself is the `environment` neighbour's concern, not this feature's.

#### R2 — Parse-to-AST then evaluate: compile templates to a reusable PlaceholderProgram (separating parse from resolve)
Split the placeholder walk into TWO phases Spring fuses: a pure **parse** of the template into a small AST (`PlaceholderTemplate`), and a separate **evaluate** of that AST against the stack. This makes the grammar a first-class, independently-testable artifact and lets a repeatedly-resolved template (a `@Value`-equivalent injection point, a banner, a logging pattern) be parsed ONCE and evaluated many times.

```rust
enum Node { Literal(Range<usize>), Placeholder { key: Box<[Node]>, default: Option<Box<[Node]>> } } // key & default are themselves node-lists (nesting)
pub struct PlaceholderTemplate { src: Box<str>, nodes: Box<[Node]> }   // parsed once, immutable
impl PlaceholderTemplate {
    pub fn parse(text: &str) -> Result<Self, PlaceholderSyntaxError> { /* one scan, builds AST, reports unbalanced ${ } at parse time */ }
    pub fn evaluate(&self, stack: &PropertySourceStack, mode: ResolveMode) -> Result<Cow<'_, str>, ResolveError> { /* walks AST, cycle set, defaults, recurse-on-value */ }
}
```
The `PropertyResolver` trait is identical to R1, but its placeholder methods internally `parse` then `evaluate`. **Defaults** are a `default: Option<node-list>` arm — evaluated only on key-miss, and itself a node-list so the default may nest placeholders. **Recursion** has two distinct sites made explicit by the AST: (a) the `key` is a node-list so `${${meta}}` parses as a placeholder whose key is itself a placeholder (resolve-on-key); (b) recurse-on-VALUE (a resolved value containing `${...}`) is handled at evaluate time by re-parsing+evaluating the resolved string, with the cycle-detection visited set spanning both. **Cycle detection** lives in evaluate (`visited` keyed by resolved key). **Strict/lenient** is the `mode` arg to `evaluate` — so the SAME parsed template can be evaluated strictly in one call and leniently in another (fixing R1's per-resolver-fixed-mode con). **Parse-time diagnostics**: an unbalanced `${` / `}` is a SYNTAX error caught at parse (charter #9, earliest legible failure) — distinct from a RESOLVE error (unresolved key) caught at evaluate. Templates whose source is a compile-time literal (a `#[value("${...}")]` attribute) could be parsed at macro/build time into a `const`-friendly node form, surfacing malformed-placeholder errors at COMPILE time.

**Pros:** Clean parse/evaluate split makes the grammar an independently-testable artifact and surfaces SYNTAX errors (unbalanced braces, empty `${}`) at parse time — earlier and more legibly than R1's fused scan, and potentially at COMPILE time for literal templates in `#[value(...)]` (charter #9 earliest-richest-diagnostic).; A template parsed once can be evaluated many times against possibly-changing stacks or modes — the natural home for a hot `@Value`-injection point that resolves on every prototype-bean creation, avoiding R1's re-walk-every-time (charter #4/#5: amortize the parse).; Mode is an evaluate-time ARGUMENT, not a resolver-construction field, so the exact Spring reality (same environment, lenient embedded resolver vs strict `resolveRequiredPlaceholders`) is a clean single template with two evaluate calls — no walk-tail duplication (fixes R1's con).; The AST makes the two recursion sites (recurse-on-key vs recurse-on-value) STRUCTURALLY distinct rather than tangled in one scanner — easier to reason about, test, and prove cycle-detection-complete.; Thin-macro friendly: a `#[value("...")]` macro can emit a CALL to `PlaceholderTemplate::parse` (or a const node array) — all heavy logic stays in the hand-writable `leaf-core` parser, the macro emits only what a user could write (charter #10).

**Cons:** More machinery than R1: an AST type, a parser, and an evaluator vs one recursive function — must be justified 'with intent' (charter #5/#11); for a template resolved exactly once (the common config-data case) the AST allocation is pure overhead vs R1's single scan.; The parsed `PlaceholderTemplate` owns a `Box<str>` copy of the source + a `Box<[Node]>`, so parsing a one-shot template heap-allocates twice where R1's `Cow::Borrowed` fast path allocates zero — the amortization only pays off for genuinely-repeated templates.; Recurse-on-VALUE still requires re-parsing the resolved string at evaluate time (a resolved value's `${...}` was not known at parse time), so the 'parse once' benefit is partial — only the OUTER template is pre-parsed; nested values discovered at evaluate are parsed on the fly (a real but bounded leak of the clean two-phase story).; A const/compile-time node form for `#[value(...)]` literals pushes grammar logic toward the macro/build boundary, which risks thickening the macro unless the const form is a trivial data array the macro merely transcribes — a Phase-2 boundary tension, not this feature's call.; Two error categories (syntax vs resolve) is more honest but is more surface for callers to handle; a caller that just wants 'expand this, leniently' now faces a parse `Result` AND an evaluate `Result`.

**Mechanisms considered:** parse-to-AST (`PlaceholderTemplate` of `Node`s) then evaluate-against-stack, separating syntax errors (parse) from resolve errors (evaluate); `mode: ResolveMode` as an evaluate-time argument so one template serves both strict and lenient calls; `Box<[Node]>` for key and default arms so nesting (`${${meta}}`, default-with-placeholder) is structural; cycle-detection visited set in `evaluate`, spanning recurse-on-key and recurse-on-value (the latter via on-the-fly re-parse of resolved values); optional const/macro-time parse of literal `#[value("...")]` templates for compile-time malformed-placeholder errors (thin macro emits a data node array); stable-only: enums, `Box<[T]>`, `Cow`, `Result`; reads synchronous, no async-trait machinery

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same kernel-residency as R1 (`PropertyResolver`, `PlaceholderTemplate` in `leaf-core`). The parse/evaluate split adds one cross-crate-relevant nicety: a `#[value("${...}")]` attribute macro (in `leaf-macros`) can emit a thin reference to `leaf_core::PlaceholderTemplate::parse(LIT)` (or, if a const node form is adopted, a `const` node array) via an absolutely-resolvable path — keeping the macro logic-free (charter #10) while moving malformed-placeholder detection to compile time. The optional resolution-hook chain (the `random.*`/synthetic-resolver and syntax-override extension points) is discovered identically to R1: a link-time `linkme` distributed-slice of `const ResolverHookDescriptor` (default 'classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned hook set (WASM 'classpath'), order from descriptor DATA, same DCE/zero-contribution guardrails. The AST is origin-agnostic: it evaluates against any `PropertySourceStack`, so native/file/WASM-backed sources are indistinguishable to the evaluator. Like R1, the core resolver needs NO cross-crate registration; extensions are opt-in.

**T2 — execution context:** `parse` is a pure synchronous CPU step (cold-path for a `#[value]` template parsed at startup, or compile-time if const-parsed); `evaluate` runs on the synchronous, non-blocking read path (hot or cold) — neither `.await`s nor blocks (charter #4). The amortization benefit means a hot `@Value`-equivalent injection point parses once at bean-definition time (cold) and evaluates per instantiation (potentially hot) without re-parsing the outer template. Same async-source discipline: any remote-backed source is snapshotted into the stack before evaluate (inherited from `property-sources`). Errors are synchronous rich values split by phase: `PlaceholderSyntaxError { text, span, kind: UnbalancedBrace|EmptyKey }` at parse (charter #9, earliest legible — possibly compile-time), and `UnresolvedPlaceholder`/`CircularPlaceholder { chain }`/`MissingProperty`/`ConvertError` at evaluate. No cancellation concern (bounded pure CPU). The parsed `PlaceholderTemplate` is immutable + `Send+Sync`, so it can be cached on a bean definition and crossed across `.await`/threads freely; evaluate borrows the stack for the call's duration, so the stack's cross-task reachability remains the `environment` neighbour's concern.

#### R3 — Lazy resolution-context with a layered StringValueResolver chain + handler-driven policy (the binder-grade, hook-extensible engine)
Model resolution as a small, composable ENGINE: a `ResolutionContext` carrying the stack borrow, a layered chain of `StringValueResolver`s (Spring's actual `StringValueResolver` abstraction), and a `ResolveHandler` that observes/customizes/validates each step — mirroring the `binder`/`BindHandler` design so this feature shares machinery with its downstream `binder` neighbour rather than inventing a bespoke walk.

```rust
trait StringValueResolver { fn resolve(&self, key: &str, cx: &ResolutionContext) -> Option<ResolvedValue>; }
// chain: [PlaceholderResolver(over stack), RandomValueResolver(random.*), <user hooks>] tried in order
pub trait ResolveHandler {
    fn on_placeholder(&self, key: &str, cx: &ResolutionContext) {}
    fn on_resolved(&self, key: &str, v: &ResolvedValue) {}
    fn on_unresolved(&self, ph: &Placeholder, cx: &ResolutionContext) -> UnresolvedAction; // Strict|Lenient|Substitute(Cow)
}
pub struct ResolutionContext<'s> { stack: &'s PropertySourceStack, chain: &'s [Box<dyn StringValueResolver>], handler: &'s dyn ResolveHandler, visited: RefCell<SmallVec<[Box<str>; 8]>> }
```
The placeholder walk is R1's recursive scanner, but each `${key}` body is resolved by TRYING the `chain` in order (first-resolver-wins, mirroring first-source-wins), and the **strict/lenient decision is delegated to the handler's `on_unresolved` → UnresolvedAction** rather than a fixed field — so policy is pluggable (strict, lenient, or even a custom 'substitute a sentinel' for tooling). **Defaults** are handled before consulting the handler (a `:default` arm short-circuits `on_unresolved`). **Recursion + cycle detection** as R1 (visited set in the context). This is the heaviest realization, but it directly SHARES the resolver-chain + handler shape with the `binder` (which already wants a `BindHandler`) and gives synthetic sources (`random.*`) a first-class home as chain links rather than fake property sources. **Strict/lenient** is just two stock handler impls (`StrictHandler`, `LenientHandler`); a custom handler can log every resolution (for the `condition-report`/diagnostics neighbour) or enforce that all placeholders resolved in a validation pass.

**Pros:** Shares machinery with the downstream `binder` neighbour (which independently wants a handler/observer hook with on-start/on-success/on-failure) — the `ResolveHandler` is the same shape as `BindHandler`, advancing charter #11 (unification, share maximal common machinery) instead of a bespoke per-feature walk.; Strict/lenient becomes a pluggable `ResolveHandler` policy rather than a hard-coded fork — naturally extends to a 'collect all unresolved and report them together' validation pass (charter #9: a dedicated startup validation pass that lists EVERY unresolved placeholder at once, not fail-on-first), and a 'substitute sentinel for tooling' mode for `config-metadata`.; Synthetic/computed sources (`random.*`-style, env-interpolation, a future secrets resolver) are first-class `StringValueResolver` chain links — Spring's actual `StringValueResolver`/RandomValuePropertySource intent — instead of being shoehorned as fake property sources, keeping the `property-sources` stack purely declarative key/value (cleaner separation).; First-resolver-wins in the chain mirrors first-source-wins in the stack — one consistent precedence mental model across both this feature and `property-sources` (charter #1 DX).; The handler observing every step is the natural hot-path-free instrumentation point for `condition-report`/`origin-tracking` diagnostics: 'why did this placeholder resolve to X from source Y' is a handler callback, not a second pass.

**Cons:** By far the most machinery (chain + handler + context) for a feature whose 95% case is 'expand `${...}` over the stack, lenient or strict' — a real risk of over-engineering the read path, in tension with charter #2/#5 (don't build mechanism for its own sake); the simple cases pay the abstraction tax.; `Box<dyn StringValueResolver>` chain + `&dyn ResolveHandler` introduce dynamic dispatch and (for the chain) heap allocation on a path that R1/R2 keep monomorphic and allocation-light — a hot-path cost that must be justified, and a `RefCell<visited>` adds interior mutability (single-threaded-per-call, but a smell on a 'no global locks' charter unless clearly scoped to one resolution call).; The handler-driven `UnresolvedAction` makes the strict/lenient outcome harder to reason about statically — the same template can resolve differently depending on which handler is installed, which is powerful but erodes the 'this resolver is strict' clarity R1's field / R2's mode-arg give.; Coupling the resolver chain + handler shape to `binder` PRE-DECIDES a unification that is properly a Phase-2 call — adopting this in Phase 1 risks biasing the meta-architecture signal (charter operating rule: Phase 1 is implementation-agnostic); presented here as a CANDIDATE shape, explicitly deferred.; The chain's discovery (user `StringValueResolver` hooks across crates) makes this the realization MOST exposed to the cross-crate registration + DCE hazard — the very complexity R1/R2 mostly avoid by being self-contained.

**Mechanisms considered:** layered `StringValueResolver` chain (first-resolver-wins) over the stack, with `random.*`-style synthetic resolvers as chain links (Spring `StringValueResolver` + RandomValuePropertySource intent); `ResolveHandler` observer/policy hook (on_placeholder/on_resolved/on_unresolved→UnresolvedAction) deliberately shaped like the downstream `binder`'s `BindHandler` for unification (charter #11); strict/lenient as stock handler impls + a 'collect-all-unresolved' validation-pass handler for a richest-diagnostic startup gate (charter #9); `Box<dyn StringValueResolver>` chain discovered via link-time `linkme` slice (default 'classpath') + optional dynamic directory-scanned hook set (WASM 'classpath'); order from descriptor DATA; `RefCell<SmallVec<..>>` visited set scoped to a single resolution call for cycle detection (interior mutability confined, not shared); stable-only: object-safe traits, `Box<dyn>`, `RefCell`, `Cow`; reads synchronous (no async on the chain — synthetic resolvers must be non-blocking / pre-materialized)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Kernel-resident core (`PropertyResolver`, `ResolutionContext`, `StringValueResolver`, `ResolveHandler` traits in `leaf-core`). This realization is the MOST cross-crate-exposed of the three because the `StringValueResolver` chain is an explicit extension point: user/integration resolvers (a secrets backend, a `random.*` synthetic source, an env-interpolation resolver) are CONTRIBUTED across crates and inherit the full static-link-registry-vs-build-codegen duality — a default link-time `linkme` distributed-slice of `const ResolverHookDescriptor { priority, matches_prefix, ctor_fn }` (the default 'classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned resolver set (the WASM-module 'classpath'). DCE guardrails are CRITICAL here (more than R1/R2): const descriptors, runtime evaluation, force-link of opted-in resolver crates, and a zero-contribution self-check that loudly warns when an expected resolver crate registered nothing (a silently-missing secrets resolver would be a security-relevant failure). Chain ORDER must come from descriptor `priority` DATA, never link/section order. The `ResolveHandler` is typically installed programmatically by the bootstrap layer (one per resolution context), so it is NOT a cross-crate-discovered set — but if leaf later lets integrations contribute handlers, the same duality + guardrails apply. Origin-agnostic: a `StringValueResolver` is identical whether native, file-derived, or WASM-provided. The chain + handler shape is deliberately the SAME substrate the `binder` neighbour would discover its `BindHandler`s through — flagged as a Phase-2 unification opportunity, not a Phase-1 decision.

**T2 — execution context:** All resolution runs on the synchronous, non-blocking read path (cold during bootstrap config-data/profile resolution, hot during steady-state `@Value`-equivalent reads). The `StringValueResolver::resolve` contract is **synchronous and non-blocking** (charter #4): a synthetic resolver backed by remote data (a live secrets service) is FORBIDDEN to block/await in `resolve()` — it must have been materialized/cached during a cold async pre-step before joining the chain (the BootstrapContext discipline, same as `property-sources`). The `ResolveHandler` callbacks are likewise synchronous, non-blocking observers (logging/collecting), never an `.await` point. Cycle-detection `RefCell<visited>` is confined to a single resolution call on a single task — no cross-thread sharing, no global lock (charter #5). Errors propagate as synchronous rich values, with the handler enabling a 'collect every unresolved placeholder and fail once with the full list' validation pass (charter #9 richest-diagnostic). No cancellation concern (bounded pure CPU; any async pre-materialization of a synthetic resolver's backing happens in a separate cold async step with its own cancellation, BEFORE resolution). The chain (`Box<dyn StringValueResolver>`) and handler are `Send+Sync` when their members are, so a resolution context can cross `.await`/threads as long as the borrowed stack outlives it — stack reachability remains the `environment` neighbour's concern.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's lenient-by-default resolver injects an unresolved `${catalog.name}` as the literal string — a silent foot-gun the docs themselves flag as 'a classic, easy-to-miss source of bugs.' Rust's charter #9 (fail with rich, actionable context, as early as the richest diagnostic allows) is in direct TENSION with replicating lenient-as-default. leaf can model BOTH modes (intent parity) but must decide whether the DEFAULT is the Spring foot-gun (lenient) or the safer strict — and whether strict should run as a dedicated startup VALIDATION PASS that collects every unresolved placeholder at once rather than failing on the first. This is intent-vs-safety tension the realizations expose but do not resolve.
- Spring's `null` == 'property absent' as the monotonic-override mechanism maps to Rust's `Option<ResolvedValue>` (None == absent, Some(empty) == present-and-wins) — sharper than Java's null. But the resolver layers a SECOND absence concept on top: a placeholder that resolves to NOTHING (no key, no default). leaf must keep these distinct: `stack.get` returning `None` (key absent → fall through / use default) vs the placeholder being UNRESOLVABLE (no source AND no default → strict-fail / lenient-pass-through). Java blurred both as 'null'; Rust forces two `Option`/`Result` layers that must not be conflated.
- Spring's `PropertyPlaceholderHelper` recurses on the JVM call stack with GC'd strings and tolerates deep nesting + on-the-fly value re-expansion freely. In Rust, recurse-on-resolved-VALUE means re-scanning a freshly-resolved `String` whose lifetime is owned-not-borrowed, breaking the `Cow::Borrowed` zero-alloc fast path the moment any expansion happens; and unbounded call-stack recursion on a pathological template is a (cycle-detection-mitigated but not depth-capped) hazard. leaf must choose between an explicit depth cap, an iterative worklist, or trusting cycle detection — a correctness/robustness decision Spring never had to make explicitly.
- Spring fuses parse and resolve into one `parseStringValue` scan; malformed placeholders (`${unbalanced`) surface only at resolve time as runtime errors. Rust's charter #9 prefers the earliest legible failure, which TEMPTS a parse/evaluate split (R2) that catches syntax errors at parse — even compile time for literal `#[value("...")]` templates. But pushing grammar parsing to compile/macro time risks thickening the macro (charter #10 thin macros) unless the const form is a trivial data array. Earliest-diagnostic vs thin-macro is a real Phase-2-flavored tension this feature surfaces.
- Spring's strict-vs-lenient mode is a property of WHICH resolver/method you call (the lenient embedded `@Value` resolver vs strict `resolveRequiredPlaceholders`/`PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer`) on a SHARED environment — a loose, contextual policy. Rust pushes toward making the mode explicit: a per-resolver field (R1, but then one env needs two resolvers), an evaluate-time argument (R2, cleanest), or a pluggable handler policy (R3, most flexible but least statically-clear). The flexible-mode DX vs Rust-explicit-policy tension has no free lunch.
- Spring's `StringValueResolver` chain and `random.*` synthetic source are dynamically discovered classpath beans. Rust has no classpath: any user-contributed resolver hook (R3's chain) must be discovered via link-time registration (DCE-hazardous, silent-empty failure mode) or build-time codegen, and ORDER cannot come from link/section order. The richest extension model (R3) is therefore the one most exposed to the cross-crate composition hazard — the same hazard `component-scanning`/`auto-configuration` face — which argues for sharing ONE discovery substrate, a Phase-2 keystone, not this feature's call.
- Spring sources can be consulted lazily at read time on the JVM where blocking IO mid-resolution is tolerated; a synthetic resolver (secrets, env interpolation) could block. leaf's async-first + hot-path-no-block discipline FORBIDS a `StringValueResolver::resolve()` (or `stack.get()`) that blocks or awaits — so any resolver whose backing is remote/async must be pre-materialized into an in-memory snapshot during a cold async pre-step BEFORE it joins the chain/stack. This narrows what a 'computed source' can do at resolve time and pushes async into a separate bootstrap pipeline (the BootstrapContext analogue), shared with what `property-sources` and `environment` already flagged.

### Interactions
- **property-sources** — HARD DEPENDENCY (this feature is the primary CONSUMER of property-sources' first-source-wins lookup primitive). property-sources owns the ordered named stack, the ordering-mutation vocabulary, and the single-key `get(key) -> Option<PropertyValue>` walk (with never-blend + null-never-overrides baked in). THIS feature borrows the sealed stack and layers the `${...}` placeholder expansion, defaults, recursion+cycle-detection, strict/lenient policy, and required/typed/contains modifiers ON TOP of that walk. We do NOT re-implement the stack, the ordering, or the never-blend rule — we inherit them; placeholder expansion must preserve single-source/single-origin (never accidentally blend across sources during expansion).
- **environment** — This feature realizes the READ half of environment's read/mutate-segregated seam. environment owns the façade, reachability (who-can-reach the resolver from where), and the &mut/token/builder mutate seam; the `PropertyResolver` trait THIS feature defines is exactly what environment's read view (`Environment: PropertyResolver`) exposes to application code. environment also decides the resolver's HANDLE shape (borrowed `&'s` vs Arc-rooted) and lifetime across `.await`/threads — we depend on that decision, we do not make it. The resolver deliberately CANNOT mutate sources (least-privilege), matching environment's intent that business code consumes but never reshapes precedence.
- **origin-tracking** — Every value this feature returns is a `ResolvedValue { raw, origin }` carrying the winning source's provenance — origin-tracking's 'which source / file+line set this' is answerable from the resolved value. CRITICAL: provenance must survive placeholder expansion meaningfully — a placeholder-expanded value's origin is a question this feature must define (the origin of the OUTER key? a composite of every source touched during expansion?). R3's handler is the natural hook to record the full set of sources consulted during a multi-placeholder expansion for diagnostics. The single-traceable-origin guarantee is inherited from property-sources for a direct key, but expansion complicates it — flagged.
- **type-conversion** — DOWNSTREAM seam: `get_property_as::<T>()` resolves to the raw winning string (after placeholder expansion, single origin, never blended) and THEN coerces via type-conversion (String→int, comma-separated→Vec, durations/sizes/enums). This feature owns resolve-to-raw-string + placeholder walk; type-conversion owns the coercion. A `ConvertError` carries the raw value + target type + origin (charter #9). The never-blend invariant is preserved BEFORE any coercion — conversion sees exactly one source's raw string.
- **value-injection** — value-injection (the `@Value`-equivalent `${property}` injection point) is a PRIMARY consumer: a `#[value("${app.mode:default}")]`-style injection ultimately calls this feature's `resolve_placeholders` + typed read. R2's parse-once `PlaceholderTemplate` is the natural amortization for a `@Value` template resolved on every prototype-bean creation, and R2's compile-time parse of the literal template is where malformed-placeholder errors could surface at compile time. value-injection owns the injection-point wiring; this feature owns the actual `${...}` resolution + strict/lenient + default semantics it delegates to.
- **profiles** — profiles reads the active-profile set (leaf's `spring.profiles.active` analogue) THROUGH this resolver via the same first-source-wins + placeholder walk — so a command-line/env source can override a file-declared active-profiles, and the active-profiles value can itself contain `${...}` placeholders that this feature expands. profiles owns the expression grammar and active/include/group levers; this feature is merely the resolver those reserved keys (and their placeholders) resolve through. Note Spring's subtlety preserved by property-sources' never-blend: `spring.profiles.include` is per-source, not list-merged.
- **config-data** — config-data is a cold-bootstrap CONSUMER: it resolves `${...}` placeholders in config-file LOCATIONS and `spring.config.import` values (Spring's `@PropertySource` location supports `${...}`) through this resolver, AND it contributes the loaded sources that this resolver later reads. Ordering matters — config-data must resolve location placeholders against the sources available SO FAR during its single-pass load. This feature provides the placeholder walk config-data calls; config-data owns the file-format loading and precedence-order policy.
- **relaxed-binding** — relaxed-binding maps one canonical kebab-case key to many source-specific forms. Spring's 'hidden concept: placeholders are asymmetric' — `${demo.item-price}` (canonical kebab) resolves via relaxed lookup across .properties and env, but `${demo.itemPrice}` will NOT. This feature must therefore consult relaxed-binding's canonicalization at the `stack.get(key)` step (or rely on each source's relaxed `get`), so a placeholder's KEY is canonicalized before lookup. relaxed-binding owns the canonical-form mapping; this feature must use the canonical form when resolving a placeholder key — a coupling at the lookup boundary.
- **binder** — binder (the programmatic engine @ConfigurationProperties uses) shares deep machinery candidates with this feature. R3 deliberately shapes its `ResolveHandler` like binder's `BindHandler` (on-start/on-success/on-failure observer) to advance unification (charter #11). binder resolves leaf-level property values THROUGH this resolver (placeholder expansion happens during binding), and binder's result-monad + handler model is the same family as this feature's strict/lenient + handler policy. Whether they SHARE one substrate is a Phase-2 unification call surfaced here, not decided.
- **expression-language** — Spring's `@Value` supports BOTH `${property}` placeholders (THIS feature) AND `#{SpEL}` expressions (expression-language). They are DISTINCT engines with distinct syntax that compose in one `@Value` string. This feature owns ONLY the `${...}` placeholder walk; expression-language owns `#{...}` evaluation. The boundary must be clean — a `@Value` string is split/dispatched between the two engines (Spring resolves `${...}` first, then `#{...}`), and this feature must not try to evaluate expressions. Flagged: who owns the dispatch/ordering between the two engines.
- **post-processor-spi** — In Spring, `${...}` placeholder resolution is implemented as a `BeanFactoryPostProcessor` (`PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer`) — the 'edit the blueprint before the product exists' tier, and it's WHERE strict mode gets turned on. leaf's analogue: this resolver is consulted during the definition-editing / bootstrap phase (resolving `${...}` in definitions/locations before instantiation) — the BFPP-tier seam. post-processor-spi formalizes that edit-blueprint phase; this feature provides the resolution the placeholder-configurer-analogue performs there, including the strict-mode toggle that BFPP enables.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- DEFAULT mode — lenient or strict? Spring defaults to lenient (unresolved `${x}` injected as literal text), a documented foot-gun in direct tension with charter #9 (fail with rich context). Should leaf flip the default to strict, keep Spring's lenient for parity, or run strict as a dedicated STARTUP VALIDATION PASS that collects EVERY unresolved placeholder at once (richest diagnostic) rather than failing on first? Cross-cuts environment (which view is handed where), value-injection, and config-data; must be one framework-wide policy, not per-resolver.
- Where does strict/lenient mode LIVE — a per-resolver field (R1), an evaluate-time argument (R2), or a pluggable handler policy (R3)? This couples to environment's read-view handle decision and to whether the same environment must simultaneously expose a lenient `@Value` resolver AND a strict required-resolver (Spring's reality). A cross-cutting policy-placement question spanning environment, value-injection, and binder.
- Should the placeholder grammar be PARSED separately from evaluation (R2's AST), enabling compile-time malformed-placeholder errors for literal `#[value("...")]` templates and parse-once amortization — or fused (R1) for simplicity? This trades charter #9 (earliest legible diagnostic) against charter #10 (thin macros) and #5 (don't build machinery for one-shot templates), and couples to value-injection's injection-point amortization needs and the macro/codegen boundary (a §3 deferred decision).
- Does the resolution engine SHARE a handler/observer substrate with the downstream `binder` (R3's `ResolveHandler` ≈ binder's `BindHandler`) and a discovery substrate with component-scanning/auto-config for any `StringValueResolver` hooks — or stay a self-contained walk (R1/R2)? This is the charter #11 unification question; adopting R3's shared shape in Phase 1 would pre-bias the meta-architecture, so it is deliberately UNRESOLVED. Spans binder, expression-language, and the cross-crate composition keystone.
- How is the `${...}` placeholder engine cleanly separated from AND composed with the `#{...}` expression-language engine inside a single `@Value`-equivalent string — who owns the dispatch and the ordering (Spring resolves `${...}` before `#{...}`)? This boundary spans value-injection, expression-language, and this feature, and needs ONE owner for the split/dispatch logic.
- What is the provenance/origin of a PLACEHOLDER-EXPANDED value (vs a direct key)? A value assembled from multiple placeholders touching multiple sources has no single origin — is it the outer key's origin, a composite, or unrepresented? This cross-cuts origin-tracking and the binder's error reporting, and decides whether the resolved-value type can always carry a single `Origin` or needs a composite-provenance representation.
- Recursion robustness: is recurse-on-resolved-VALUE (a resolved value containing `${...}`) capped by an explicit depth limit, an iterative worklist, or trusted entirely to cycle detection? Spring trusts its visited-set; leaf must decide a robustness policy against pathological/adversarial templates, and whether the cap is a configurable knob — a small but real correctness decision with no Spring precedent.
- Where does the async pre-materialization pipeline live for a synthetic/computed resolver (R3's `StringValueResolver` for secrets/remote interpolation) or an async-backed source, given resolve() MUST be synchronous non-blocking? This is the same BootstrapContext-analogue async-fetch→snapshot→sync-use pipeline that property-sources and environment already flagged — must align framework-wide, not be re-invented here.

### Notes
Scope discipline: I treated this feature strictly as the READ-PATH ENGINE layered on top of the `property-sources` first-source-wins primitive (which was handed to me as a completed upstream design and which I build on concretely, not reinvent). This feature owns: the least-privilege `PropertyResolver` read interface (get/get-or-default/get-typed/get-required/contains/resolve-placeholders/resolve-required-placeholders); the `${...}` placeholder expander (defaults via `:`, recursive resolution at BOTH the key site `${${meta}}` and the resolved-value site, cycle detection with a full-chain diagnostic, partial/literal-text expansion); the strict-vs-lenient policy (the signature Spring foot-gun); and the required/typed/contains modifiers. I deliberately did NOT redesign the stack/ordering/never-blend (property-sources, upstream), the read/mutate seam + reachability (environment, upstream), profile grammar/levers (profiles), file-format loading + precedence policy (config-data), canonical-key mapping (relaxed-binding), string→T coercion (type-conversion), provenance internals (origin-tracking), or the `#{...}` engine (expression-language). The genuinely divergent axis for THIS feature is HOW the placeholder walk + strict/lenient mode + recursion-with-cycle-detection are represented in Rust: R1 = eager recursive char-scanner over a borrowed stack view (max intent fidelity to PropertySourcesPropertyResolver+PropertyPlaceholderHelper, simplest, allocation-light Cow fast path, one-shot-optimal); R2 = parse-to-AST then evaluate (syntax-vs-resolve error split, possible compile-time malformed-placeholder detection for literal #[value] templates, parse-once amortization for hot injection points, mode-as-evaluate-arg); R3 = lazy ResolutionContext with a layered StringValueResolver chain + BindHandler-shaped ResolveHandler policy (most extensible, first-class home for random.*/synthetic/secrets resolvers, strict/lenient/validation-pass as pluggable handlers, deliberately shares shape with the downstream binder for charter #11 unification — but heaviest and most cross-crate-exposed). All three keep reads SYNCHRONOUS and non-blocking (charter #4 — placeholder expansion is pure CPU over already-materialized strings; any async/remote source or synthetic resolver must be pre-snapshotted in a cold async step before resolve), borrow the sealed stack rather than owning/cloning it (charter #5 — no gratuitous Arc; R3's dyn chain is the one place dynamic dispatch enters, justified only by extensibility), encode unresolved-vs-absent as distinct Option/Result layers, and surface rich split diagnostics (MissingProperty / UnresolvedPlaceholder / CircularPlaceholder{chain} / ConvertError, plus R2's parse-time PlaceholderSyntaxError) per charter #9. Charter alignment: hot read path synchronous + lock-free in all three; macros stay thin (charter #10 — at most a #[value] macro emitting a call to a hand-writable parse/template API, never logic); intent parity is literal in the read-method names + the lenient-leaves-literal/strict-fails fork + the recurse-on-key-and-value algorithm, while the representation is rust-native (charter #2/#11). Nightly review: nothing needs nightly — recursive scanning, enums/Box<[T]> for an AST, Cow, Option/Result, object-safe traits, RefCell for a call-scoped visited set all on stable; reads synchronous so no AFIT/RPITIT boxing tax. The cross-crate touchpoints (optional StringValueResolver hooks + optional syntax override) are OPT-IN — the core resolver is cross-crate-inert — and where present they share the SAME link-time-registry/DCE substrate (linkme default 'classpath' + optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM 'classpath', order from descriptor DATA, zero-contribution self-check) as component scanning; whether the engine SHARES one handler/discovery substrate with binder and the discovery features is flagged UNRESOLVED for Phase 2, consistent with the operating rule that Phase 1 stays implementation-agnostic.

## Externalized Config & Config Data API  `[config-data]`

Spring intent (doc §"The Config Data API", lines 1447-1521, plus §"Boot's externalized configuration precedence" 1415-1443). `config-data` is the engine that (a) DISCOVERS and LOADS application/profile config files and (b) places every loaded document into the `property-sources` stack at its correct precedence, replacing pre-2.4 Boot's confusing bootstrap-context + EnvironmentPostProcessor-with-profile-ordering mess with a DETERMINISTIC SINGLE-PASS DECLARATIVE model. The load-bearing intent to replicate (INTENT, not the JVM mechanism):

(1) DETERMINISTIC SINGLE-PASS TRAVERSAL. Loading is one forward pass over an ordered worklist of *locations* (classpath root, classpath /config, cwd, ./config/, ./config/*/), resolving `application.{properties,yaml}` and, per active profile, `application-{profile}.{properties,yaml}`. NO order-dependent cascades, NO mutual recursion between loading and activation. Within the layer the precedence (low→high) is: jar `application.*` < jar profile-specific < external `application.*` < external profile-specific; profile-specific ALWAYS overrides non-specific; with multiple active profiles a LAST-WINS strategy applies at the location-GROUP level (`prod,live` → `application-live` beats `application-prod`), resolved per config-location group during traversal, NOT by textual file order on disk; if both `.properties` and `.yaml` exist in one location, `.properties` WINS.

(2) `spring.config.import` — DECLARATIVE COMPOSITION FROM INSIDE A DOCUMENT. A document can `spring.config.import: "optional:configtree:/etc/config/,extra.properties"` to pull further documents, inserted immediately BELOW (higher precedence than) the importing document. Buried rules: imports are IDEMPOTENT (loaded once no matter how many declarations, keeping first-discovery position); locations are FIXED (start with `/` or a URL prefix like `file:`/`classpath:`) or IMPORT-RELATIVE (resolved relative to the declaring file), with `optional:` stripped BEFORE that determination and chained imports compounding relatively; bracketed hints `[encoding=utf-8]`/`[extension=.yaml]`; missing locations raise `ConfigDataLocationNotFoundException` unless `optional:` or `spring.config.on-not-found=ignore`.

(3) ORIGIN-AGNOSTIC SOURCE PREFIXES — your platform's config IS a property source. `env:MY_CONFIG` parses a (multiline) env var as a properties/yaml document; `configtree:/etc/config/` maps a directory tree to keys (each file's path → key, contents → value; dotted filenames map directly; `*/` aggregates trees alphabetically) — purpose-built for K8s ConfigMaps/Secrets and Docker `/run/secrets/`. The mental model: no adapter needed; a directory-of-files or a big env var is already a property source.

(4) MULTI-DOCUMENT ACTIVATION. One file holds ordered logical documents split by `---` (or `#---`/`!---` in `.properties`), processed in order, later overriding earlier. `spring.config.activate.on-profile` (a profile EXPRESSION, reusing the `profiles` grammar) and `.on-cloud-platform` conditionally enable a document — the Boot-level analogue of `@Profile` for property DOCUMENTS. HARD RULE (2.4+): a document carrying `activate.on-profile` may NOT itself set `spring.profiles.active/.include/.group/.default` (raises `InvalidConfigDataPropertyException`) — profiles are decided UP FRONT, then documents are FILTERED, never mutually recursive. The two profile layers (Config-Data document activation vs `@Profile` bean gating) SHARE one active-profile set but run at DIFFERENT PHASES: properties resolve first, then beans filter.

(5) EARLY-CONSUMED CONTROL KEYS. `spring.config.name` (base filename, default `application`), `spring.config.location` (REPLACES defaults), `spring.config.additional-location` (ADDS to defaults) are consumed EXTREMELY EARLY — before files are located — so they only work as an env var/system property/command-line arg; placing them inside `application.properties` is silently ignored. `RandomValuePropertySource` (`random.*`) and `SPRING_APPLICATION_JSON` (a JSON blob flattened into the namespace) sit at fixed precedence rungs.

(6) THE FULL EXTERNALIZED-CONFIG PRECEDENCE ORDER (lowest→highest, lines 1419-1433): defaultProperties < @PropertySource < Config-Data files < random.* < OS env vars < Java system props < JNDI < servlet < SPRING_APPLICATION_JSON < command-line args < test `properties` < @DynamicPropertySource < @TestPropertySource < devtools. Design philosophy: specificity/intentionality RISE to the top — things known later/closer-to-runtime override things known earlier/at-build-time; one immutable artifact behaves correctly across all environments (12-factor). `null` never overrides (absence is the only fall-through — inherited from `property-sources`).

SCOPE BOUNDARY. The upstream `property-sources` design owns the ordered named stack, the `PropertySource` trait, the add_first/add_last/add_before/add_after vocabulary, first-source-wins, and never-blend; `profiles` owns the expression grammar/evaluator AND the active/include/group/default activation algebra (the sealed `ActiveProfiles`); `origin-tracking` owns the `Origin` type and is the consumer config-data PRODUCES fine origins for (file+line at parse time); `relaxed-binding` owns key canonicalization; `environment` owns the seal/customize seam; `binder`/`config-properties` consume the resolved stack. THIS feature owns ONLY: the format LOADERS (.properties/.yaml/configtree/env:/SAJ parse → in-memory `PropertySource` + fine origins), the deterministic single-pass LOCATION-RESOLUTION + import-traversal engine, the multi-document SPLIT + activation-filter (delegating the predicate to `profiles`), the early-control-key bootstrap, and the OWNERSHIP of the full externalized-config PRECEDENCE-ORDER POLICY (which loaded document/source lands at which rung, expressed as ordering-mutation calls on the `property-sources` stack). The genuinely divergent Rust axis: HOW the single-pass import-traversal + multi-doc activation + precedence assembly is structured — an eager imperative worklist that mutates the stack as it goes, a pure plan-then-apply contribution model, or a lazy resolver-graph — and how async file/network IO is fenced off from the synchronous stack the result must join.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Eager imperative single-pass loader: a worklist that resolves locations, splits multi-docs, and mutates the stack via add_* as it walks (literal ConfigDataEnvironmentPostProcessor transcription)
The most direct Rust transcription of Boot's `ConfigDataEnvironmentPostProcessor` + `ConfigDataImporter`. One cold-path async function drives a deterministic forward pass over an explicit worklist, and PLACES each loaded document onto the `property-sources` stack as it goes using that feature's ordering vocabulary.

```rust
pub struct ConfigDataLoader<'e> { env: &'e mut EnvBuilder, loaders: LoaderRegistry, loaded: HashSet<ConfigDataResource> }
pub trait ConfigDataLoader: Send + Sync {           // origin-agnostic format loader
    fn handles(&self, loc: &ConfigDataLocation) -> bool;     // .properties / .yaml / configtree: / env:
    async fn load(&self, loc: &ConfigDataLocation, ctx: &LoadCtx) -> Result<Vec<LoadedDocument>, ConfigDataError>;
}
pub struct LoadedDocument { props: Vec<(KeyRef, PropertyValue)>, activate_on: Option<ProfileExpr>, declares_imports: Vec<ConfigDataLocation> }
```
The pass (all on the cold bootstrap path, single-threaded ownership of `&mut EnvBuilder`): (1) read the early-control keys (`config.name`/`location`/`additional-location`) from the ALREADY-PRESENT high-precedence sources (env/sysprops/cmdline) — never from files; (2) compute the ordered location worklist (default locations or replaced/extended); (3) for each location in order, pick the winning loader (`.properties` beats `.yaml` in the same location), `loader.load(..).await` to get the ordered `Vec<LoadedDocument>`; (4) split is done by the loader; for each document, evaluate `activate_on` against the sealed `ActiveProfiles` via `profiles::matches` — a non-matching document is SKIPPED entirely (graceful, recorded in a load trace); (5) a matching document's `spring.config.import` locations are PUSHED onto the worklist immediately after the current item (so imports insert directly below the importer), deduped against `loaded` for idempotency; (6) the document's props are wrapped as an in-memory `MapPropertySource` (carrying the loader-stamped fine `Origin` per value) and inserted into the stack via `add_after`/`add_before` at the rung the precedence policy assigns. The full externalized-config order is encoded as a fixed table of named anchor sources the loader seeds first (defaultProperties at the bottom, command-line/SAJ near the top), and config-data documents are spliced between the `defaultProperties` anchor and the `random.*` anchor. The HARD RULE (a document with `activate.on-profile` must not set `profiles.active/include/group/default`) is checked inline when the document is admitted, raising a rich `ConfigDataError::IllegalActivationDocument`. After the pass the env seals and the stack is frozen.

**Pros:** Maximal intent fidelity to Boot's actual engine: a single forward pass with a worklist, imports inserted below the importer, idempotency via a loaded-set, profile-document filtering inline — a Boot maintainer recognizes `ConfigDataImporter`/`ConfigDataEnvironmentContributors` directly (charter #1 DX, #2 intent-shape).; The deterministic single-pass + insert-below-importer + last-wins-at-location-group semantics fall out naturally from walking an ordered worklist and using `property-sources`' explicit add_before/add_after vocabulary — no separate ordering machinery, the stack IS the order.; Async file/network IO is cleanly fenced: `loader.load(..).await` happens on the cold bootstrap path inside the loop, each loaded document is MATERIALIZED to an in-memory `MapPropertySource` BEFORE it joins the synchronous stack — exactly the async-pre-step→in-memory-snapshot→sync-add discipline property-sources/environment mandate (charter #3).; Origin production is local and eager: the loader stamps file+line/column onto each value as it parses, so origin-tracking gets fine origins for free at the natural place (the parse) — no second pass.; Simplest mental model and fewest moving parts; the load trace (skipped docs, import chain, dedup hits) is a direct byproduct for condition-report-style diagnostics (charter #9).

**Cons:** Eager stack mutation during the walk means the env mutate-seam must hold `&mut EnvBuilder` for the whole async loading pass — a long-lived exclusive borrow across `.await` points; if any loader needs the partially-built stack (e.g. to resolve a placeholder in an import location like `import: ${EXTRA_DIR}/x.yaml`) the borrow discipline gets delicate (read-then-mutate interleaving on the same builder).; The precedence-order POLICY is baked into the imperative insertion calls (add_after this anchor, add_before that one); changing the global order means editing the walk, and the policy is implicit in code rather than a declarative table that auto-config/profiles can co-own (tension with the cross-feature precedence ownership question).; Hard to make the pass re-entrant or incremental: a late env customizer that wants to add a location after the main pass forces a partial re-walk; Boot's single-pass assumption is faithful but inflexible (matches Spring, but the rigidity is real).; Diagnostics for a precedence surprise ('why did application-prod beat my file?') require reconstructing the walk order, since order is an emergent property of insertion calls rather than stored data — weaker than a model that records each contribution's intended rung as data (R2).; Mixing IO, parsing, activation-filtering, import-expansion, and stack-mutation in one loop is a lot of distinct concerns in one async fn — testable end-to-end but the sub-steps are entangled, less unit-testable than a plan-then-apply split.

**Mechanisms considered:** one cold-path `async fn` driving an ordered worklist (VecDeque) with insert-imports-after-current for the single-pass traversal; `ConfigDataLoader` async trait (AFIT, stable 1.75) per format — origin-agnostic so .properties/.yaml/configtree:/env:/SAJ and a future WASM-module loader all conform; `HashSet<ConfigDataResource>` loaded-set for import idempotency keyed by resolved canonical location; eager `add_before`/`add_after` calls on the property-sources stack using a fixed anchor table for the externalized-config precedence order; inline activation-filter via `profiles::matches(&ProfileExpr, &ActiveProfiles)` (delegated to the profiles feature), document skipped on no-match; loader stamps fine `Origin` (file+line/column) per value at parse time for origin-tracking; cross-crate loaders discovered via the SHARED link-time descriptor substrate (linkme default + optional dynamic/WASM source) — candidate, not decision

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The single-pass engine + `ConfigDataLoader` trait + `ConfigDataLocation`/`LoadedDocument` types are kernel/feature types in `leaf-core`/the config-data crate, named by absolute path. The cross-crate surface is WHICH FORMAT LOADERS exist (a `.toml` loader, a remote-config loader, a Vault loader) — these are the discovered set and ride the SAME link-time-registry-vs-build-codegen substrate as property-sources' source-contributors and component scanning: a default `linkme` distributed-slice of `const ConfigDataLoaderDescriptor` (fn pointer `fn() -> Box<dyn ConfigDataLoader>` + the location prefixes/extensions it `handles`) = the static 'classpath', PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned loader set = the WASM-module 'classpath'. DCE guardrails apply verbatim (const descriptors, runtime evaluation, force-link of opted-in loader crates, zero-contribution self-check: a `configtree:` prefix with no registered loader must fail LOUDLY, never silently produce an empty stack). CRITICAL: loader-selection MUST be by descriptor DATA (the `handles` predicate), never link/section order. The engine itself is a single owned object the binary constructs once at the env-seal point (the only place the full resolved loader set + the real filesystem are visible) — consistent with 'the final binary sees the whole graph'. Origin-agnostic: a native loader, a configtree directory loader, and a future WASM loader are indistinguishable to the worklist.

**T2 — execution context:** STRICTLY COLD-PATH, the whole pass runs once during environment-seal in refresh-lifecycle bring-up, before any bean/eager-singleton exists. It is the ONE place in config-data that performs real ASYNC IO: file reads, directory walks (configtree:), and potentially network (remote config) all happen via `loader.load(..).await` on the bootstrap executor — this is permitted because it is cold-path (charter #3/#4: cold-path async IO is fine, polling/retry/backoff for a flaky remote source is a permitted cold-path poll loop). Each loaded document is MATERIALIZED to an in-memory `MapPropertySource` before it joins the stack, so all subsequent reads stay synchronous/non-blocking (the sync-read invariant property-sources requires). Two happens-before edges this imposes: (a) the early-control keys and the active-profile set (`ActiveProfiles`, resolved by `profiles` from the high-precedence sources) must be available BEFORE the location worklist is computed and BEFORE document activation is filtered — config-data depends on `profiles` having sealed activation up front (the 'decide profiles, then filter' rule); (b) the stack must be sealed AFTER the pass completes. Cancellation: the loading pass is cancellable as a unit (e.g. a startup timeout) since it is a single awaited task tree; a cancelled or failed load surfaces a rich `ConfigDataError` (location, prefix, optional-or-not, the IO cause, the import chain that led here) aggregated into the startup validation gate (charter #9) — a missing non-`optional:` location fails loudly; an `optional:` miss is a recorded no-op. Errors never panic; async Drop being unavailable means any partially-opened resource (file handle, dir iterator) is closed by explicit RAII guards within the cold pass.

#### R2 — Plan-then-apply: a pure resolver produces an ordered list of (rung, document-contribution) descriptors; a separate applier mutates the stack
Split the engine into a PURE deterministic PLANNER and a trivial APPLIER, so the precedence-order policy and the single-pass traversal become inspectable DATA rather than emergent insertion calls. The planner does the async IO and emits a fully-ordered plan; the applier just folds the plan onto the `property-sources` stack.

```rust
pub struct ConfigDataPlan { contributions: Vec<Contribution> }   // already in final precedence order, low→high
pub struct Contribution { rung: PrecedenceRung, source_name: SourceName, props: Vec<(KeyRef, PropertyValue)>, provenance: LoadStep }
pub enum PrecedenceRung { DefaultProperties, ConfigDataFile { group: u16, profile_specific: bool, external: bool }, Random, SpringApplicationJson, CommandLine, /* ... the full externalized-config ladder as an ENUM */ }
pub async fn plan(levers: &EarlyControlKeys, profiles: &ActiveProfiles, loaders: &LoaderRegistry, fs: &dyn ConfigFs) -> Result<ConfigDataPlan, ConfigDataError>;
pub fn apply(plan: ConfigDataPlan, stack: &mut PropertySourceStack);   // pure, sync, infallible-ordering
```
The planner runs the single-pass worklist EXACTLY as R1 (locations → loaders → multi-doc split → activation filter via `profiles::matches` → import expansion with idempotency), but instead of mutating the stack it records each admitted document as a `Contribution` tagged with its `PrecedenceRung` (a total-ordered enum encoding the full externalized-config ladder) plus the location-group/profile-specific/external flags that drive last-wins-at-location-group. The planner then STABLE-SORTS contributions by `(rung, group, profile_specific, discovery_index)` to realize 'profile-specific always overrides non-specific' and 'last active profile wins at group level' as a DECLARATIVE SORT KEY, not as insertion order. `apply` then folds the sorted plan onto the stack with `add_last` in order (lowest first) — a pure, synchronous, allocation-bounded step. The precedence POLICY lives entirely in the `PrecedenceRung` ordering + the sort comparator, co-ownable with profiles/auto-config (which contribute their own rungs). Origins are stamped by loaders into the `Contribution.props` during planning; the `LoadStep` provenance (which location, which import chain, whether it overrode a sibling) is retained for diagnostics.

**Pros:** The precedence-order POLICY is DECLARATIVE DATA (the `PrecedenceRung` total order + sort comparator), not emergent insertion calls — directly answers Boot's PropertySourceOrderingBeanFactoryPostProcessor concern and lets profiles/auto-config CO-OWN the ladder by contributing their own rungs without editing config-data's walk (charter #11 unification).; The planner is a PURE async function over an injected `ConfigFs` abstraction → exhaustively unit-testable across the whole matrix (multi-profile last-wins, .properties-beats-.yaml, import idempotency, optional-miss, activation filtering) WITHOUT a real filesystem or container — far higher test confidence on the buried rules than R1's entangled loop (charter #9 correctness).; `apply` is pure/sync/infallible-ordering: stack mutation needs `&mut` only for a brief non-async fold, so the long-lived `&mut EnvBuilder`-across-`.await` borrow problem of R1 disappears — IO holds no stack borrow, the applier holds no awaits (cleaner ownership, charter #5).; Last-wins-at-location-group and profile-specific-overrides-non-specific become a stable sort key (`group, profile_specific, discovery_index`) — exactly Boot's 'resolved per config-location group, NOT by textual file order' semantics expressed as a comparator, with a recorded discovery_index for tie-break determinism.; The `ConfigDataPlan` is a natural diagnostic artifact and an actuator-env-analogue input: 'here is every contribution, its rung, its origin, and whether it overrode a sibling' is a single inspectable value (charter #9), and a `--check`-style dump can be compared in CI.

**Cons:** A placeholder INSIDE an import location (`import: ${EXTRA_DIR}/x.yaml` or `import: configtree:${SECRETS}`) needs property-resolution over the partially-built stack DURING planning — but the stack isn't built until `apply`; the planner must therefore carry its own provisional lookup view (the contributions accumulated so far, in provisional order) to resolve such placeholders, partially reconstructing the stack it was trying to avoid touching (a real wrinkle Boot handles because it mutates eagerly).; Two-stage model is more machinery than R1's single loop: a `PrecedenceRung` enum that must enumerate the FULL externalized-config ladder (and stay in sync with what profiles/auto-config/test layers contribute) plus a sort comparator plus a plan type — more surface to get right and to keep faithful to Boot's exact 15-rung order.; Buffering every contribution's props in the plan before applying means the whole resolved config is held twice transiently (in the plan and then in the stack) — more peak memory during bootstrap than R1's stream-into-stack (cold-path, bounded, but real).; The pure-planner abstraction (`ConfigFs` injection) is a layer Boot doesn't have; it is great for testing but is extra indirection a reviewer must learn, and the real `ConfigFs` impl still does all the async IO R1 did (the complexity moves, it doesn't vanish).; Stable-sort determinism depends on a well-defined `discovery_index`; if two loaders in the same location/group race (parallel IO), the index must be assigned by the deterministic worklist order, not IO completion order — a subtle constraint the parallelism must respect.

**Mechanisms considered:** pure `async fn plan(...) -> Result<ConfigDataPlan>` separated from a pure sync `apply(plan, &mut stack)`; `PrecedenceRung` total-ordered enum encoding the full externalized-config ladder as DATA (co-ownable rungs for profiles/auto-config/test layers); stable-sort by `(rung, location_group, profile_specific, external, discovery_index)` to realize last-wins-at-group + profile-specific-overrides + .properties-beats-.yaml declaratively; injected `ConfigFs` trait (real impl does async IO; test impl is in-memory) for exhaustive unit testing of the buried rules; provisional accumulated-contributions lookup view inside the planner for placeholders in import locations (the one place it must reconstruct partial-stack reads); loader-stamped fine `Origin` carried in `Contribution.props`; `LoadStep` provenance retained for diagnostics and the actuator-env dump; same shared link-time loader-descriptor substrate as R1 for cross-crate format loaders (candidate, not decision)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same kernel residency as R1 for the engine types; the cross-crate format-loader discovery is identical (shared link-time `linkme` descriptor slice = static 'classpath' + optional dynamic directory-scanned = WASM 'classpath', selection by `handles` DATA never link order, DCE/zero-contribution guardrails). The plan-then-apply split adds ONE cross-feature-friendly cross-crate property: because the precedence ladder is a `PrecedenceRung` enum + comparator rather than hard-coded insertion calls, OTHER features that contribute to the externalized-config order (profiles' `spring.profiles.active` resolution reading through the stack, auto-config's default-properties analogue, a test-support crate's `@TestPropertySource` analogue) can contribute their OWN rungs/contributions into the same plan via the shared descriptor substrate — making the precedence policy a UNIFIED, co-owned artifact rather than a config-data-private secret. The `ConfigFs` abstraction is also the seam where the dynamic/WASM 'second classpath' slots in: a WASM-provided loader that resolves `configtree:`-like trees from a sandboxed FS implements `ConfigFs`/`ConfigDataLoader` identically to a native one. The plan is computed at the final binary's env-seal point (the only place the full resolved loader set + the real FS are visible).
