This is a Spring IMPLEMENTATION limit, not a deep intent; leaf may choose to relax it (reuse config-data's loaders) but must then NOT accidentally grant multi-doc-activation semantics at this rung (that would smuggle a second, mis-phased activation engine in below Config Data).\n\nSCOPE BOUNDARY (what THIS feature owns vs neighbours): `property-sources` owns the ordered named stack + `add_first`/`add_last`/`add_before`/`add_after` vocabulary + first-source-wins + never-blend + name-as-identity; `config-data` owns the cold-bootstrap single-pass file LOADERS, the import-traversal engine, multi-doc activation, AND the ownership of the FULL externalized-config precedence-order policy (which rung everything lands at); `property-resolution` owns the `${...}` walk this feature reuses to expand the location; `relaxed-binding` owns key canonicalization; `origin-tracking` owns provenance; `configuration-classes`/`import-composition` own the config-class parsing phase this feature hooks into; `conditions`/`profiles` own the gating. THIS feature owns ONLY: the DECLARATIVE attachment surface (the `@PropertySource`-equivalent on a config item), the contract that it contributes DURING config-class parsing at REFRESH (the phase, distinct from cold Config Data), the PLACEMENT POLICY that the contributed source lands at the bottom-but-one rung (expressed as an ordering-mutation call delegated to `property-sources`, with the actual rung NUMBER deferred to config-data's precedence-policy ownership), the `${...}`-in-location + repeatable + idempotent-by-name semantics, the format-capability scope (whether YAML/multi-doc is allowed), and — leaf's value-add over Spring — the \"too-late bootstrap-key\" DETECTION + rich diagnostic. The genuinely divergent Rust axis: WHERE the declared location lives (a const descriptor emitted by an attribute macro and link-time-collected vs a programmatic registration on the config-class builder vs a runtime closure the config item returns) and WHEN it is loaded relative to the config-class parsing phase, given Rust has no refresh-time bytecode-annotation read.

### Candidate realizations

#### R1 — Const PropertySource descriptor on the config-class descriptor, collected with the config class, applied during the config-parse refresh step
A `#[property_source("classpath:app-defaults.properties")]` attribute (repeatable) sits ALONGSIDE the existing config-class attribute (`#[configuration]` / the `configuration-classes` declaration). The macro does NOT emit a new top-level registration — it folds the location into the config class's OWN descriptor as a `&'static [PropertySourceDecl]` field. A `PropertySourceDecl` is a thin const: `{ raw_location: &'static str, optional: bool, encoding: Option<&'static str>, ignore_resource_not_found: bool, format_hint: Option<Format> }`. Because it rides the config-class descriptor that `configuration-classes`/`import-composition` already link-time-collect, NO separate registry is introduced — the contribution is discovered exactly when (and gated exactly as) its enclosing config class is. During the refresh phase, after config-class CONDITIONS have been evaluated (so an excluded class contributes nothing), a `ConfigSourceContributionStep` walks the surviving config classes' `PropertySourceDecl`s IN PARSE ORDER: for each, it resolves `${...}` in `raw_location` against the already-built environment (reusing `property-resolution`), loads it (a `.properties` reader for parity, or — if leaf relaxes — delegating to a config-data single-doc loader), and calls `environment.property_sources().add_after(LOW_WATERMARK_ANCHOR, src)` so every contribution clusters at the bottom-but-one rung. The rung anchor name is OWNED by config-data's precedence policy; this feature only asks for "just above defaults, below everything else." Idempotency-by-name: a duplicate location keeps its first-discovery position. API feel: `#[configuration] #[property_source("classpath:db-defaults.properties")] #[property_source("${ext.dir:/etc/app}/overrides.properties", optional)] struct DbConfig;` and the programmatic escape hatch `ConfigClassBuilder::new().property_source(PropertySourceDecl::of("..."))`.

**Pros:** Maximal UNIFICATION (charter #11): introduces zero new link-time registry — reuses the config-class descriptor collection that `configuration-classes` already owns, so phase-gating (conditions/profiles exclude the class → no source) falls out for free and correctly.; Phase fidelity is structural, not bolted on: the contribution literally cannot be seen before config-class parsing, exactly mirroring Spring's 'tied to ConfigurationClassPostProcessor' timing and its 'too late' consequence.; Thin macro (charter #10): emits only a const array field on a struct the user could have written by hand; all loading/ordering logic lives in the normal `ConfigSourceContributionStep` crate.; Const descriptor is `linkme`-friendly and zero-cost; no life-before-main, compatible with async-first init since it carries data not live objects.; Declaration order among multiple sources is recoverable (they are a slice on one class), and cross-class order follows the config-parse order the container already defines — deterministic without relying on link/section order.

**Cons:** Couples this feature's discovery to the config-class descriptor SHAPE — if `configuration-classes` changes its descriptor, this rides along; needs a stable embedded sub-descriptor contract.; A `@PropertySource` on a config class that the binary never references (Layer-0 DCE) vanishes silently with the class — acceptable (same as the class itself disappearing) but must be covered by the same expected-presence self-check the config-class registry uses.; Const-only location string means a fully runtime-computed location (e.g. derived from another bean) is not expressible here — must fall back to the programmatic builder or a different realization.; Loading is forced to happen at one fixed refresh sub-step; a source whose location depends on a value only known LATER in refresh cannot be expressed.

**Mechanisms considered:** attribute-macro emitting a const sub-descriptor folded into the existing config-class descriptor (chosen sketch); linkme distributed-slice of standalone PropertySourceDecl keyed by owning-config-class TypeId (rejected: re-introduces a second registry + re-implements gating); build.rs codegen of the descriptor table (rejected: build.rs sees only its own crate, cannot aggregate config classes across crates)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No new cross-crate registry: the `PropertySourceDecl` slice rides the config class's existing link-time descriptor, so it is collected at the final binary exactly where `configuration-classes` collects config classes, and inherits that feature's Layer-0/DCE survival story (the enclosing class must be referenced; the same expected-count self-check applies). A `@PropertySource` declared in a sibling/downstream crate is discovered IFF that crate's config class is — correct Spring-like scoping, never implicitly global. The macro (in `leaf-macros`, proc-macro=true) emits only the const sub-descriptor via fully-qualified paths into `leaf-core`'s `PropertySourceDecl` type; the loader/ordering logic lives in a normal crate. Generics: a generic config class cannot be registered (no monomorphized descriptor) — the macro hard-errors with a clear diagnostic, same constraint as the config-class registry. `${...}` in the location is expanded at runtime, so cross-crate-authored locations referencing app-defined properties resolve against the unified environment, not at compile time.

**T2 — execution context:** Runs entirely on the synchronous refresh/bootstrap thread inside `ConfigSourceContributionStep`, AFTER config-class condition evaluation and BEFORE eager singleton instantiation — a COLD path, so synchronous file IO is acceptable per charter #4 (polling/blocking allowed on cold paths) but should still be fenced: the loader reads the resource via the `resource-loading` abstraction and must complete before the step returns (no detached work, no `.await` leaking into the sync stack). Errors propagate as a rich startup-validation failure: a missing non-`optional` location fails the whole refresh with the location, the resolved-after-placeholder path, the declaring config class, and candidate search locations (charter #9). The 'too-late bootstrap key' detection runs here too: if a contributed source defines a key in the reserved bootstrap namespace (logging/framework-main/profile-activation), the step emits a diagnostic naming the key, its origin, and why it has no effect — converting Spring's silent no-op into an actionable warning/error. Cancellation: bootstrap is all-or-nothing; if refresh is aborted, the partially-built stack is discarded with the context. No global lock — the step holds the single environment-mutate seam already serialized by the bootstrap phase.

#### R2 — Config item returns its sources programmatically via a trait method invoked at the parse step (closure/fn-based, runtime-computed locations)
Instead of a const attribute, the config-class declaration gains an OPTIONAL trait method (auto-implemented as empty by the derive, overridable): `fn declare_property_sources(env: &dyn PropertyResolver, c: &mut PropertySourceContributions)`. The derive for `#[configuration]` emits a default no-op impl; a user (or the macro, sugaring the `#[property_source(...)]` attribute) overrides it to push contributions: `c.add("classpath:db.properties").optional(); c.add(format!("{}/app.properties", env.get_property_or("ext.dir", "/etc")));`. The key divergence from R1: the location is computed at RUNTIME, with READ access to the environment-so-far, so a location can be derived from another property (not just `${...}` substituted). The `PropertySourceContributions` collector records ordered, named `(location, opts)` entries; `add_resource(name, impl PropertySource)` lets a config item contribute an already-built in-memory source (origin-agnostic — a WASM-module-provided source or a generated map slots in identically). During refresh, the same `ConfigSourceContributionStep` invokes `declare_property_sources` on each surviving config class in parse order, then loads/places exactly as R1. The trait method takes only a read-only `&dyn PropertyResolver` (least-privilege — it can read the env but cannot reshape ordering or touch the container), and a sink it can only push onto. API feel: macro sugar for the common const case, with the trait override as the documented escape hatch for computed locations and pre-built sources.

**Pros:** Expresses runtime-computed locations and pre-built in-memory/dynamic sources that R1's const-only path cannot — directly satisfies the origin-agnostic mandate (a WASM-module or programmatic source contributes through the SAME seam).; Still phase-correct: the method is invoked at the config-parse refresh step, gated by the same class conditions, so the 'too-late' and bottom-rung semantics are preserved.; Least-privilege seam (charter spirit + DX): the method gets a read-only resolver and a push-only sink — it can read config to compute a location but provably cannot mutate ordering or inject beans, keeping the contribution honest.; Charter #10 escape-hatch alignment: the attribute macro lowers to a hand-writable trait impl, so `cargo expand` shows exactly the override a user could have authored.; Naturally handles 'this source's location depends on an earlier property' without a second pass.

**Cons:** Heavier than a const descriptor: the contribution is a fn call, not statically inspectable — `config-metadata`/tooling cannot enumerate declared sources at build time (R1's const slice can).; Running user code at the parse step widens the blast radius — a panicking or blocking `declare_property_sources` jeopardizes refresh; needs clear 'cold-path, must-not-block-indefinitely, must-not-async' contract.; Order/determinism rests on parse order of classes + push order within the method; fine, but the method body is opaque, so a misordered push is a silent user error rather than a structural guarantee.; Tempts scope creep: giving the method the resolver invites reading half-built config and could leak the same mis-phased-activation hazard if a user tries to compute profile-gated locations here.

**Mechanisms considered:** trait method with default no-op impl, macro-sugared from the attribute (chosen sketch); FnOnce closures registered on a config-class builder (rejected as the primary: closures are not const, complicate the link-time descriptor, and obscure cargo expand); runtime registry of boxed `dyn PropertySourceProvider` (rejected: re-introduces a global registry and dyn-dispatch on a cold path with no benefit over the trait-method-on-class approach)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The trait impl rides the config class's existing registration (same as R1) — no new registry, same Layer-0/DCE story, same 'discovered iff the class is referenced/linked' scoping. The difference is the descriptor carries a FN POINTER (`declare_property_sources`) rather than (or in addition to) a const location slice; a `const`-constructible fn pointer is linkme-friendly and erases the config-class type to a uniform signature. Because the method runs at the binary's refresh, it sees the FINAL resolved environment of THIS binary — so a downstream-crate config class computing a location from an app property works correctly. Pre-built `add_resource` contributions make the origin-agnostic/WASM path first-class: a dynamically-loaded module can hand a `Box<dyn PropertySource>` to the sink through the identical seam, and the link-time vs dynamic-directory duality (static config-class registry as the default 'classpath' PLUS an optional dynamic source supplier) is expressible without changing the placement policy. Macro still hard-errors on generic config classes. build.rs is not involved.

**T2 — execution context:** `declare_property_sources` is invoked synchronously on the bootstrap thread at the config-parse refresh step (cold path) — it receives a `&dyn PropertyResolver` (read-only) and a `&mut PropertySourceContributions` sink, NOTHING else. Contract (enforced by the signature + documented): it must be a pure, fast, non-async, non-blocking-indefinitely computation that only decides WHAT to load (locations + pre-built sources); the actual file IO happens in the step AFTER the method returns, so blocking IO is centralized and fenced exactly as R1 (sync resource reads on the cold bootstrap thread, no `.await` into the sync stack). Errors: a panic in the method is caught and rendered as a startup failure naming the config class; a non-optional location that fails to load fails refresh with full context; the too-late-bootstrap-key detection runs on the resulting sources identically to R1. Cancellation/all-or-nothing semantics match the refresh template; no global lock (single serialized environment-mutate seam). The widened blast radius (user code at parse) is the cost paid for runtime-computed/dynamic sources.

#### R3 — Refresh-phase contribution as a thin re-use of the config-data loader pipeline at a fenced low rung (capability-relaxed, format-aware)
Rather than a bespoke `.properties`-only mini-loader, this realization treats a `@PropertySource`-equivalent declaration as a REQUEST submitted into the SAME format-loader pipeline `config-data` owns — but executed at the REFRESH phase and pinned at the bottom-but-one rung. The declaration (const attribute as in R1, or programmatic) carries `{ location, optional, format_hint }`. At the config-parse step, a `RefreshSourceLoader` resolves `${...}`, picks the loader from config-data's registered format loaders by extension/hint (so `.properties`, `.yaml`, even `configtree:`/`env:` prefixes COULD work if leaf opts in), loads to one or more in-memory sources WITH fine origins (file+line), and places them at the low rung. The CRITICAL discipline: this path deliberately DISABLES config-data's multi-document ACTIVATION and `spring.config.import` traversal — it loads a single document's worth of keys per location and refuses (with a diagnostic) any `spring.config.activate.*` / import directive found in such a file, because activation/import are cold-bootstrap, up-front-profile-decided concerns that must NOT be re-run at a lower rung in a later phase. So leaf RELAXES Spring's 'no YAML' implementation limit (YAML parses fine) while PRESERVING the deeper intent (no second mis-phased activation engine below Config Data). API feel: identical surface to R1/R2; the divergence is purely in the loader it dispatches to and the explicit capability fence. A leaf-native knob (`#[property_source("...", allow = properties)]` vs default) lets a user opt back into strict Spring parity (.properties only) for portability.

**Pros:** DX win over Spring: a user can put bottom-rung defaults in YAML (the format they already use for `application.yaml`) instead of being forced into `.properties` — removes a documented Spring papercut while keeping the precedence semantics.; Maximal loader UNIFICATION (charter #11): one format-loader registry serves both cold Config Data and refresh-phase contribution; no duplicate `.properties`/`.yaml` parsers; fine-origin tracking comes for free from the shared loaders.; The explicit activation/import FENCE makes the phase/precedence distinction LOUD and checkable — exactly the ordering-hazard the critic flagged — instead of relying on a format limitation as an accidental guardrail.; Origin-agnostic by construction: because it dispatches to config-data's loader set (which already includes `configtree:`/`env:`), dynamic/platform sources can slot in at this rung too, if leaf chooses.

**Cons:** Risk of BLURRING the very distinction the feature exists to preserve: reusing config-data's pipeline invites users (and maintainers) to assume full Config Data semantics (import, activation, last-wins profile groups) apply here — the fence must be airtight and well-diagnosed, or the two phases collapse into one confusing rung.; Larger surface area pulled into the refresh path: config-data's loaders may have been designed for the cold single-pass; invoking them at refresh demands they be phase-agnostic (pure location→sources), which constrains config-data's own design.; Relaxing 'no YAML' diverges from Spring parity — intentional, but means leaf's precedence docs/migration story must call out the difference; a strict-parity opt-in mitigates but adds a knob.; If config-data's loaders are async/IO-heavy, invoking them synchronously at the refresh step needs a clean sync entry point or a fenced block_on — an execution-context seam that must be designed deliberately (T2).

**Mechanisms considered:** dispatch the declared location into config-data's shared format-loader registry, fenced to single-doc + no-activation (chosen sketch); fully independent .properties-only loader for strict Spring parity (rejected as default: duplicates parsers, keeps the YAML papercut; kept as an opt-in mode); promote @PropertySource to a full config-data location with low priority (rejected: would grant import/activation at the wrong phase/rung — exactly the hazard to avoid)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Discovery/registration is identical to R1 (const descriptor on the config class) or R2 (trait method) — this realization is orthogonal to HOW the declaration is collected and concerns WHAT loader runs. The cross-crate consideration unique to R3 is a DEPENDENCY-DIRECTION one: the refresh-phase contributor (in `leaf-framework`) must reach config-data's format-loader registry. Per charter topology, config-data and this feature both live in/near the framework core, so the loader set must be exposed as a shared, origin-agnostic `FormatLoaderRegistry` that BOTH the cold config-data pass and this refresh pass consume — no cycle, dependencies point inward. If a downstream integration crate registers a new format loader (e.g. a TOML loader) via the link-time loader registry, it becomes available to BOTH phases automatically — a nice unification, but it means the set of formats this rung accepts is itself a function of the linked crates (must be documented; condition evaluation differs between `-p` and `--workspace` per the cross-crate doc). The activation/import FENCE is enforced in shared code so both phases agree on what 'single-doc, no-activation' means.

**T2 — execution context:** Runs on the synchronous bootstrap thread at the config-parse refresh step (cold path), same as R1/R2. The new execution-context concern: config-data's loaders may be authored async (the config-data design fences async file/network IO off from the synchronous stack). This feature needs a SYNCHRONOUS load entry point at the refresh step; the design must either (a) provide a sync facet on the format loaders for the local-file/classpath case (the common case here — bottom-rung defaults are almost always local), or (b) fence a bounded `block_on` for any genuinely-async loader, justified because this is a cold one-shot bootstrap step, never a hot path. Errors propagate as rich startup failures (location, resolved path, format chosen, declaring config class). The activation/import fence is an ERROR seam: encountering `spring.config.import`/`activate.*`-equivalent keys in a refresh-phase-loaded document raises a precise diagnostic ('this directive is only honored by the cold Config Data pass; it has no effect at the @PropertySource rung') rather than silently ignoring or, worse, half-applying it. The too-late-bootstrap-key detection runs identically. No global lock; single serialized environment-mutate seam; all-or-nothing with the refresh template.

### Rust clashes
- NO REFRESH-TIME ANNOTATION READ. Spring's `ConfigurationClassPostProcessor` reads `@PropertySource` off bytecode metadata DURING refresh — Rust has no runtime reflection and no classpath, so the location cannot be 'discovered while parsing config classes' at runtime. leaf must move discovery to COMPILE/LINK time (a const descriptor riding the config-class descriptor, or a fn pointer), then merely APPLY it at a refresh-phase step. The 'phase' becomes a runtime application step over link-time-collected data, not a runtime scan.
- THE 'TWO PHASES' DISTINCTION HAS NO NATURAL RUST BOUNDARY. In Spring, config-data (EnvironmentPostProcessor) and @PropertySource (ConfigurationClassPostProcessor) run at genuinely different JVM lifecycle moments. In leaf both are 'code that runs during bootstrap'; the phase separation must be DESIGNED and ENFORCED (an explicit ordering of bootstrap steps + an activation/import fence) rather than inherited from a framework lifecycle. Easy to accidentally collapse the two rungs into one.
- SILENT-FAILURE CULTURE CLASH. Spring's 'too late, silently no effect' for logging/main keys, and Spring's silent 'YAML not supported', both rely on quiet no-ops — directly contrary to charter #9 (rich, early, actionable diagnostics). leaf must replicate the PRECEDENCE FACT (these keys genuinely land too low to matter) while REPLACING the silence with detection + a clear message. This is a faithfulness-vs-charter tension that must be resolved by 'same semantics, louder diagnostics'.
- DCE / LAYER-0 LINKING. A `@PropertySource`-bearing config class in a crate the binary never references contributes nothing and emits no error — Rust's no-classpath reality. Acceptable only because it tracks the config class's own visibility, but it means the contribution shares the config-class registry's expected-presence self-check; it cannot be a standalone 'registration-only' source crate.
- CONST-LOCATION vs RUNTIME-COMPUTED LOCATION. Spring's location is a String literal with `${...}` placeholders resolved at refresh — partly static, partly dynamic. A pure const descriptor (R1) cannot express a location DERIVED from another bean/property beyond placeholder substitution; expressing that needs a fn/trait seam (R2), which trades static inspectability (tooling/metadata) for runtime flexibility. The placeholder-in-location case sits awkwardly between compile-time and runtime.
- FORMAT-CAPABILITY DECISION IS A FORK IN THE ROAD. Spring's 'no YAML' is an implementation accident that doubles as a guardrail. Rust has no such accident — leaf's YAML loader works fine at any rung. So leaf must make a DELIBERATE choice (relax to YAML for DX vs stay .properties-only for parity) and, if relaxing, must independently re-erect the guardrail the format limit used to provide (no multi-doc activation / no import at this rung) as an explicit fence.

### Interactions
- **config-data** — THE defining sibling — must be kept DISTINCT, not merged. config-data owns the cold-bootstrap single-pass loaders, import-traversal, multi-doc activation, AND the master precedence-order policy. This feature is the SEPARATE refresh-phase, config-class-driven contribution that lands at config-data's defined bottom-but-one rung. Interaction contract: this feature ASKS config-data's precedence policy for the 'just-above-defaults, below-all-Config-Data-files' rung anchor and places its source there; config-data must expose that rung as a stable named watermark. R3 additionally reuses config-data's format-loader registry but MUST disable config-data's activation/import traversal at this rung (the airtight fence).
- **property-sources** — Delegates ALL stack mechanics. The contributed source is a `PropertySource` (named, origin-agnostic) placed via `add_after(low_watermark, src)` / the ordering-mutation vocabulary this neighbour owns; first-source-wins + never-blend + null-never-overrides + name-as-identity (idempotent re-declaration keeps first position) all come from here unchanged. This feature owns only the DECISION to place at the low rung, not the stack itself.
- **configuration-classes / import-composition** — The discovery vehicle. The declaration rides the config-class descriptor these features own; the contribution is gated by the config class's conditions/profiles (excluded class → no source) and is applied at the config-parse refresh step these features define. R1/R2 fold the declaration INTO the config-class descriptor to avoid a second registry — tight coupling to that descriptor's shape.
- **property-resolution** — Reuses the `${...}` placeholder walk to expand the location string against the already-built environment BEFORE loading. Must use the same strict/lenient policy and cycle-detection; an unresolved placeholder in a required location is a rich failure (charter #9). Note the asymmetry: the location is resolved against the env that EXISTS at the refresh step, which by definition excludes this very source (it isn't loaded yet) and any higher rung is visible.
- **conditions / profiles** — Gating, inherited via the config class. Because the source is contributed only if its config class survives condition/profile evaluation, `@Profile` on the config class transitively gates the property source — but CRUCIALLY this is at the BEAN-gating phase using the already-decided active-profile set; the contributed source itself CANNOT influence profile activation (it lands too low and too late) — the 'too-late' gotcha. The fence must reject any profile-activation key found in a refresh-loaded source.
- **origin-tracking** — Each contributed value carries provenance (the named source as coarse origin; file+line as fine origin when a text loader is used — R3 gets this free from config-data's loaders). Powers the 'too-late bootstrap key' diagnostic ('logging.level.app set in db-defaults.properties line 4 has no effect because @PropertySource lands below Config Data').
- **resource-loading** — The location is resolved through the `resource-loading` abstraction (classpath:/file:/URL), so `@PropertySource("classpath:...")`-equivalents resolve identically to other resource reads; optional-vs-required and not-found semantics flow from here + this feature's `optional` flag.
- **value-injection / property-resolution consumers** — Downstream consumers (`@Value`, `@ConfigurationProperties`) read the contributed keys through the normal resolver — they neither know nor care the value came from a refresh-phase @PropertySource rather than a file; the contribution is transparent except for its (low) precedence. This is the payoff of delegating to property-sources.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- WHERE does the bootstrap-step ordering live that guarantees the config-parse contribution runs AFTER cold Config Data loading and AT the right (low) rung? This is the refresh-lifecycle / bootstrap-step-ordering meta-concept — the 'two phases at two rungs' separation is a cross-cutting bootstrap-sequencing decision, not ownable by this feature alone.
- Should leaf RELAX Spring's '.properties-only' limit to allow YAML at this rung (R3), and if so, is the activation/import FENCE strong enough to prevent smuggling a second mis-phased activation engine below Config Data? This trades a real DX win against the risk of collapsing the very phase/rung distinction the feature exists to preserve — a Phase 2/3 parity-vs-DX call.
- Const-descriptor (R1, statically inspectable, tooling/metadata-friendly) vs fn/trait seam (R2, runtime-computed/dynamic-source-capable): which becomes the DEFAULT, and is the other an escape hatch? This is an instance of the deferred metadata/codegen-boundary keystone — does the @PropertySource declaration need to be build-time-enumerable (for a 'what config does this app load' tooling rollup)?
- How is the 'too-late bootstrap key' RESERVED NAMESPACE defined and owned? Detecting that logging/framework-main/profile-activation keys arriving at this rung have no effect requires a canonical list of bootstrap-consumed key prefixes — a cross-cutting registry of 'early-consumed control keys' shared with config-data's early-control-key bootstrap (spring.config.name/location etc.).
- Does the contributed source participate in the origin-agnostic dynamic-source duality (a WASM-module or dynamically-loaded directory contributing at this rung, R2's add_resource)? If so, the link-time-static config-class registry ('classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic-directory source supplier must both be able to feed this rung — a cross-cutting discovery-substrate decision.
- If config-data's format loaders are async (per its own async-IO fencing), what is the SYNCHRONOUS entry point this refresh-phase step uses (R3 T2)? Whether to provide a sync facet on loaders or a bounded cold-path block_on is part of the broader async-execution-context meta-concept (T2), shared with config-data's own sync-stack-join problem.
- Is 'precedence rung' a first-class, named, stable concept the precedence-policy owner (config-data) exposes — so this feature can say 'place at the @PropertySource rung' declaratively rather than computing an index? The whole 15-rung Boot precedence order's representation (named watermarks vs integer priorities vs a sealed enum) is a cross-cutting config-environment decision.

### Notes
PHASE/RUNG IS THE WHOLE POINT. This feature's reason to exist (per the critic) is that it is a config-class-driven, REFRESH-phase, bottom-but-one contribution DISTINCT from the cold Config Data pass. Every realization must preserve that distinctness; the temptation (especially R3) is to merge it into config-data and lose the phase separation. The recommended framing for Phase 3: discovery is link-time (riding the config-class descriptor), but APPLICATION is a distinct, well-ordered refresh-phase step at a named low rung, with an airtight fence against activation/import.\n\nR1 (const descriptor on config-class) is the cleanest default — maximal unification, thin macro, phase-gating for free. R2 (trait-method seam) is the escape hatch for runtime-computed locations and origin-agnostic pre-built/dynamic sources; recommend R1 as default WITH R2's `add_resource`/programmatic path as the documented fallback (charter #10). R3 is orthogonal to R1/R2 (it is about WHICH loader runs, not how the declaration is collected) and should be evaluated as a capability CHOICE layered on whichever of R1/R2 wins.\n\nLEAF'S DELIBERATE DIVERGENCE FROM SPRING: (1) replace Spring's silent 'too late, no effect' with detection + a rich diagnostic (charter #9); (2) likely relax 'no YAML' for DX while re-erecting the activation/import guardrail explicitly (R3). Both are intent-parity-not-mechanism-parity moves and should be called out in Phase 3 docs + any migration guidance.\n\nKEY FILES READ: spring intent at /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md lines 1419-1443 (precedence order + the '@PropertySource is too late' hidden concept) and 924-932 (ConfigurationClassPostProcessor as the BDRPP that reads it); charter at /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md; cross-crate reality at /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (linkme-default, Layer-0/DCE, no build.rs aggregation, scope-not-global); nightly envelope at /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (const descriptors + linkme/inventory all stable; no nightly needed).

## spring.main.* self-binding + PropertySourceOrderingBeanFactoryPostProcessor refresh re-ordering  `[extra-11]`

Spring intent (docs §"Environment preparation order", lines 256-262, plus §prepareContext line 293 and the externalized-precedence §1415-1443). This feature owns TWO coupled bootstrap mechanics that the Configuration & Environment area otherwise leaves unowned because every neighbour silently assumes the property stack is sealed ONCE at a single defined point.

(1) SELF-BINDING FEEDBACK LOOP (`bindToSpringApplication`). During environment preparation — AFTER config-data loading has populated the stack, but BEFORE the context is created/refreshed — Boot binds the `spring.main.*` subtree (`banner-mode`, `web-application-type`, `lazy-initialization`, `allow-bean-definition-overriding`, `allow-circular-references`, `register-shutdown-hook`, `keep-alive`) BACK ONTO the live bootstrap object (`SpringApplication`). The load-bearing intent: externalized config must be able to OVERRIDE programmatic setters the developer called BEFORE `run()`. It is a genuine feedback edge — the object that is *configuring* the environment is then *reconfigured by* that same environment. These bound values then steer downstream bootstrap decisions (whether to print a banner, which context/env type to deduce, whether to add the lazy-init BFPP, whether to register the shutdown hook, whether to keep the process alive). The precedence rule that makes it work: because `spring.main.*` reads through the normal first-source-wins stack, a CLI arg or env var beats `application.yaml` beats a programmatic default — and the binding is the LAST write, so config wins over the pre-`run()` setter. Corollary foot-gun (docs line 1441): setting `spring.main.*` via `@PropertySource` is too late (added at refresh, lands at the bottom) and silently no-ops.

(2) LATE RE-ORDERING (the `PropertySourceOrderingBeanFactoryPostProcessor` analogue). Boot deliberately moves `DefaultPropertiesPropertySource` and `ApplicationInfoPropertySource` to the END (lowest precedence) during `prepareEnvironment`, AND registers a BFPP in `prepareContext` that RE-APPLIES that ordering again at refresh. So the stack's effective precedence is NOT sealed once: there is a SECOND ordering pass that runs at container refresh, after definitions are loaded and possibly after other BFPPs (e.g. `@PropertySource` contributors) have mutated the stack. This is a real determinant of effective precedence: anything that slipped in during context prep gets re-pinned so the default-properties and application-info sources stay at the bottom where they belong.

The intent leaf must replicate (NOT the JVM mechanism): a DEFINED, OBSERVABLE feedback edge from the resolved environment back onto the bootstrap object's effective settings (replacing programmatic defaults, in precedence order, with rich diagnostics on conflict), AND an explicit acknowledgement that property-source ordering has a SECOND deterministic re-pinning pass at a defined late point — owned by a named owner, not emergent — that keeps designated 'always-lowest' sources at the bottom regardless of what mutated the stack between the seal and refresh. leaf does NOT own: the stack data structure / ordering-mutation vocabulary / first-source-wins (`property-sources`), the read/placeholder walk (`property-resolution`), the binder tree-descent (`binder`), canonicalization of `leaf.main.*` keys (`relaxed-binding`), or the file-loading that populates the stack (`config-data`). It owns the bootstrap-settings binding ORCHESTRATION and the WHO/WHEN of the second ordering pass.

### Candidate realizations

#### Bootstrap-settings as a bound config-properties object with a single defined re-pin step (declarative seal)
Model the bootstrap object's `leaf.main.*` knobs as a strongly-typed `BootstrapSettings` record (banner_mode, web_application_type, lazy_initialization, allow_bean_definition_overriding, allow_circular_references, register_shutdown_hook, keep_alive). The fluent builder (`LeafApplication::builder().banner_mode(Off)...`) does NOT write final values — it records each programmatic call as a Layer in an ordered overlay: `Layer::Programmatic(field, value, call_site)`. At a single defined bootstrap step (the leaf analogue of `prepareEnvironment`, AFTER config-data has sealed the stack the first time), the binder (the `binder` neighbour, reused unchanged) binds the `leaf.main` prefix into a `BootstrapSettings` via the SAME tree-descent used for any `@ConfigurationProperties` type, producing `Layer::Resolved(field, value, origin)`. Effective value = highest-precedence layer per field, exactly mirroring first-source-wins; config beats programmatic because the resolved layer is added last and carries an origin. The result is a frozen `BootstrapSettings` consulted by every downstream bootstrap decision via a read-only handle.

For the second mechanic: precedence ordering is NOT a free-for-all. Each PropertySource carries an explicit `OrderPolicy` enum on the descriptor — `Pinned(PinTarget::Bottom)` / `Pinned(PinTarget::Top)` / `Floating`. The DefaultProperties and ApplicationInfo analogues are tagged `Pinned(Bottom)`. A single named function `repin(stack)` (the leaf analogue of the BFPP) is a pure deterministic stable-sort by pin policy that the bootstrap pipeline invokes at exactly TWO defined points: once at seal time, once again right before refresh eager-instantiation. `repin` is idempotent — re-running it on an already-ordered stack is a no-op — so the 'second pass' is safe and self-documenting rather than a hidden mutation. Pseudocode: `fn repin(stack: &mut PropertySources) { stack.stable_sort_by_key(|s| s.pin_rank()); }` where pin_rank maps Top→0, Floating→1, Bottom→2.

The two mechanics share machinery: both are 'apply an ordered, origin-carrying overlay at a defined late point,' and both expose their effective result + provenance for diagnostics (charter #9). `cargo expand` of the builder shows plain layer-pushes; nothing is macro-exclusive (charter #10).

**Pros:** Reuses the already-designed `binder` for `leaf.main` binding — zero bespoke parsing, maximal unification (charter #11); self-binding is 'just another @ConfigurationProperties target bound very early'.; Origin-carrying layers give a rich diagnostic for the classic confusion ('banner-mode came from CLI --leaf.main.banner-mode, overriding your .banner_mode(On) call at main.rs:14') — directly serves charter #9.; The `OrderPolicy`/`repin` model makes the SECOND ordering pass an explicit, named, idempotent, testable operation instead of an emergent surprise — it OWNS the unowned re-ordering the critic flagged.; All stable, all sync, no global state; the overlay is a stack-local Vec, no Arc/lock churn (charter #5).; Idempotent stable-sort repin means extra passes are provably harmless, so neighbours (config-data, @PropertySource analogue) can mutate the stack freely between passes without breaking the 'always-lowest' invariant.

**Cons:** Introduces a `BootstrapSettings` type coupled to the bootstrap crate; if a downstream wants to add a new `leaf.main.*` knob it must extend this type (vs an open string-keyed map) — though that is arguably correct (these are framework-owned knobs, not user-extensible).; The two-pass repin must be wired into the refresh-lifecycle template at the right step; getting that ordering wrong reintroduces the silent-precedence bug it set out to kill — needs a lifecycle test asserting pinned sources are at the bottom post-refresh.; Records call-site provenance for programmatic setters, which the thin builder must capture (e.g. `#[track_caller]` or a `Location`), adding a little machinery to the otherwise-trivial builder.

**Mechanisms considered:** Reuse the `binder` neighbour to bind `leaf.main` into a typed record (no new parser); Ordered overlay of Layer{source, value, origin} resolved by first-source-wins to mirror config-beats-programmatic; Explicit `OrderPolicy` pin tags on each PropertySource descriptor + a pure idempotent stable-sort `repin` invoked at two defined lifecycle points; `#[track_caller]`/`Location` to capture programmatic-setter call sites for diagnostics

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** `BootstrapSettings` and the `repin` ordering function live in the kernel/boot crate (`leaf-boot` per charter §6), referenced directly by the binary's `#[leaf::main]` — no cross-crate discovery needed for the SETTINGS themselves (they are framework-owned, not contributed by sibling crates). The `OrderPolicy` tag rides on the existing PropertySource descriptor that `property-sources` already defines, so no new registry. The one cross-crate subtlety: a sibling integration crate (e.g. `leaf-web`) may contribute a property source that should be `Pinned(Bottom)` (like ApplicationInfo); it does so by setting the pin policy on the descriptor it pushes — origin-agnostic, so a future WASM-module source can tag itself identically. No link-time collection is involved here; the bootstrap pipeline owns the stack imperatively in the binary, which is the ONLY place the full graph is visible (cross-crate doc, Layer 0). This sidesteps the DCE hazard entirely because nothing is link-registered.

**T2 — execution context:** Both mechanics run on the SYNCHRONOUS cold bootstrap path, single-threaded, before any async runtime work begins — this is exactly the 'cold path, polling/imperative OK' zone (charter §4). The self-binding bind() is a synchronous read over the already-loaded stack (config-data's async file IO has already completed and joined before the seal — see phase2 question on fencing). No `.await` inside repin or bind; no cancellation concern because it precedes the first cancellable async work. Errors (e.g. `leaf.main.banner-mode=banananana` failing conversion) propagate as a startup `BindValidationException`-equivalent that ABORTS the run with full origin context, consistent with fail-fast-at-boot (charter #9). The repin sort is O(n log n) over a handful of sources — negligible, no blocking.

#### Effects-pipeline: bootstrap settings + ordering as ordered EnvironmentPostProcessor-style steps over a mutable bootstrap state
Generalize BOTH mechanics into a single ordered pipeline of `BootstrapStep`s, the leaf analogue of Spring's `EnvironmentPostProcessor` chain plus the ordering BFPP. A `BootstrapStep` is `trait BootstrapStep { fn order(&self) -> i32; fn apply(&self, env: &mut Environment, app: &mut BootstrapState) -> Result<(), BootstrapError>; }`. Two framework-shipped steps realize this feature: `SelfBindStep` (reads `leaf.main.*` from `env`, writes resolved values into `BootstrapState`, recording a conflict report vs the programmatic defaults already in state) and `RepinStep` (re-applies the always-lowest ordering to `env`'s sources). Steps are collected (origin-agnostic registry — link-time slice OR explicit list, deferred) and run in `order()` sequence at the defined bootstrap phase. Crucially, BOTH the early seal AND the late refresh re-pin are just the `RepinStep` SCHEDULED TWICE in the pipeline at two order ranks — the 'second pass' is literally the same step instance re-invoked, making the re-ordering a first-class, observable pipeline entry rather than a hidden BFPP.

`BootstrapState` holds the live, mutable bootstrap knobs as `Cell`/owned fields; `SelfBindStep::apply` is the feedback edge made explicit — it takes `&mut BootstrapState`, the very object steps mutate, and overwrites programmatic values with config-resolved ones (last-writer-wins == config wins). The conflict report is emitted as a structured diagnostic. A downstream crate can contribute its OWN step (e.g. a web integration that wants to bind `leaf.main.web-application-type` more richly, or pin its own source) by registering a `BootstrapStep` — the same SPI Spring exposes via `EnvironmentPostProcessor`.

**Pros:** Unifies self-binding AND re-ordering AND config-data's own environment mutation under ONE ordered-step SPI — strong charter #11 unification, and matches Spring's actual mechanism (both are EPP/BFPP-shaped) at the intent level.; Open/closed: integration crates extend bootstrap by adding a step, never by editing the pipeline — the rust-native `EnvironmentPostProcessor`/`ApplicationContextInitializer` analogue; the 'second ordering pass' is just a re-scheduled step, fully owned and observable.; The `order()` rank makes the relative timing of self-bind vs re-pin vs config-import explicit and testable, killing the 'who runs when' ambiguity that hid the second pass.; Naturally absorbs the early-vs-refresh duality: the same RepinStep at two ranks documents that ordering is re-applied, no special-case code.

**Cons:** A step SPI invites a discovery mechanism (link-time slice / inventory) for downstream-contributed steps, which drags in the full DCE/force-link hazard (cross-crate doc Layers 0/A/B) for what is otherwise a tiny, mostly-framework-internal concern — risks over-engineering a 7-knob binding.; `&mut BootstrapState` shared across ordered steps is a mutable-shared-state seam; if steps run concurrently it needs discipline (must be strictly sequential — which it is on the cold path, but the type invites misuse).; Heavier than mechanic-1: a trait-object pipeline with ordering + a registry is more machinery than the two operations strictly need; weighs against low-overhead #2 (though it is cold-path).; Ordering across contributed steps is unspecified at link time (cross-crate doc), so `order()` must be a runtime topological/priority sort — correct, but means the pipeline can't rely on registration order.

**Mechanisms considered:** `BootstrapStep` trait-object pipeline (the EnvironmentPostProcessor + ordering-BFPP analogue) with i32 order ranks; Origin-agnostic step registry — link-time distributed-slice (default 'classpath') OR explicit builder list (escape hatch / wasm path); Same RepinStep instance scheduled at two order ranks to model the early-seal + late-refresh duality as one observable concept; `&mut BootstrapState` as the explicit feedback-edge surface SelfBindStep writes into; Runtime priority/topo sort of steps (never link/registration order, per cross-crate doc)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** This realization deliberately leans on a cross-crate step registry, so it engages the cross-crate keystone head-on: framework steps (SelfBind, Repin) ship in `leaf-boot` and are registered locally (no DCE risk), but DOWNSTREAM-contributed steps need the link-time substrate (distributed-slice default + explicit-list/codegen fallback for wasm) with the documented force-link + self-check discipline. Because steps register thin `const` descriptors (an order rank + a fn pointer), they fit the recommended descriptor shape (cross-crate doc 'Leaf implications'); ordering is resolved at runtime by `order()`, never by link order. The self-check should warn if an EXPECTED bootstrap step (e.g. the web crate's) contributed zero entries — the classic silently-GC'd-crate symptom. Keeping the descriptor origin-agnostic lets a WASM-module layer contribute a step later. NOTE this is heavier T1 surface than realization 1, which needs NO discovery for the core feature.

**T2 — execution context:** Runs on the synchronous cold bootstrap path; steps execute strictly sequentially in `order()` rank, so the `&mut BootstrapState` aliasing is sound without locks. No step may `.await` in this phase (config-data's async IO must have completed and joined before the step pipeline runs over the sealed stack — same fencing question as realization 1). A step that performs blocking work would violate execution-context discipline; the trait contract should document `apply` as sync-non-blocking, with any async/IO bootstrap work pushed to an earlier explicitly-async config-data phase. Errors propagate as a `BootstrapError` that aborts the run with the failing step named and full origin/conflict context (charter #9). Cancellation is not a concern pre-first-async-work; if a future async bootstrap phase is added it sits BEFORE this sync step pipeline, not interleaved.

#### Pure plan-then-apply: an immutable EffectivePlan computes both the bound settings and the final source order in one pass, no live mutation
Reject in-place mutation of a 'live bootstrap object' entirely (it is the JVM mechanism, not the intent). Instead, bootstrap produces an immutable `ProgrammaticConfig` (the builder's recorded calls) and a sealed `PropertySources` stack; a single pure function `resolve_bootstrap(programmatic, env) -> EffectivePlan` computes the answer in one shot. `EffectivePlan` holds: (a) the effective `BootstrapSettings` (per field: config-resolved value if `leaf.main.<field>` is present with its origin, else the programmatic value with its call-site, else the framework default — a three-way precedence fold mirroring first-source-wins exactly), and (b) the final, fully-ordered `PropertySources` with always-lowest sources already pinned at the bottom. There is NO second mutation pass and NO feedback edge onto a live object — the 'self-binding' is reframed as 'the plan is computed from BOTH the programmatic config AND the environment,' so there is nothing to bind BACK onto. The re-ordering is reframed as 'the final order is a pure function of the source set + each source's pin policy,' computed ONCE; if any later phase adds a source (the `@PropertySource`-at-refresh analogue), the plan is RECOMPUTED (cheap, pure, idempotent) rather than a stateful BFPP re-pinning in place. Recomputation determinism guarantees the always-lowest invariant holds after any addition without a dedicated 'second pass' concept.

The plan is the single source of truth every downstream bootstrap decision reads (immutable, shareable by `&` or one `Arc`); diagnostics render straight off it ('field X: effective=Off from leaf.main.banner-mode@cli; shadowed programmatic=On@main.rs:14; default=Console'). API feel: `let plan = EffectivePlan::resolve(&programmatic, &env);` then `if plan.settings.register_shutdown_hook { ... }`.

**Pros:** Most rust-native and aligned with charter #5 (immutability, no shared mutable bootstrap object, minimal Arc): the feedback loop becomes a pure fold, eliminating an entire class of order-of-mutation bugs.; No 'second ordering pass' to forget-to-wire: final order is a pure function recomputed on any source-set change, so the always-lowest invariant is structurally guaranteed, not maintained by a BFPP that must run at the right moment.; The three-way precedence fold (config > programmatic > default) is trivially testable in isolation and renders a complete diagnostic with all three candidates — strongest charter #9 story.; Trivial to reason about across phases: any actor can ask 'what's the effective plan given THIS source set?' deterministically; no hidden temporal coupling.

**Cons:** Recomputing the plan when a late source is added is clean in theory, but a naive design could recompute at many points; needs a defined rule for WHICH late additions trigger recompute (the `@PropertySource`-analogue, devtools) — otherwise it drifts back toward Spring's implicit multi-pass.; Some downstream consumers may have ALREADY read an earlier plan (e.g. banner printed before a late source arrives); an immutable plan that changes means stale reads — must define which settings are 'frozen at first read' vs 're-derivable', reintroducing temporal nuance the mutation models made explicit via the BFPP step.; Less directly maps to Spring's mental model (some users expect a live, inspectable 'application' object whose fields you can read back) — an intent-parity vs familiarity tension.; If a downstream crate needs to CONTRIBUTE a bootstrap knob, a pure fold over a fixed `ProgrammaticConfig` type is less extensible than the step pipeline of realization 2 (closer to realization 1's closed-type limitation).

**Mechanisms considered:** Pure `resolve_bootstrap` function producing an immutable `EffectivePlan` (no live mutable bootstrap object — rejects the JVM mechanism); Three-way precedence fold (config-with-origin > programmatic-with-call-site > framework-default) mirroring first-source-wins; Per-source `OrderPolicy` as input to a pure final-order computation, recomputed idempotently on source-set change rather than a stateful re-pin; Immutable plan shared by `&`/single `Arc`, diagnostics rendered directly off the plan's retained candidate list

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Lowest cross-crate surface of the three: `resolve_bootstrap`/`EffectivePlan` are pure functions/types in `leaf-boot`, invoked once in the binary where the full source set is visible (the only place it can be, per Layer 0). No registry, no link-time collection, no DCE hazard for the core feature — strongest portability incl. wasm/embedded (the cross-crate doc's universal-fallback property comes for free because there is nothing to discover). Per-source pin policy rides the existing origin-agnostic PropertySource descriptor, so native/file/env/WASM sources all declare their pin identically and the pure fold treats them uniformly. The only cross-crate question is how a downstream crate registers a NEW `leaf.main.*` knob into `ProgrammaticConfig`'s type — which this model does not natively support (a con); that extensibility, if needed, would borrow realization 2's step SPI, surfaced as a phase2 question.

**T2 — execution context:** Purely synchronous, cold-path, single invocation; a pure function has no execution-context hazards — nothing to block, nothing to cancel, no shared mutable state, no `.await`. The recompute-on-late-source-addition is likewise sync and pure. The only T2 nuance is the 'stale read' temporal issue (a consumer that read the plan before a late source arrived): this is an ordering/visibility concern resolved by defining a single late point after which the plan is final for all consumers (the leaf analogue of the refresh re-pin moment) — i.e. publish-once semantics, which is naturally safe. Errors (conversion/validation of a `leaf.main.*` value) surface as a `resolve_bootstrap` Result that aborts the run with the full three-candidate diagnostic before any async work begins.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's `bindToSpringApplication` MUTATES A LIVE, long-lived `SpringApplication` object in place — a feedback edge that relies on a GC'd, freely-aliasable mutable object. In Rust, a 'live bootstrap object you bind back onto' is an aliasing/borrow hazard; the idiomatic move is either a single owned mutation point on the cold path (realization 1/2) or rejecting the live-object framing for a pure plan (realization 3). The mechanism does not survive; the intent (config overrides pre-run() programmatic defaults) does.
- Spring re-applies ordering via a `BeanFactoryPostProcessor` that runs AGAIN at refresh — relying on the JVM's runtime BFPP auto-detection and a single mutable bean-factory the BFPP can reach into. leaf has no runtime BFPP auto-detection of this kind on the property stack; the 'second pass' must be an explicitly-wired lifecycle step (or, in realization 3, eliminated by making final order a pure idempotent function). The hidden temporal coupling Spring tolerates (mutate-then-mutate-again) clashes with Rust's preference for a single seal or a pure recompute.
- The whole AREA's neighbours (`environment` seal-boundary, `config-data` precedence-policy) implicitly assume the stack is SEALED ONCE. Spring's reality has a second re-ordering at refresh AND a feedback edge mutating the configurator. This is a cross-cutting ownership gap: in Rust we must NAME the owner and the exact lifecycle points of the second pass (charter wants explicit, not emergent), where Spring left it as an implicit BFPP nobody documents.
- `spring.main.*` keys flow through relaxed-binding (env `LEAF_MAIN_BANNERMODE` etc.) and bind via the binder — but they configure the BOOTSTRAP, which runs BEFORE the container the binder normally serves exists. There is a phase-ordering clash: the binder machinery must be usable on the cold bootstrap path over an environment that is only partially sealed, distinct from its normal early-refresh invocation.
- Spring's `EnvironmentPostProcessor`/ordering-BFPP are discovered via `spring.factories` (runtime classpath SPI). Rust has no classpath; if leaf wants downstream crates to contribute bootstrap steps (realization 2) it inherits the full link-time DCE/force-link hazard for what is mostly a framework-internal concern — a cost Spring never pays. Realizations 1 and 3 avoid this by keeping the settings type framework-owned and closed.

### Interactions
- **environment** — Environment owns the seal-boundary/customize seam; THIS feature is the actor that performs the SECOND, late re-pin at/after that seal and reads the sealed stack to self-bind. Environment must expose a defined late point (its mutate seam, ConfigurableEnvironment-analogue) where the re-ordering pass and the bootstrap-settings bind() may run, and must acknowledge that 'sealed' has a precise second-pass meaning rather than 'frozen once forever'. Tension: environment's single-seal assumption must widen to a seal-then-late-repin contract.
- **property-sources** — This feature consumes the ordering-mutation vocabulary (add_first/add_last/the pin operations) and the never-blend/first-source-wins primitive to express the always-lowest re-pin; it adds an `OrderPolicy`/pin tag concept on top of the descriptor property-sources already defines. The re-pin is implemented AS ordering-mutation calls, so property-sources owns the verbs and this feature owns the WHEN/WHO and the always-lowest policy. Must confirm pin policy rides on the existing origin-agnostic source descriptor.
- **config-data** — config-data owns the full externalized-precedence-order POLICY and the deterministic single-pass load that SEALS the stack the first time; THIS feature's late re-pin and self-bind run AFTER config-data's pass. The DefaultProperties/ApplicationInfo 'always-lowest' sources this feature pins are at the bottom of config-data's precedence ladder — so the two must agree on which sources are pinned-lowest and that config-data's seal is the FIRST of (at least) two ordering moments. Coupling: config-data populates `leaf.main.*` into the stack; this feature reads it back.
- **binder** — This feature REUSES binder's tree-descent (realizations 1/2) to bind the `leaf.main` prefix into a typed BootstrapSettings/BootstrapState — self-binding is 'a @ConfigurationProperties bind, but onto the bootstrap object and very early'. Binder must be invocable on the cold bootstrap path over a partially-sealed environment, before the container exists. Shares binder's BindResult three-state monad and BindHandler chain for the conflict/diagnostic reporting.
- **relaxed-binding** — The `leaf.main.*` keys must canonicalize through relaxed-binding so an operator can set `LEAF_MAIN_BANNER_MODE`/`--leaf.main.banner-mode` and have it bind onto the bootstrap object. This feature is a CONSUMER of relaxed-binding's canonical-form matching at the bootstrap altitude; no new mapping rules, but confirms the env four-candidate rule applies to bootstrap knobs too (e.g. `leaf.main.log-startup-info` → `LEAF_MAIN_LOGSTARTUPINFO`).
- **origin-tracking** — The self-binding conflict diagnostic ('config value from X shadowed your programmatic call at Y') reads the WINNING value's origin off origin-tracking for the config side, and a call-site Location for the programmatic side. This feature is a consumer that renders both provenance altitudes side-by-side; origin-tracking must carry the config origin through the bind into BootstrapSettings unmodified (never-blend short-circuit preserved).
- **refresh-lifecycle** — The late re-pin pass (the BFPP analogue) must be scheduled at a defined step in the refresh-lifecycle template — after definitions/late sources are loaded, before/at eager singleton instantiation. refresh-lifecycle owns WHEN steps run; this feature contributes one ordered step (or, realization-3, a recompute trigger). Tension: refresh-lifecycle's fixed-order template must include an explicit 'final property-order re-pin' slot so the second pass is not emergent.
- **lazy-initialization** — `leaf.main.lazy-initialization` is one of the self-bound knobs; its resolved value decides whether the global lazy-init pass/BFPP is added during bootstrap (Spring adds LazyInitializationBeanFactoryPostProcessor when true). So this feature's self-bind output is an INPUT to lazy-initialization's global toggle — the feedback edge directly steers a downstream container feature.
- **bean-naming** — `leaf.main.allow-bean-definition-overriding` (self-bound) controls whether same-name re-registration is a loud error or a silent override — bean-naming/bean-registry owns that collision policy, and this feature supplies the effective flag value (config-overridable) that configures it. Confirms the bootstrap-settings object is the source of truth for these container-policy toggles.
- **context-close** — `leaf.main.register-shutdown-hook` and `leaf.main.keep-alive` (self-bound) determine whether a shutdown hook is registered and whether the process is kept alive — both consumed by context-close/runtime-lifecycle. This feature's resolved settings gate those teardown behaviours; the feedback edge reaches lifecycle teardown, not just startup.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Async fencing of the cold bootstrap path: config-data does async file/network IO to populate the stack, but the self-bind + re-pin run synchronously over the sealed stack. WHERE exactly does the async config-data phase end and the synchronous bootstrap-resolution phase begin, and how is that boundary enforced (a join point? a typed phase transition?) so no step in this feature ever `.await`s over a half-loaded stack? (T2 keystone — touches the async execution & context-propagation model.)
- Single-seal vs seal-then-late-repin: the area's neighbours (environment, config-data) assume ONE seal. This feature proves there are at least TWO ordering moments. Phase 2 must decide the canonical model: (a) one seal + explicit idempotent re-pin step at refresh, (b) a pure recompute-on-change plan with publish-once final semantics, or (c) a mutable stack with a named BFPP-analogue. This choice ripples across environment's seal-boundary and config-data's precedence-policy and must be unified, not decided per-feature.
- Does the bootstrap-settings binding REUSE the @ConfigurationProperties/binder machinery, or is it a separate early micro-binder? Reuse maximizes charter #11 unification but forces the binder to run on the cold path over a partially-sealed environment before the container exists — a phase/altitude the binder was not otherwise designed to serve. Resolve whether binder is 'container-early' only or also 'bootstrap-early'.
- Extensibility of bootstrap knobs: should `leaf.main.*` be a CLOSED framework-owned type (realizations 1/3, predictable, no discovery) or an OPEN downstream-contributable step SPI (realization 2, the EnvironmentPostProcessor analogue, but drags in the link-time DCE/force-link keystone)? This is the discovery-duality (static link-time registry vs explicit list) applied to bootstrap — must align with the global cross-crate composition decision, not be solved locally.
- Stale-read / publish-once semantics: which bootstrap settings are frozen at first read vs re-derivable if a late source arrives (the @PropertySource-at-refresh analogue)? E.g. the banner may already be printed before a late source changes banner-mode. Phase 2 must define a single 'bootstrap settings final' point and which downstream consumers may read before it — a temporal-coupling question the mutation and pure-plan models answer differently.
- Conflict policy: when config overrides a programmatic setter, is that always silent-with-diagnostic (Spring's behaviour), or should some conflicts be hard errors (charter #9 fail-fast)? E.g. config saying web-application-type=none while the binary explicitly set Servlet — silently honour config, warn, or refuse? This is a DX-vs-safety policy that should be consistent with how the broader value-injection/binder lenient-vs-strict decision lands.

### Notes
Scope confirmed against docs lines 256-262, 293, 1415-1443. This feature sits in `leaf-boot` (charter §6), not `leaf-framework` — it is bootstrap mechanics, distinct from the pure environment/property neighbours it consumes. It is genuinely an UNOWNED gap: the catalog has `environment` (seal boundary), `config-data` (precedence policy, single-pass), and `property-sources` (ordering vocab), but NONE owns (a) the feedback edge from resolved config back onto bootstrap settings, nor (b) the second, late re-ordering pass — exactly what the critic flagged.\n\nThe self-binding knob set to replicate: banner-mode, web-application-type, lazy-initialization, allow-bean-definition-overriding, allow-circular-references (docs line 2192/2539 confirm Boot defaults this false), register-shutdown-hook, keep-alive. Each is also a DOWNSTREAM INPUT to another feature (lazy-initialization, bean-naming overriding policy, context-close shutdown/keep-alive), so the feedback edge fans OUT into several container features — the design must treat BootstrapSettings as a read-only source-of-truth those features consult.\n\nThe always-lowest pinned sources to replicate: the DefaultProperties analogue (leaf's setDefaultProperties equivalent) and an ApplicationInfo analogue. Both sit at the BOTTOM of config-data's precedence ladder (line 1419: defaultProperties is rung 1, lowest). The re-pin's job is to KEEP them there after any late stack mutation.\n\nKey foot-gun to preserve as a diagnostic (line 1441): setting `leaf.main.*` via the @PropertySource-analogue is too late and silently no-ops — leaf should turn this silent no-op into a rich warning per charter #9, since the second-pass timing is exactly what makes it too late.\n\nAll three realizations are stable-buildable (no nightly needed; confirmed against nightly-tradeoffs doc — binder reuse, const descriptors, plain sync functions). Realization 1 is the unification-forward middle; realization 2 is the most Spring-mechanism-faithful but heaviest on the cross-crate keystone; realization 3 is the most rust-native (pure, immutable) and lightest on T1 but reframes the 'live object' mental model. Phase 3 will likely merge 1's binder-reuse + pin-tag with 3's pure-fold resolution, reserving 2's step SPI only if downstream bootstrap extensibility is actually required.

## The ${...}-vs-#{SpEL} dispatch owner within a @Value string  `[extra-12]`

Spring's `@Value` string is a COMPOSITE micro-language: one string may interleave THREE segment kinds — `${placeholder}` (externalized property), `#{SpEL}` (runtime expression), and literal text — and they COMPOSE within the same string (`#{...}` may wrap a `${...}`; literal text surrounds both: `https://#{systemProperties['h']}:${port:8080}/`). Someone must own the GRAMMAR that splits one raw string into ordered segments, and the ORDERING law that Spring fixes: `${...}` placeholder expansion happens FIRST (over the property-source stack), and the RESULT is then fed to `#{...}` expression evaluation SECOND — so a placeholder can produce text that becomes part of an expression's input, but never the reverse. This dispatch/ordering seam is the orphaned hole: `value-injection` explicitly disclaims it ("does NOT own the `${...}` walk nor the `#{...}` engine"), `property-resolution` owns only the `${}` half and points at an expression-language neighbour, and that expression-language neighbour is NOT one of the 11 designs in the Configuration & Environment area. So WITHIN this area no design owns: (a) the lexical split of a `@Value`-class string into `${}` / `#{}` / literal segments (escaping `\$`/`\#`, nesting where a `#{}` body itself contains `${}`, the `:`-default living inside `${}` not the dispatcher), (b) the strict TWO-PHASE ordering contract (`${}`-then-`#{}`, non-recursive across the seam — once expression phase begins, its output is NOT re-scanned for `${}`), (c) the routing of each segment to the correct downstream engine, and (d) the policy/diagnostic seam for when one half is absent (no expression engine linked → a `#{}` segment is a hard, well-explained error, not a silent literal). This feature is a pure DISPATCHER/ORCHESTRATOR over two engines it does not implement; it is the grammar+ordering owner Spring leaves implicit inside `BeanExpressionResolver` + the embedded `StringValueResolver` chain. NOTE: this feature exists ONLY if leaf chooses a `#{}`-equivalent expression layer at all (a Phase-2/area-scope question — expression-language is a Cross-cutting Abstractions catalog entry, not an in-area design); if leaf has no expression engine, the dispatcher degenerates to 'placeholders + literal' and the `#{}` arm becomes a diagnostic-only stub. The genuinely divergent Rust axis: WHERE the split+order lives — a compile-time segmenter inside the `@Value` macro that lowers each string to a typed pipeline of resolver calls, vs a runtime two-pass string-resolver chain the container holds, vs a parse-to-segment-AST-once + interpret model — and HOW the absent-engine/strict-vs-lenient policy and origin/diagnostics ride the seam.

### Candidate realizations

#### Compile-time segmenter: the @Value macro lowers each literal into a typed resolver pipeline
The dispatch grammar lives ENTIRELY in `leaf-macros` at the `#[value("...")]` site, because the string literal is visible to the macro at expansion time (charter §10 thin-macros: emit only hand-writable code). The macro parses the literal ONCE at compile time into an ordered `Vec<Segment>` where `Segment ∈ {Literal(&'static str), Placeholder{key, default: Option<SegmentSeq>}, Expr(&'static str)}`, validates the grammar (balanced braces, escapes `\$`/`\#`, a `#{}` body may textually contain `${}` so the placeholder phase reaches inside it), and EMITS plain code: a sequence of calls against two object-safe runtime traits the kernel exposes — `PlaceholderResolver` (the `property-resolution` read seam) and `ExpressionEvaluator` (the optional expression layer). The two-phase ORDER is baked structurally into the emitted code: phase 1 expands every `Placeholder` (and recursively its default) into an owned `String`/`Cow`, splicing literals, producing an intermediate string; phase 2, IF any `Expr` segment exists, hands that intermediate to `ExpressionEvaluator::eval(intermediate_or_expr_body, ctx)`. Because the target field type is statically known, the macro also emits the final `TypeConverter::convert::<FieldTy>(...)` call — no runtime type metadata. cargo-expand shows a literal `resolver.resolve_placeholder("port", Some("8080"))` then `expr.eval(...)` then `parse::<u16>()`, all hand-writable. The dispatcher OWNS the segmenter (a normal, unit-testable function in `leaf-core`, called by the macro AND available at runtime for the dynamic escape hatch) and the emitted ordering skeleton; it does NOT own either engine.

**Pros:** Zero runtime parsing of the dispatch grammar on the hot path — the split is paid once at compile time; steady-state injection is just two trait calls + a convert (charter §2.2 low-overhead, §2.4 no hot-path polling).; Maximally thin/legible macro per charter §10: emitted code is exactly the two-engine pipeline a user could hand-write; the segmenter logic lives in a normal testable crate, the macro only calls it and emits.; Absent-engine policy becomes a COMPILE-TIME diagnostic: if the workspace links no `ExpressionEvaluator` and a string contains `#{}`, the macro can emit a call to a trait that is unimplemented, yielding a `#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]` 'this @Value uses #{...} but no expression engine is configured' error pointing at the exact span (charter §9 richest-context-as-early-as-legible).; Strict-vs-lenient and origin ride as explicit arguments in the emitted calls — the dispatcher carries policy as data it threads to the placeholder resolver, never hides it.; Statically-known field type lets the conversion target be emitted inline (the value-injection rust-native opportunity) with no boxing/`dyn` for the common scalar case.

**Cons:** Only works when the @Value string is a LITERAL visible to the macro; a computed/runtime-sourced dispatch string (rare, but Spring allows resolving arbitrary strings via `resolveEmbeddedValue`) needs the runtime escape-hatch segmenter — so the segmenter must exist in BOTH places, and the two must stay byte-for-byte equivalent (a unification risk the dispatcher must own via one shared `leaf-core` function).; Pushes grammar-version coupling into emitted code: if the segment-AST shape or the trait ABI changes, already-expanded downstream code hard-codes the old call shape (the cross-crate hard-coded-path hazard from cross-crate doc line 102) — the emitted surface must be an ultra-stable kernel ABI.; The expression engine's own grammar is opaque to the macro (it only knows the `#{}` body is a string); so deep expression errors still surface at runtime, splitting the diagnostic story across two phases.

**Mechanisms considered:** proc-macro literal parsing at the @Value site (primary); a shared `leaf-core` segmenter fn called by both macro and runtime; object-safe `PlaceholderResolver` + `ExpressionEvaluator` kernel traits as the two dispatch targets; `#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]` for the absent-engine compile error; const-promoted `&'static [Segment]` table embedded at the call site (const-eval baseline is stable per nightly doc)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The macro lives in `leaf-macros` (proc-macro=true, emits only via absolute paths `::leaf_core::...`) and CANNOT host the segmenter logic or the traits (cross-crate doc line 91); the segmenter fn + the two dispatch traits live in `leaf-core`, which both emitted code and the runtime depend on. NO link-time registration is needed for the dispatcher itself — each @Value site emits its own self-contained pipeline, so there is NO DCE hazard for the dispatch seam (unlike component discovery). The ONLY cross-crate contract is that the `ExpressionEvaluator`/`PlaceholderResolver` traits and the emitted call ABI stay stable across the workspace, since downstream expanded code hard-codes paths to them. The expression ENGINE itself (if it is auto-discovered as a pluggable provider) rides whatever registry the container uses; the dispatcher only needs a `dyn ExpressionEvaluator` handle from the container, origin-agnostic so a WASM-hosted evaluator slots in identically.

**T2 — execution context:** The compile-time segmentation runs in the proc-macro host process — pure, no IO, no async. At runtime, phase-1 placeholder expansion reads the property-source stack (sync, in-memory, never blocks — the stack is resolved before injection); phase-2 expression eval runs on whatever execution context the injection point runs on. Since injection happens during async bean wiring, the emitted pipeline is `async` where the `ExpressionEvaluator::eval` is async (an expression may touch other beans). Cancellation: the whole `@Value` resolution is a leaf await inside bean construction; if construction is cancelled the partially-built value is simply dropped (no shared state). Errors propagate as a typed `ValueResolveError` carrying the segment index, the originating span (compile-time captured via `proc_macro::Span`), the phase that failed, and the placeholder origin — never a panic.

#### Runtime two-pass StringValueResolver chain held by the container (Spring-faithful)
Mirror Spring's structure directly: the container holds an ordered `EmbeddedValueResolver` chain, and the dispatcher is a `CompositeValueResolver` runtime object that owns the grammar and the two-phase order. The `@Value` macro is MINIMAL — it emits only a runtime descriptor `ValueSpec { raw: &'static str, target: TypeId-or-converter-fn, origin_span, policy }` and a call `container.resolve_value::<FieldTy>(raw, policy)`. At runtime the dispatcher: (1) PASS ONE walks `raw` with a streaming char-scanner, recognizing `${...}` (delegating each to `PropertyResolver::resolve_placeholders`, which itself handles defaults/recursion/cycles) and copying literal/`#{}`-bytes through untouched, producing an intermediate `Cow<str>`; (2) PASS TWO re-scans the intermediate for `#{...}` segments ONLY, handing each body to the `BeanExpressionResolver`-analogue, splicing results; (3) hands the final string to `type-conversion`. The dispatcher is a single replaceable bean (Spring's `setBeanExpressionResolver` / `addEmbeddedValueResolver` seam) so the whole grammar+order is one swappable strategy. The two-pass non-recursion across the seam is enforced because pass two never re-enters pass one. Strict/lenient and origin are fields on the resolver, applied uniformly.

**Pros:** Faithful to Spring's mental model and seam (one replaceable resolver chain), so the intent-not-mechanism parity is high and the escape hatch (swap the resolver) is exactly Spring's.; Handles NON-literal dispatch strings uniformly — banner text, `@PropertySource` locations, any `resolveEmbeddedValue` caller — because the grammar is a runtime service, not tied to a macro site (one owner, no dual-implementation drift).; The dispatcher is a single bean: ordering between the two engines, the escape/escape-char policy, and the absent-engine behavior are all configured in ONE place, easy to reason about and to make consistent across every value-resolution caller.; Cleanly origin-agnostic: the resolver holds `dyn PropertyResolver` + `Option<dyn ExpressionEvaluator>`; a missing expression engine makes the `Option` `None`, and a `#{}` segment then produces a rich runtime error 'expression segment at col N but no expression engine configured'.
