**Cons:** Re-parses the dispatch grammar on EVERY resolution at runtime — for @Value this is once per bean field at startup (acceptable, cold path), but it forfeits the compile-time-known-type optimization unless paired with a cached compiled form; pure runtime parsing leans against charter §2.2 low-overhead for hot re-resolution (e.g. prototype-scoped beans re-resolving values per instance).; Type conversion target must be carried as runtime data (a converter fn-ptr or TypeId) since the macro emits a descriptor, not an inline `parse::<T>()` — slightly heavier than the compile-time pipeline and loses the fully-static scalar path.; A malformed dispatch string is a RUNTIME error (startup validation pass at best), not a compile error — weaker than option A on charter §9 'as early as the richest diagnostic allows', though still rich.; Putting the dispatcher behind a single `dyn` bean adds an Arc + dynamic dispatch per resolution (charter §5 minimize Arc) — justified only because resolution is a cold/startup path.

**Mechanisms considered:** a runtime `CompositeValueResolver` bean (Spring `EmbeddedValueResolver` analogue); streaming `Cow`-returning char-scanner for each pass; `Option<Arc<dyn ExpressionEvaluator>>` for the optionally-present engine; container-held replaceable strategy (the `setBeanExpressionResolver` seam); a per-spec compiled-form cache keyed by the `&'static str` raw to avoid re-parsing on re-resolution

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The dispatcher is a normal `leaf-core` bean; no proc-macro or link-time concern for the grammar itself. The expression engine is injected as an OPTIONAL dependency resolved through the container's normal registry — so whether a `#{}`-capable engine exists is a runtime fact (the `Option` is `Some` iff an `ExpressionEvaluator` bean was discovered/registered). This sidesteps DCE entirely for the dispatcher but inherits the registry's discovery story for the engine: if the expression engine is an auto-discovered provider in a sibling crate, it faces the cross-crate doc's link-time-registration DCE hazard, and the dispatcher must surface 'engine expected but registry empty' as a self-check (cross-crate doc line 152). The `ValueSpec` descriptor the macro emits hard-codes a path to the `container.resolve_value` API, so that API is the stable ABI surface.

**T2 — execution context:** Resolution runs as an async call into the container during bean wiring. Pass one is sync/in-memory over the stack; pass two awaits `ExpressionEvaluator::eval` (may touch other beans, so may await further resolution — the dispatcher must guard against re-entrant cycles via the same visited-set discipline property-resolution uses for `${}`). The single dispatcher bean is shared (`Arc`), but it holds no mutable state per call (the visited-set is per-call/stack-local), so no global lock. Cancellation of the wiring future drops the in-flight resolution; errors return a `ValueResolveError` with phase + column + origin, aggregated into the startup validation report.

#### Parse-once segment-AST + interpret: a compiled ValueTemplate shared by macro and runtime
A hybrid that makes the SEGMENT-AST the single owned artifact and splits compile-time vs runtime cleanly along it. The dispatcher defines one type, `ValueTemplate = &'static [Segment]` (Segment as in option A), plus an `interpret(template, &PlaceholderResolver, Option<&ExpressionEvaluator>, policy) -> Result<String, ValueResolveError>` function in `leaf-core` that encodes the two-phase order ONCE. The `@Value` macro parses the literal at compile time and emits a CONST-promoted `&'static [Segment]` (const-eval + const TypeId are stable per the nightly doc) plus a call `interpret(TEMPLATE, resolver, expr, POLICY)` then the inline typed convert. For non-literal/runtime strings, the SAME `interpret` is fed a `Vec<Segment>` produced by the runtime segmenter (the identical parser, shared). Thus: ONE grammar parser (used at compile time for literals, at runtime for dynamic strings), ONE ordering interpreter (always runtime, but trivial — it walks a pre-split AST, no re-lexing), and the macro contributes only the const AST + the inline convert. The two-phase law is a property of `interpret` alone: it iterates segments resolving `${}` first into a scratch buffer, then if the template's `has_expr` flag is set, evaluates. The AST also records per-segment the source column so errors point precisely; for literal-origin templates the macro additionally stamps the `proc_macro::Span`.

**Pros:** Best-of-both: compile-time grammar validation + const AST for the common literal case (cheap, early diagnostics), but a SINGLE ordering interpreter and a SINGLE parser shared with the runtime path — eliminates option A's dual-implementation drift (direct charter §11 unification win).; The ordering law lives in exactly one function (`interpret`), so the `${}`-then-`#{}` contract and the non-recursion-across-the-seam rule are defined, tested, and reasoned about in one place regardless of how the AST was produced.; Macro stays thin: it emits a const data structure + one fn call + one convert — all hand-writable; the heavy logic (parser, interpreter) is in normal `leaf-core` crates, cargo-expand legible (charter §10).; Const AST means no runtime re-parsing on re-resolution (fixes option B's re-parse cost) while still supporting dynamic strings via the same code (fixes option A's literal-only limit).; Absent-engine policy: `interpret` takes `Option<&ExpressionEvaluator>`; a `None` + a template whose `has_expr` is true yields one canonical rich error; for literal templates the macro can ALSO emit a compile-time `#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]` guard, giving the earliest legible diagnostic where possible and a uniform runtime one otherwise.

**Cons:** The interpreter still runs at runtime even for fully-static templates (the AST is const but the walk is not const-folded), so it is marginally heavier than option A's fully-inlined straight-line pipeline — a small price for the unification.; Defining a `Segment` type that is BOTH const-promotable (`&'static [Segment]` for the macro path) AND constructible at runtime (`Vec<Segment>`) constrains the type (it must hold `&'static str` for the const path; runtime-produced dynamic strings need an owned/`Cow` variant) — a mild representational tax the dispatcher must absorb (likely a `Cow<'static, str>`-carrying segment or two parallel constructors).; Two slightly different Segment lifetimes (static vs owned) may leak into the `interpret` signature (generic over the str-storage), adding a type parameter the kernel ABI must commit to.

**Mechanisms considered:** const-promoted `&'static [Segment]` AST emitted by the macro (const-eval stable); one shared `interpret` ordering function in leaf-core; one shared grammar parser used at both compile and runtime; `Cow<'static, str>`-carrying Segment to unify static-literal and runtime-dynamic storage; `proc_macro::Span` stamping for literal templates + `#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]` absent-engine guard; `has_expr` flag on the template to skip phase two entirely when no `#{}` exists

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same split as A/B: `leaf-macros` emits the const AST via absolute paths and a call to `leaf-core::interpret`; `leaf-core` hosts `Segment`, the parser, the interpreter, and the two engine traits. The const AST is embedded at each call site (no registration, no DCE hazard for the dispatcher). The kernel ABI to keep ultra-stable is `Segment`'s shape + `interpret`'s signature + the two traits — narrower than option B because the macro emits DATA (a const) rather than a call into a wider container API. The expression engine's discovery (if pluggable) inherits the registry's link-time story exactly as in B, but the dispatcher's coupling to it is only `Option<&dyn ExpressionEvaluator>`, origin-agnostic (native or WASM evaluator both satisfy it).

**T2 — execution context:** `interpret` is the only runtime piece and is itself sync over the placeholder phase; the expression phase is the single await point (the trait method is async to allow bean-touching expressions). It holds no shared state — the scratch buffer and visited-set are stack-local per call — so no lock, no Arc churn beyond the borrowed engine handle. Re-entrancy (an expression resolving a value that contains another expression) is bounded by a depth/visited guard inside `interpret`, sharing the cycle-detection discipline with property-resolution's `${}` recursion. Cancellation drops the in-flight call cleanly; the returned `ValueResolveError` carries phase, segment column, captured span (when literal), and the winning placeholder's origin for charter §9 diagnostics, feeding the same startup validation aggregation as the other config features.

### Rust clashes
- Spring's `${}`-then-`#{}` ordering is enforced implicitly by running two distinct resolver objects in sequence inside `AbstractBeanFactory.resolveEmbeddedValue` → `BeanExpressionResolver`; there is no explicit 'dispatcher' class because the JVM glues them at runtime via the bean post-processor pipeline. In Rust there is no implicit runtime glue layer — the ordering and the split must be made an EXPLICIT, named artifact (a segmenter + an interpret order), which is actually a parity improvement but forces leaf to NAME and OWN a seam Spring left anonymous.
- Spring's lenient default (an unresolved `${catalog.name}` injected as the literal string) is a silent foot-gun the docs themselves flag; Rust's culture + charter §9 push toward strict-by-default with a rich diagnostic. The dispatcher must decide whether the SEAM defaults strict — but the placeholder-half strictness is owned by `property-resolution`, so the dispatcher only owns whether an ABSENT expression engine (a `#{}` with no evaluator) is lenient-literal or hard-error. Charter §9 argues hard-error-with-context; Spring would silently pass the `#{}` through as text. This is a deliberate divergence.
- Spring evaluates `#{SpEL}` against a rich runtime `EvaluationContext` (bean references, `systemProperties`, method calls) that assumes reflection and a GC'd object graph. Rust has no runtime reflection; an `ExpressionEvaluator` cannot freely reflect over arbitrary beans. This is WHY expression-language is a Cross-cutting Abstractions catalog entry and NOT designed in this area — and why the dispatcher must treat the `#{}` engine as a fully opaque, optional, possibly-WASM-hosted provider it only hands a string and a context to, never introspecting it.
- Spring resolves the dispatch string at runtime regardless of where the value is bound; Rust's killer advantage is that for `@Value` the string is a LITERAL the macro sees AND the target type is statically known — so the most rust-native realization (option A/C) moves the split to compile time, which has no Spring analogue and creates a literal-vs-dynamic-string duality (compile-time-known vs `resolveEmbeddedValue`-style runtime strings) that Spring never has to distinguish.
- The escape semantics (`\$`, `\#`) and the nesting rule (a `#{}` body may itself contain `${}` which is expanded in phase one) are under-specified in Spring's prose and emerge from the interplay of two independent resolvers; making them precise in a single Rust grammar forces decisions Spring never wrote down (e.g. is `${a:#{b}}` — an expression inside a placeholder default — legal? Spring's answer falls out of resolver order; leaf must state it).
- Whether this feature EXISTS at all is contingent: if leaf's area scope excludes a `#{}`-equivalent (because expression-language is a separate Cross-cutting catalog area), the dispatcher collapses to a placeholder+literal splitter and the entire `#{}` arm is a diagnostic stub — Spring has no equivalent 'maybe the second engine isn't there' state because SpEL is always present in the framework core.

### Interactions
- **value-injection** — PARENT/CONSUMER. value-injection owns the injection-point seam and explicitly disclaims the `${}`/`#{}` split; this feature IS the dispatcher value-injection delegates to. value-injection calls the dispatcher with (raw string, statically-known target type, strict/lenient policy) and receives a resolved-and-typed value. The dispatcher returns the pre-conversion string (or the macro emits the convert inline); value-injection lands it in the constructor arg/field. Clean ownership: value-injection = WHERE/WHEN at the injection point; this feature = the in-string grammar+order.
- **property-resolution** — DOWNSTREAM ENGINE (phase one). The dispatcher routes every `${}` segment to property-resolution's `resolve_placeholders`/`resolve_required_placeholders`. property-resolution owns the `${}` grammar INTERNALS (defaults `:`, recursion, cycle detection, partial-text, strict/lenient of the placeholder half); the dispatcher owns only the OUTER split that decides a substring is a `${}` segment and hands it over. The `:`-default lives inside property-resolution, NOT the dispatcher — the dispatcher must not try to parse `${a:b}` itself, only delimit it.
- **expression-language** — DOWNSTREAM ENGINE (phase two) — the ORPHANED neighbour. The dispatcher routes every `#{}` body to an `ExpressionEvaluator` provider. This is the seam that motivated the feature: expression-language is not an in-area design, so the dispatcher must treat it as an OPTIONAL, origin-agnostic `dyn ExpressionEvaluator` (native or WASM), present-or-absent at runtime, and own the absent-engine diagnostic. The dispatcher defines the trait shape it needs (eval(body, ctx) -> string) but NOT the expression grammar/semantics.
- **type-conversion** — DOWNSTREAM (final step). After both phases produce a string, it is coerced to the statically-known target type via type-conversion. In options A/C the macro emits the convert inline (target type known at compile time); in option B it is carried as a converter fn-ptr. The dispatcher owns the ORDER (convert is strictly last, after both placeholder and expression phases) but not the coercion logic.
- **origin-tracking** — PROVENANCE PASS-THROUGH. When phase one resolves a `${}` to a value, that value carries an Origin (which source/file+line). The dispatcher must thread that origin into any error it raises (e.g. 'expression phase failed; its `${host}` input came from application.yaml line 12') and not synthesize a merged origin — honoring never-blend. For literal segments the origin is the @Value declaration site (captured via `proc_macro::Span` in options A/C).
- **relaxed-binding** — INDIRECT (via property-resolution). A `${demo.item-price}` segment must be looked up in canonical kebab form; the asymmetric-placeholder gotcha (canonical-form-only relaxed lookup) is owned by relaxed-binding + property-resolution. The dispatcher passes the placeholder key through unchanged and must NOT canonicalize it itself — it only delimits the segment; canonicalization happens downstream in the placeholder resolver.
- **config-properties** — CONTRAST/BOUNDARY. config-properties is deliberately INERT (no `#{}` evaluation) — so it does NOT use this dispatcher's expression arm. This feature must preserve the philosophical split: the dispatcher (with its `#{}` arm) is the `@Value` path; config-properties binding never invokes phase two. The dispatcher's existence sharpens, rather than blurs, the '@Value is code-ish / config-properties is inert data' line.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Does leaf adopt a `#{}`-equivalent expression layer at all? expression-language is a Cross-cutting Abstractions catalog entry, NOT an in-area design — so the dispatcher's phase-two arm may be a permanent diagnostic stub. Phase 2 must decide whether expression-language is realized, and if so whether it is core or an optional auto-discovered provider (which determines whether the dispatcher's `ExpressionEvaluator` is always-present or `Option`).
- WHERE does the split live — the compile-vs-runtime resolution boundary (charter §3 deferred keystone) directly governs whether the dispatcher is a compile-time segmenter (option A), a runtime resolver bean (option B), or the hybrid const-AST + shared-interpreter (option C). This cannot be decided in-area; it rides the metadata/codegen-boundary and compile-vs-runtime meta-decisions.
- Should the SEAM default to strict (a `#{}` with no engine, or an unresolved `${}`, is a hard error) given charter §9, deliberately diverging from Spring's lenient default? The placeholder-half strictness is property-resolution's; but the cross-engine absent-engine policy is THIS dispatcher's, and it must be reconciled with whatever unified strict/lenient policy Phase 2 sets for the whole config read path.
- Is the segmenter's grammar (escapes, nesting of `${}` inside `#{}` bodies and inside `${}` defaults, whether `${a:#{b}}` is legal) part of a UNIFIED leaf micro-string-template grammar shared with banner text, `@PropertySource` locations, scheduling cron strings, and event `condition` filters — i.e. is there ONE embedded-value grammar across the framework (charter §11 unification), or per-site grammars?
- Does the dispatcher's `ExpressionEvaluator` trait shape (and its EvaluationContext) get unified with the event-listener `condition` SpEL filter, `@ConditionalOnExpression`, cache-key SpEL, and bean-reference SpEL — all of which the catalog lists as expression-language consumers? If so, the dispatcher is just ONE caller of a shared evaluator and must not over-specify the context shape; that unification is a Phase 2/3 cross-area concern.
- How does an async `ExpressionEvaluator::eval` (an expression may touch async beans) interact with the execution-context discipline (charter §2.3) — what context does expression evaluation run on during bean wiring, what may it block, and how is re-entrant value resolution (an expression whose result needs another `${}`) bounded? This ties to the deferred async execution & context-propagation model.

### Notes
Grounding read: charter (all 11 invariants), Spring doc §`@Value` injection (lines 798-804, the lenient foot-gun) + §Read-vs-mutate / PropertySource first-source-wins (1281-1316) + SpEL references (lines 800, 1146, 1818-1820 for the event `condition` SpEL filter — a sibling expression-engine caller); cross-crate doc §global-classpath-gap + link-time-registration layers (lines 89-154, esp. the macro-emits-thin-code-via-absolute-paths constraint at 91/102, the DCE silent-failure at 152, and 'generics cannot be registered' at 153); nightly doc (const-eval + const TypeId stable, `#[diagnostic::on_unimplemented]` stable, inventory/linkme available, NO nightly needed). KEY FRAMING for Phase 2/3: the prompt is precise that this is a HOLE relative to the Configuration & Environment area's scope, not merely a deferred keystone — the placeholder half (property-resolution) lives in-area but the dispatch seam to `#{}` points at expression-language which is OUT of area. So the dispatcher's most defensible in-area scope is: own the OUTER split grammar + the `${}`-then-`#{}` ORDER + the routing + the absent-engine policy, while treating BOTH engines as downstream (one in-area: property-resolution; one out-of-area/optional: expression-language). All three realizations keep the expression engine as a pluggable, origin-agnostic `dyn` provider so a WASM-hosted evaluator can slot in later (honoring the origin-agnostic-provider guardrail). The discovery-duality guardrail (static link-time registry default 'classpath' + optional dynamic WASM-module 'classpath') applies ONLY to how the expression-engine PROVIDER is discovered, NOT to the dispatcher itself — the dispatcher has no discovery surface; each @Value site emits its own self-contained pipeline, so the dispatcher is DCE-immune by construction (a deliberate contrast with component-scanning's link-time hazard). I did NOT resolve: whether expression-language exists, the compile-vs-runtime boundary, the unified-grammar question, or strict-default — all surfaced as phase2Questions per the implementation-agnostic guardrail.

## Placeholder escaping and the configurable value-separator  `[extra-13]`

This feature is the MISSING fifth sub-behaviour of the `${...}` placeholder engine that the existing `property-resolution` design enumerated only four of (defaults, recursion+cycle-detection, partial/literal text, strict-vs-lenient) — it owns the GRAMMAR's escape mechanism and the configurability of the grammar's delimiters. Spring's `PropertyPlaceholderHelper` (rewritten in Framework 6.2 for a cleaner, lenient parser) supports: (1) ESCAPING A LITERAL `${` — a configurable escape character (default `\`, settable via `spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default`, and explicitly DISABLE-able by setting it to none) so `\${test}` yields the literal text `${test}` and is NOT treated as a placeholder; (2) ESCAPING THE VALUE-SEPARATOR inside a key/default so a value can legitimately contain a `:` — `${sub\://host}` treats the first `:` as literal, not as the default-splitter, yielding key `sub://host` (or a default beginning with `:`); (3) a CONFIGURABLE PLACEHOLDER PREFIX/SUFFIX — `${...}` is the default but it can be reconfigured to e.g. `%{...}` (Spring does this by declaring a differently-configured `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer`); (4) a CONFIGURABLE VALUE-SEPARATOR — the `:` in `${key:default}` is the default but is itself reconfigurable. WHY IT MATTERS (correctness, not Phase-2 polish): without an escape mechanism, any config value that legitimately contains a literal `${` (URL templates, regexes, shell snippets, money formats, JSON fragments) or a `:` (URLs like `sub://host`, Windows paths `C:\...`, time formats `HH:mm:ss`, IPv6 literals) is MIS-PARSED — silently expanded, silently cycle-detected, or silently default-split. The escape char and the delimiters are part of the placeholder GRAMMAR DEFINITION; `property-resolution` owns the walk/strict-lenient/recursion but its design omitted the grammar's own escape + delimiter-configurability. SCOPE BOUNDARY: `property-resolution` owns the placeholder WALK (defaults, recursion, cycle-detection, partial-text, strict/lenient mode, the read interface); `relaxed-binding` owns canonicalizing a placeholder KEY before lookup; `property-sources` owns first-source-wins/never-blend; `config-data` owns file-format parsing (where doubled-backslash `\\` source-level escaping interacts). THIS feature owns ONLY: the escape-character policy (what it is, that it is configurable and disable-able), the two escape SITES (literal-`${` escape + separator escape), the de-escaping rule (a recognized escape is consumed and the following char emitted literally; an escape before a non-special char is preserved verbatim), and the configurability of prefix/suffix/separator as a GRAMMAR CONFIG object carried into the walk. It does NOT re-decide the walk algorithm — it parameterizes it. The genuinely divergent Rust axis: WHERE the escape/delimiter config lives and HOW de-escaping threads through the scanner (a config-struct passed into a hand-written char-scanner that handles escape inline and returns Cow; vs a two-stage tokenizer that lexes escapes/delimiters into a token stream the existing recursive expander consumes; vs a grammar/parser-combinator that bakes the configurable delimiters into a compiled matcher), and HOW the per-resolver-instance grammar config is represented without a global mutable knob.

### Candidate realizations

#### Inline escape-aware char-scanner driven by a borrowed PlaceholderSyntax config (Cow-returning, zero-alloc fast path)
Extend property-resolution's char-scanner expander so the scan loop is parameterized by a small value-type `PlaceholderSyntax { prefix: &'static str (default "${"), suffix: &'static str (default "}"), value_separator: Option<&'static str> (default Some(":")), escape: Option<char> (default Some('\\')) }`. The resolver holds ONE `PlaceholderSyntax` (built once at environment-seal time), borrowed read-only on every resolve — no global, no per-call alloc. The scanner walks chars left-to-right; at each position it asks the syntax: is this the escape char? If so and the NEXT chars are the prefix OR (inside a placeholder body) the separator OR the escape char itself, consume the escape and emit the escaped sequence LITERALLY (the `${` is not opened; the `:` is not treated as a separator). An escape before any OTHER char is emitted verbatim (escape + char) — Spring's rule: the escape only 'bites' on the special tokens. Matching the prefix/suffix/separator is a substring compare against the configured strings (supports multi-char delimiters like `%{`). Output is `Cow<str>`: if no placeholder and no escape fired, return `Cow::Borrowed` (the charter low-overhead win on the common literal-config path); only allocate when expansion or de-escaping actually occurs. The separator split inside a body becomes 'find first UNESCAPED separator', and cycle-detection/default/recursion (owned by property-resolution) operate on the already-de-escaped key/default. API feel: `resolver.with_placeholder_syntax(PlaceholderSyntax::default().escape(None))` to disable escaping; the default resolver ships `PlaceholderSyntax::default()`.

**Pros:** Smallest, most surgical change — escaping/delimiters become PARAMETERS of the existing scanner rather than a new subsystem; honors charter §11 (share the existing walk machinery, no bespoke engine); Zero-alloc Cow fast path: literal config strings and the no-escape case borrow, matching low-overhead #2 and the existing property-resolution 'lazy/streaming Cow-returning resolver' axis; De-escape is fused with the scan in one pass — no separate tokenization buffer, minimal heap, stack-friendly (charter §5); Disable-able escape (escape: None) and configurable separator (value_separator: None disables default-splitting entirely) fall out naturally as config variants — directly mirrors Spring's spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default disable and the no-default mode; Multi-char prefix/suffix (%{...}, <<...>>) supported by substring compare with no grammar recompile

**Cons:** Hand-rolled escape logic interleaved with the expansion loop is the classic off-by-one hazard zone (escape-at-end-of-string, escape-before-prefix vs escape-before-escape, separator-inside-default); demands exhaustive property tests; Decision of WHERE the de-escape boundary sits relative to recursion is subtle: must de-escape the LITERAL `\${` BEFORE deciding it's not a placeholder, but must NOT de-escape inside a resolved value that itself came from a source (re-scanning a resolved value re-applies escape rules — Spring does, so this must too) — the recursion contract has to state this explicitly; `&'static str` delimiters force compile-time-known syntax; a fully runtime-chosen prefix (from a config key) needs `Cow<'static, str>` in the syntax struct, slightly heavier; Borrowed-config lifetime threads through the whole recursive walk signature — ergonomic friction if the walk is already generic over mode/handler

**Mechanisms considered:** Hand-written char-scanner (chosen core) with the syntax as a borrowed value-type param; Cow<str> return to preserve the existing zero-alloc lenient-passthrough behaviour; const-default PlaceholderSyntax built once at environment-seal, never a global mutable knob; no proc-macro / no link-time registration needed — this is a pure runtime parameterization of a value-resolution function

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No cross-crate registry involvement: `PlaceholderSyntax` is a plain value type in the kernel config crate (alongside the resolver), constructed by the bootstrap/`environment` seal seam, never discovered. A downstream crate that wants a non-default syntax (e.g. `%{...}`) constructs its own `PlaceholderSyntax` and installs it on its resolver instance via the public builder — fully hand-writable, no macro, no linker section, so the charter's DCE/reachability hazards are entirely sidestepped. The only cross-crate subtlety is the SOURCE-LEVEL escaping that `config-data` loaders apply: a `.properties`/`.yaml` parser may itself consume one backslash (Spring's 'doubled backslash' rule, `${sub\://host}` in a file), so the syntax's `escape` char is applied to the string AFTER file-parsing has already done its own unescape — this feature must document that the placeholder escape operates on the post-file-parse string, and that contract is shared with `config-data` across the crate boundary (a documented invariant, not a registered artifact).

**T2 — execution context:** Pure synchronous CPU-bound string scan; runs wherever `property-resolution` runs (eager at injection for `@Value`, at bind time for the binder, on-demand for `resolve_placeholders`). Never blocks, never awaits, no IO — escaping/de-escaping is in-memory char work, safe to call from any async context without a spawn. Cancellation is irrelevant (synchronous, bounded by input length). Errors: a malformed/unterminated placeholder is the property-resolution layer's concern, but THIS feature adds two diagnostic-bearing failure inputs in STRICT mode — an escape char immediately before end-of-input, and (if the framework chooses to validate) an unrecognized escape — which must surface via the unified rich-diagnostic path (charter §9) carrying the original text + the offending column. In lenient mode these pass through verbatim (escape preserved) consistent with Spring.

#### Two-stage lex-then-expand: a configurable tokenizer emits an escape-resolved token stream the existing recursive expander consumes
Split the concern cleanly in two. STAGE 1 (this feature) is a `PlaceholderLexer` parameterized by the same `PlaceholderSyntax` config, but instead of expanding, it produces a flat `Vec<Token>` / iterator of `Token::Literal(Cow<str>)`, `Token::Open`, `Token::Sep`, `Token::Close` where ALL escapes have already been resolved into Literals (an escaped `\${` becomes `Token::Literal("${")`, an escaped `\:` inside a body becomes part of a key Literal, never a `Token::Sep`). STAGE 2 is property-resolution's existing recursion/default/cycle-detection engine, rewritten to consume tokens rather than re-scan raw chars — so it NEVER sees an escape and is structurally incapable of mis-splitting. The grammar config thus lives entirely in the lexer; the expander becomes escape-agnostic and delimiter-agnostic. API feel: `PlaceholderLexer::new(&syntax).tokens(text)` → fed into `expander.expand(tokens, &resolver, mode)`. Recursion re-lexes a resolved value with the SAME syntax before re-expanding (preserving Spring's 'resolved values are re-scanned, and escapes in them are honoured' contract), so the lexer is the single choke-point for all grammar rules at every recursion depth.

**Pros:** Clean separation of concerns: the grammar/escape rules live in ONE place (the lexer); the walk/cycle/default/strict logic stays pure and can't accidentally re-interpret an escaped delimiter — eliminates the whole class of 'escape leaked into the expander' bugs; Highly testable in isolation: feed text + syntax, assert the token stream — property tests target the grammar boundary directly without the recursion machinery; The expander becomes simpler and delimiter-agnostic, arguably a NET simplification of property-resolution's existing scanner (charter §11 unification: one canonical token shape both this feature and the walk share); Natural home for rich diagnostics: the lexer knows exact spans of every Open/Sep/Close and every escape, so strict-mode errors and origin-tracking column info are produced at the lexing boundary with full context; Trivially supports disabling escape (lexer just never recognizes the escape token) and reconfiguring delimiters (lexer matches different strings) with zero change to the expander

**Cons:** Materializing a token Vec costs an allocation per resolve even on the common no-placeholder path — defeats the Cow zero-alloc fast path unless the lexer returns a borrowing iterator (doable but adds lifetime complexity to the token type, since Literals must borrow the source); Re-lexing each resolved value at every recursion step repeats tokenization work the inline scanner could fuse — more total passes over recursive text (cold path, so acceptable, but a real cost); Heavier refactor of the EXISTING property-resolution expander (it must be rewritten to consume tokens), so this feature's design bleeds into a neighbour's already-settled internals — more coordination, larger blast radius; Two representations of 'the same scan' (lexer + expander) risk drift if the token contract isn't airtight

**Mechanisms considered:** Two-stage tokenizer + escape-agnostic recursive expander (the 'parse-to-tokens-then-evaluate' point on property-resolution's stated divergent axis); Borrowing token iterator (Token<'a> with Cow literals) to recover a near-zero-alloc path; Shared Token type as the unification primitive between this feature and property-resolution's walk; no global state, no codegen — runtime value transformation only

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Same as Realization A on the registry front — no link-time discovery, the `PlaceholderSyntax` and `PlaceholderLexer` are plain kernel types constructed at the bootstrap seam. The cross-crate consideration is sharper here in a GOOD way: because the Token contract is the single grammar boundary, it is the natural place to define a STABLE public token ABI that `config-data` loaders or a future dynamic (WASM-module) config source could emit directly — a dynamic source that already knows where its escapes are could hand pre-lexed tokens across the boundary, keeping the provider origin-agnostic (a WASM layer need not re-implement escape scanning). That makes the token stream a candidate cross-crate interchange shape, not just an internal one — surfaced as a possibility, not a decision.

**T2 — execution context:** Identical execution profile to A: synchronous, CPU-bound, non-blocking, no IO, runs in whatever context property-resolution runs in. The token Vec allocation (if not using the borrowing iterator) is short-lived heap on the resolve path — acceptable on cold/bind paths, weighed against the Cow path for the hot `@Value` literal case. Lexing is the diagnostic-rich point: an unterminated placeholder, a trailing escape, or a span-tagged malformed token are caught here with precise column info for the strict-mode rich-diagnostic (charter §9). No cancellation concern (bounded synchronous work).

#### Grammar-config as a compiled matcher object + escape rule, attached to the Environment as immutable sealed policy (delimiter-set precompiled once)
Treat the placeholder grammar as a COMPILED, immutable policy object owned by the `environment` seal seam, not as ad-hoc strings re-compared on every char. At environment-seal time, build a `CompiledPlaceholderGrammar` from the configured `PlaceholderSyntax` — precompute the prefix/suffix/separator/escape matchers into a cheap branchless dispatch (e.g. first-byte dispatch table + length-checked compare, or a tiny DFA for the multi-char delimiters), and freeze it. The resolver borrows `&CompiledPlaceholderGrammar` (one per environment, immutable, shareable without Arc churn since it's `&'static`-able or owned by the sealed environment). The scan itself can be either inline (as A) or token-based (as B) — this realization is ORTHOGONAL on that axis and instead nails down WHERE the configurability lives and HOW it's frozen: the three configurable knobs (prefix/suffix, separator, escape) are read ONCE from the property stack itself (Spring reads `spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default` as a property), compiled, sealed, and thereafter immutable — so the grammar config is bootstrapped from config but can never be a runtime-mutable global. API feel: `ConfigurableEnvironment::set_placeholder_grammar(PlaceholderSyntax)` is on the MUTATE seam (bootstrap-only), `PropertyResolver` (read seam) only ever sees the frozen `&CompiledPlaceholderGrammar`. Disabling escape / changing separator is a bootstrap-time policy decision, not a per-call flag.

**Pros:** Strongest fit with the existing `environment` read/mutate-segregation invariant: grammar config is a MUTATE-seam, bootstrap-only concern (you can't reshape the grammar from application code), exactly mirroring 'who-may-mutate' for property ordering — conceptual economy with the environment design; Bootstrapping the escape char/separator FROM the property stack matches Spring precisely (it's `spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default`), and the 'activation is itself a property' unification the environment design already established for profiles; Compiled-once matcher amortizes delimiter matching across millions of resolves — branchless first-byte dispatch is faster than naive substring compare on the hot path (low-overhead #2), and multi-char/custom delimiters cost nothing extra at scan time; Immutable sealed policy → safely shared across threads with no lock and minimal/no Arc (charter §5 memory discipline); no global mutable knob anywhere; Cleanly origin-agnostic: the grammar is the same object regardless of whether values came from native, file, env, or a future WASM source

**Cons:** Chicken-and-egg bootstrap ordering: the escape char is read FROM a property, but reading properties needs a resolver, which needs the grammar — so the grammar that resolves `spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default` itself must use a DEFAULT grammar, then re-seal. This staged self-reference is a real ordering wrinkle that must be specified (Spring resolves it by reading the control key with the default grammar early); Heaviest of the three — introduces a compiled-grammar artifact and a seal step that A (a borrowed struct) and B (a lexer) don't need; risks over-engineering a feature that may only ever use the `${...}`/`:`/`\` defaults in 99% of apps (charter cautions against gold-plating); Precompiled DFA/dispatch for delimiters is premature optimization unless profiling shows substring-compare is hot — the matching cost is tiny relative to the actual source-stack lookup it guards; Couples this feature tightly to the `environment` seal lifecycle, so its design can't be finalized until environment's seal/customize seam is pinned (a sequencing dependency)

**Mechanisms considered:** Compiled immutable grammar policy object sealed at environment bootstrap (the 'where the mode/diagnostic policy is carried' axis from property-resolution); Reading the escape/separator config from the property stack itself (Spring's spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default), bootstrapped with a default grammar then re-sealed; First-byte dispatch table / tiny DFA as the delimiter matcher (optional optimization, orthogonal to A/B scan style); environment mutate-seam for setting grammar vs read-seam only seeing the frozen grammar — least-privilege, no global mutable state; no link-time/proc-macro mechanism — runtime config bootstrapped from the property stack

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** The grammar is bootstrapped from the property stack, which `config-data` and `property-sources` populate across crates — so the escape-char/separator config can itself arrive from any crate's config file or env var (`SPRING_PLACEHOLDER_ESCAPECHARACTER_DEFAULT`-analogue), discovered via the ordinary first-source-wins stack rather than any bespoke registry. No linker/DCE exposure. The cross-crate contract is that the grammar is sealed ONCE at the environment level for the whole context (and child contexts inherit/merge the parent's grammar unless they re-seal, paralleling the environment child-wins merge) — so a downstream crate cannot silently introduce a conflicting grammar; if it needs a different one it must do so on its own (child) context. This keeps the grammar a single-origin, traceable policy consistent with never-blend.

**T2 — execution context:** Two distinct execution phases. SEAL phase (cold, bootstrap): reads the control properties (sync, may touch env/file sources that `config-data` already loaded synchronously into the stack — no async IO at seal since the stack is already materialized), compiles the grammar, freezes it; this is a one-time startup cost on the refresh path, fail-fast with a rich diagnostic if the configured escape char or delimiter is malformed (e.g. empty prefix). RESOLVE phase (hot/warm): borrows the frozen grammar, fully synchronous and non-blocking exactly as A/B. The frozen grammar is `Send + Sync` and immutable so it crosses `.await` points and threads freely with no lock and no cancellation hazard. The only execution subtlety is the staged self-reference at seal (default-grammar-then-reseal), which is strictly synchronous and bounded.

### Rust clashes
- Spring carries the escape char / separator / prefix-suffix as MUTABLE instance fields on a `PropertyPlaceholderHelper` / `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer` bean, and even a JVM-global default (`spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default` read via a static). Rust has no comfortable home for a runtime-mutable global grammar knob without violating the charter's no-global-mutable-state discipline (§5) — the config must be a sealed immutable value threaded through the resolver, not a settable static.
- Spring's escape semantics depend on the JVM String already having been through one layer of escaping by the file/properties parser, producing the notorious 'doubled backslash' (`${sub\\://host}` in a .properties file). In Rust the `config-data` loaders are separate crates that own their own format-level unescaping, so the LAYERING of 'who unescapes what' must be an explicit cross-crate contract — there is no single JVM Properties.load() doing it implicitly. Getting this boundary wrong means a backslash is consumed twice or zero times.
- Spring's lenient default (an unresolved or escaped construct silently passes through as literal text) is a Java-idiomatic foot-gun that the charter §9 (rich, early, complete diagnostics) actively resists. The escape feature INTERACTS with strict/lenient: in strict mode a trailing/dangling escape or a malformed escaped delimiter should arguably be a rich error, but Spring just passes it through — so leaf must decide whether escaping inherits strictness or is always lenient, a divergence from Spring's pass-everything-through behaviour.
- Spring reconfigures the placeholder prefix/suffix by registering a DIFFERENT `PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer` BEAN (a runtime container mechanism). leaf has no bean-as-grammar-source idiom at the property layer (the environment is sealed before most beans exist); the configurability must be a bootstrap/environment-seal concern, not a bean wired into the container — an inversion of Spring's mechanism.
- Java's `char` escape is a single UTF-16 code unit; Rust's `char` is a Unicode scalar value and string indexing is byte-based. A multi-byte escape char or multi-char delimiter (`%{`) requires careful char-boundary handling in the scanner that the JVM's code-unit model hides — an easy source of panics/incorrect splits if the scanner indexes by byte naively.

### Interactions
- **property-resolution** — PARENT / owns the host walk this feature parameterizes. property-resolution owns the placeholder walk (defaults, recursion, cycle-detection, partial-text, strict/lenient, read interface); this feature supplies the escape rule + delimiter configurability that the walk must consult. The two MUST share one scanner/expander — this feature is explicitly NOT a parallel engine (charter §11). The contract: de-escaping happens before key/default split and before placeholder-open detection, and resolved values re-scanned during recursion are re-subjected to the same escape rules. This feature's design likely modifies property-resolution's internals (a borrowed PlaceholderSyntax param, or a token boundary), so the two designs must converge.
- **relaxed-binding** — ORDERING TENSION at the key boundary. relaxed-binding canonicalizes a placeholder's KEY before lookup (the asymmetric-placeholder gotcha: only the canonical kebab form works in ${...}). This feature DE-ESCAPES the key first (an escaped `\:` must not be split as a separator) and THEN hands the de-escaped raw key to canonicalization. The precedence — de-escape → split key/default → canonicalize key → relaxed lookup — must be fixed jointly; a `:` that survives escaping is part of the key identity that relaxed-binding then normalizes.
- **config-data** — SOURCE-LEVEL ESCAPE LAYERING contract. config-data's .properties/.yaml/env: loaders own format-level unescaping; this feature's placeholder escape operates on the POST-parse string. The doubled-backslash rule (`${sub\://host}` in a file) only makes sense as a composition of file-escape + placeholder-escape. The two crates must agree on who consumes which backslash and document it, or values containing `:`/`${` from files are mis-parsed.
- **environment** — CONFIG-OWNERSHIP / SEAL SEAM. The escape char, separator, and prefix/suffix are grammar POLICY. Realization C puts them on the environment mutate-seam (bootstrap-only, sealed, read-seam never mutates), mirroring environment's read/mutate segregation and 'who-may-mutate'. Even in A/B the grammar config is owned/constructed at the environment seal point. Spring reads spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default as a property, so the config can itself ride the property stack — the environment unification.
- **value-injection** — CONSUMER at the injection point. @Value strings are the most common place a literal `${` or `:` must be escaped (URL templates, time formats, regexes in @Value("...")). This feature's escape rule is what lets a @Value carry a literal placeholder-looking string. The strict/lenient policy @Value carries flows into whether a dangling escape errors. (Note the Kotlin-style `\${` double-escape gotcha is analogous to how a Rust macro literal might need escaping at the @Value attribute site.)
- **origin-tracking** — DIAGNOSTIC SUPPLIER. When strict-mode escaping fails (trailing escape, malformed escaped delimiter) the rich diagnostic (charter §9) needs the value's origin (file+line/column). The lexer/scanner knows the offending COLUMN within the value; origin-tracking supplies the WHERE (which source/file/line). The two compose to produce 'escaped placeholder malformed at application.yaml line 12, column 8'.
- **expression-language** — GRAMMAR-COEXISTENCE. @Value composes `${...}` (this engine) with `#{SpEL}` (expression-language), resolved ${...} first then #{...}. The escape mechanisms must not collide — escaping a `$` (Spring/Kotlin `\${`) vs escaping a `#` for the expression layer are SEPARATE escape domains. The boundary between which engine owns which escape character must be coordinated so a value can carry a literal `#{` or `${` independently.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Where does the placeholder GRAMMAR config (escape char, value-separator, prefix/suffix) live in the unified model — a sealed-at-environment immutable policy (Realization C), a borrowed value-type threaded through the resolver (A), or part of the lexer (B)? This depends on the deferred 'unified error/diagnostics model' and the scope/ownership model (how immutable policy is shared without Arc churn).
- Should escaping inherit the strict/lenient mode (a dangling/malformed escape = rich error in strict, pass-through in lenient), or always be lenient like Spring? This is a §9-vs-Spring-parity tension that touches the unified diagnostics model and must be decided alongside property-resolution's strict/lenient policy, not in isolation.
- Is the placeholder Token stream (Realization B) the right cross-cutting UNIFICATION primitive shared between property-resolution's walk, this escape feature, config-data loaders, and a potential dynamic/WASM config source — i.e. should the grammar boundary be a stable token ABI? This is a charter §11 unification candidate spanning four features.
- How do the SEPARATE escape domains of the `${...}` placeholder engine and the `#{SpEL}` expression engine compose at a single @Value site without their escape characters colliding — is there one unified expression/placeholder escaping policy or two independent ones? Cross-cuts expression-language, value-injection, and this feature.
- Who owns the de-escape ordering across the full pipeline (file-format unescape in config-data → placeholder de-escape here → key/default split → relaxed-binding canonicalization → first-source-wins lookup)? The exact stage ordering is a cross-feature contract spanning config-data, relaxed-binding, property-resolution, and this feature that no single Phase-1 design can fix alone.

### Notes
VERIFIED against live Spring docs (context7 /spring-projects/spring-framework, Framework 6.2 release notes): the placeholder parser was REWRITTEN in 6.2 for leniency; default escape char is `\` configurable via `spring.placeholder.escapeCharacter.default` and explicitly DISABLE-able; literal-placeholder escape `\${test}` → `${test}`; separator escape `${sub\://host}` (doubled backslash `${sub\\://host}` in a .properties file because the file parser consumes one); prefix/suffix reconfigured by declaring a differently-configured PropertySourcesPlaceholderConfigurer (e.g. `%{...}`); the value-separator `:` is itself configurable. All four sub-behaviours from the feature INTENT are confirmed. KEY FRAMING: this is correctly scoped as the 5th sub-behaviour of the placeholder GRAMMAR that property-resolution's design omitted — NOT a new subsystem; every realization parameterizes the EXISTING walk rather than forking it (charter §11). NO cross-crate registry/link-time mechanism is needed for the core feature (it is pure runtime value-resolution config), which keeps it clear of the DCE/reachability hazards in the cross-crate doc; the only cross-crate concern is the source-level-escape LAYERING contract with config-data and the optional token-ABI unification candidate. Grounding files read: charter /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/superpowers/specs/2026-06-14-leaf-charter-design.md; Spring intent /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/spring-boot-4-di-design.md (lines 800-804 @Value/lenient, 1281-1316 resolver/stack, 1443 null-never-overrides, 1539-1543 relaxed-binding placeholder asymmetry); Rust capability /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-nightly-features-tradeoffs.md (Cow/char-scanner stable, no nightly needed); cross-crate /home/jan/projects/testing/leaf/docs/research/rust-cross-crate-composition.md (confirms no registry needed here). One charter caution flagged: Realization C risks gold-plating given 99% of apps use the `${...}`/`:`/`\` defaults — surfaced as a con, not resolved.

## spring.config.activate.on-cloud-platform and cloud-platform detection  `[extra-14]`

probe

### Candidate realizations

#### Closed enum plus match-arm fingerprints
non_exhaustive enum CloudPlatform; is_detected reads env via the Environment seam not std::env; get_active enforces spring.main.cloud-platform else first detected; on_cloud_platform is a conditions preset.

**Pros:** faithful, zero overhead; reads assembled stack so testable; fully stable

**Cons:** leaf-closed catalog; wildcard-arm tax; K8s fallback needs enumeration

**Mechanisms considered:** enum + match; const catalog; non_exhaustive

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** No cross-crate registry; catalog in one kernel crate, immune to DCE; closed catalog trades extensibility for reliability.

**T2 — execution context:** Synchronous IO-free read over the assembled stack on the bootstrap thread before any await; only error is an unknown enforced name.

#### Open PlatformDescriptor registry
Each platform is a const PlatformDescriptor in a linkme distributed_slice; built-ins and extensions register the same way; get_active sorts by order then enforce-vs-detect; explicit builder-add fallback for wasm.

**Pros:** origin-agnostic and open; additive growth; unifies with the component registry

**Cons:** heavyweight for 7 entries; DCE empties the slice silently; by-name identity not compile-checked

**Mechanisms considered:** linkme slice; inventory submit; explicit builder fallback

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Cross-crate-heavy; subject to all three DCE layers; needs force-link, used-linker/Rust 1.89+, and a startup self-check; ordering is the order field at runtime.

**T2 — execution context:** Same read as R1; slice materializes at first access (linkme) or pre-main (inventory); detect fns are non-blocking read-only; a DCE-emptied catalog is caught by the self-check.

#### Hybrid closed-core plus thin opt-in probe
R1's closed enum is the default; plus an explicitly-installed list of PlatformProbe (name + detect) on a runtime CloudPlatformResolver; on_cloud_platform accepts a built-in variant or a name string; extras are added by the binary, never auto-discovered.

**Pros:** best DX-vs-reliability balance; origin-agnostic without the DCE tax; built-in identity stays a compile-checked enum

**Cons:** two identity kinds; small runtime state on the Environment; resolution-order rule to document

**Mechanisms considered:** closed enum core; explicit add_probe list; WASM probe via add_probe seam

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Built-ins compiled in unconditionally; the extension seam is explicit registration in the binary, sidestepping DCE/Layer-0 entirely; a WASM layer feeds probes through add_probe at runtime, origin-agnostic.

**T2 — execution context:** Synchronous IO-free read like R1/R2; probes are synchronous predicates; any async probe-LOAD happens in the bootstrap phase and installs a ready sync detect fn; probe-list mutation is bootstrap-only before the platform freezes.

### Rust clashes
- std::env vs the assembled stack: detection must read THROUGH the Environment, not std::env (which is also unsound concurrent with setenv); config-data snapshots env into a property source first.
- Spring's closed enum grows each release; in Rust this needs non_exhaustive (wildcard tax) and tensions with the origin-agnostic open-registry guardrail.
- KUBERNETES SERVICE_HOST/SERVICE_PORT-pair heuristic needs key enumeration only some sources support; must degrade explicitly not silently.
- Document-activation (property phase) and the bean condition (registration phase) share one platform that must be frozen once up-front then read lock-free, mirroring decide-profiles-up-front-then-filter.
- spring.main.cloud-platform matches enum NAMES case-insensitively; reuse relaxed-binding UNIFORM-form so cloud-foundry/CLOUD_FOUNDRY/cloudFoundry map to one identity; unknown value errors richly not silent NONE.

### Interactions
- **config-data** — PRIMARY CONSUMER 1; owns the document split and activation-filter wiring but delegates the predicate here (like on-profile to profiles); calls get_active==requested; this was the ownerless gap the critic flagged.
- **condition-family** — PRIMARY CONSUMER 2; the on-cloud-platform bean condition is one preset over the conditions SPI; this feature supplies the reusable on_cloud_platform(platform) predicate; runs at registration sharing the one active value with config-data.
- **profiles** — SIBLING not delegation; the second document-activation predicate but reuses NOTHING of the grammar (enum-equality); structural symmetry is why it needs its own owner; mirrors the two-layers-one-active-set-different-phases pattern.
- **environment** — read seam + reachability; detection/enforcement read the PropertyResolver view not std::env; in R3 the probe list sits behind the mutate seam (bootstrap-only); the active platform is a where-I-run fact like active profiles.
- **relaxed-binding** — the enforce value parses via case-insensitive UNIFORM-form normalization to one platform identity, reusing the equivalence relation.
- **property-resolution** — reads the enforce key and detection keys via first-source-wins; enforcement-over-detection rides config precedence for free; the K8s heuristic needs the enumerable variant.
- **property-sources** — depends on process env being snapshotted into an enumerable source before detection; first-source-wins lets an injected test source shadow a real env var.
- **condition-report** — a no-match document/bean records a human-readable reason (platform not detected; active NONE) so gating stays accountable, reusing the match/no-match accounting.

### Open questions for Phase 2
- Closed non_exhaustive enum vs open by-name descriptor registry for the catalog? Same question the registry-substrate and conditional-strategy keystones face; platform detection should likely inherit Phase 2's component/condition registry choice.
- Should platform detection ride the unified descriptor/condition registry (charter 11) or is a 7-entry closed catalog the case where a bespoke local solution wins?
- WHERE in the async bootstrap is the active platform frozen? After the env stack incl the enforce key is assembled, before the document-activation filter and before bean-condition evaluation; depends on the undecided bootstrap/refresh phase ordering.
- Does leaf inherit the hard rule that an activated document may not mutate the activation inputs, and is there a cloud-platform analogue of the may-not-set-spring.profiles ban (rejecting spring.main.cloud-platform set inside an activated document)?
- Does the detection predicate signature require an EnumerablePropertyResolver for the K8s pair heuristic, and how does it degrade on on-demand sources without a silent wrong answer?
- Should the active platform be a first-class readable Environment fact (injectable like active profiles) or strictly internal? Spring exposes getActive(env) publicly; affects the environment read-view surface.

### Notes
Grounding: Spring Boot 4 CloudPlatform.java via WebFetch + doc anchors 1120/1146/1489-1491/1505-1507. Signals: CLOUD_FOUNDRY=VCAP_APPLICATION|VCAP_SERVICES; HEROKU=DYNO; SAP=HC_LANDSCAPE; NOMAD=NOMAD_ALLOC_ID; KUBERNETES=KUBERNETES_SERVICE_HOST+KUBERNETES_SERVICE_PORT (+SERVICE_HOST/SERVICE_PORT-suffixed pair fallback); AZURE_APP_SERVICE=all of WEBSITE_SITE_NAME/INSTANCE_ID/RESOURCE_GROUP/SKU; AWS_ECS(v4)=AWS_EXECUTION_ENV starts-with AWS_ECS; NONE=false. Enforcement: spring.main.cloud-platform overrides; is_active=enforced-match OR (absent AND is_detected); get_active=first active in catalog order. v4 added AWS_ECS, NOMAD added 3.1. context7 MCP was unusable (conflicting required-arg schema); used WebFetch. Three realizations span the cross-crate spectrum (R1 closed/explicit, R2 open registry, R3 hybrid). No meta-architecture chosen; detection kept synchronous, IO-free, over the assembled stack.

## EnvironmentConverter: re-typing the Environment when the deduced application type changes  `[extra-15]`

Spring's `EnvironmentConverter` (invoked at the tail of `SpringApplication.prepareEnvironment`, doc line 258) handles a mid-bootstrap hazard: the `Environment` is created EARLY, before the application type is finally fixed. The default `StandardEnvironment` is built first (so `EnvironmentPostProcessor`s / ConfigData loading and `bindToSpringApplication` can run against a live environment), but the deduced application type (plain / servlet / reactive — chosen from the classpath via `WebApplicationType.deduce()`, then possibly OVERRIDDEN by `spring.main.web-application-type` bound back from config during `bindToSpringApplication`) may require a DIFFERENTLY-TYPED environment whose default-source SEEDING differs (e.g. `StandardServletEnvironment` prepends `servletConfig`/`servletContext`/`jndiProperties` ahead of `systemProperties`/`systemEnvironment`, per doc line 1307). When the live environment's concrete type does NOT match the deduced type, Spring `EnvironmentConverter.convertEnvironmentIfNecessary` constructs a fresh environment of the right subtype and TRANSPLANTS the existing state into it: (1) it COPIES the existing `MutablePropertySources` stack — preserving order and the per-source instances, removing only the convention-named defaults that the new type will re-seed itself — and (2) it COPIES the active and default PROFILE sets, then (3) re-attaches `ConfigurationPropertySources` (the relaxed-binding adapter source) onto the new stack. The conceptual load: the environment is built ONCE conceptually, but its TYPE is provisional until the application type is finally pinned, so the framework needs a state-preserving way to migrate the WHOLE source stack + profile sets from a provisionally-typed instance into a finally-typed one without re-running config loading. The intent leaf must replicate (intent, NOT Spring's `Class`-subtype-swap mechanism): a deployment-flavor-specific environment SEEDING is decided during bootstrap; if the seeding chosen early turns out wrong, the accumulated source stack + active/default profiles must transplant LOSSLESSLY into the correct seeding, with the relaxed-binding/config-property-sources adapter re-attached afterward — and this must NOT re-load config, NOT re-order user-contributed sources, and NOT lose origin/provenance.

### Candidate realizations

#### Flavor-as-data: re-seed the stack, never re-type the struct (transplant by stack splice)
There is exactly ONE concrete `Environment` value type in leaf (the `environment` feature already mandates one owned environment). 'Deployment flavor' (Plain / Servlet / Reactive / WASM-host / custom) is NOT a distinct struct type — it is a `Flavor` value (an open enum-ish descriptor) that determines only ONE thing: WHICH default property sources get seeded and in what relative order at the BOTTOM of the stack. So 'conversion' becomes a pure stack operation, not a struct swap. Bootstrap flow: (1) build the single `Environment` with a PROVISIONAL flavor seeding (the safest superset/plain seeding); (2) run config-data loading, profile activation, and the `spring.main.*` bind-back which may FINALLY pin the flavor; (3) call `environment.reseed_flavor(final_flavor)`: it removes ONLY the sources tagged with the well-known `FlavorSeed` provenance marker (a coarse origin from `origin-tracking`), then asks the new flavor's `FlavorSeeder` to contribute its default sources via the SAME ordering-mutation vocabulary `property-sources` already owns (`add_first`/`add_before(name)`/`add_last`). User-contributed and config-data sources are NEVER touched — they are not flavor-seeds. Active/default profile sets are already held in the one environment and are untouched by definition. (4) re-attach the `ConfigurationPropertySources` adapter (relaxed-binding source) which `property-sources` lets you `replace(name, src)` against the new stack. A `FlavorSeeder` is an origin-agnostic provider: `trait FlavorSeeder { fn seed(&self, sources: &mut PropertySources); }` — a native servlet/reactive integration crate registers one; a WASM-host module could register one identically. API feel: `app.set_flavor(Flavor::Reactive)` is the escape hatch (Spring's `setWebApplicationType`); default is deduce-then-bind-back.

**Pros:** No struct re-typing at all — sidesteps Rust's lack of subtype reflection entirely; the 'conversion' is just three ordering-mutation calls the stack already supports, so transplant is LOSSLESS by construction (the stack is never rebuilt, sources keep identity + origin).; Honors charter #11 (unification): re-uses `property-sources` ordering vocabulary, `origin-tracking` provenance to tag seeds, and the `profiles` active-set holder — adds almost no new machinery, just a `Flavor` tag + a `FlavorSeeder` provider + a tagged-removal helper.; Flavor is a runtime value over collected descriptors, exactly matching the cross-crate doc's guidance to keep all conditional/flavor logic in RUNTIME evaluation, not link-time/pre-main. Servlet/reactive/WASM seedings slot in as origin-agnostic providers.; Stays stable-by-default and stack-friendly: no `dyn` upcast tricks, no `Any` downcast of the environment, minimal allocation (only the seed sources move).

**Cons:** The single environment type must hold a UNION of any per-flavor state (e.g. a servlet flavor wanting a typed handle to a servlet-context-like object); a flavor that needs MORE than 'extra default sources' (genuinely flavor-specific typed fields) doesn't fit cleanly and would force an escape-hatch extension map — re-introducing some of the heterogeneity this avoids.; Requires a reliable convention for tagging flavor-seeded sources (well-known names or an origin marker) so re-seed removes exactly the right ones and nothing user-contributed; a bug here silently drops/duplicates a default source.; 'Provisional then re-seed' means the seed sources briefly sit at the wrong place; any consumer that read a flavor-specific default BETWEEN seed and re-seed (shouldn't happen pre-context, but EnvironmentPostProcessors run in that window) could observe the transient — needs the re-seed to be the LAST bootstrap step (mirrors Spring doing it at the tail of prepareEnvironment).

**Mechanisms considered:** runtime registry of FlavorSeeder providers (origin-agnostic dyn trait); linkme/inventory link-time registration of native FlavorSeeders (so servlet/reactive crates self-register); origin-tracking coarse-origin marker to identify flavor-seed sources for safe removal; property-sources ordering-mutation vocabulary as the transplant primitive (no new mechanism)

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Flavor SEEDERS are the cross-crate contributions: a `leaf-servlet`/`leaf-reactive` integration crate (or a WASM-host crate) registers a `FlavorSeeder` for its flavor. This is the classic discovery duality from the cross-crate doc: a static link-time registry (linkme distributed slice of `&'static FlavorSeederDescriptor`, the default 'classpath') PLUS an optional dynamic directory-scanned source (a WASM-host module exporting a seeder world, the loadable 'classpath' from the wasm-modules exploration) — CANDIDATES, not a decision. DCE hazard applies: a flavor crate added to Cargo.toml but never path-referenced may not link and its seeder silently vanishes (empty registry), so the deduce-flavor step must cross-check (charter #9 rich diagnostic: 'flavor Reactive deduced but no FlavorSeeder registered — did you add leaf-reactive AND force-link it?'). The base/plain seeding lives in the kernel so it always exists. The `FlavorSeeder` ABI (fn over `&mut PropertySources`) must be minimal and ultra-stable since emitted registration code hard-codes its path. Flavor DEDUCTION itself ('which flavor does the binary want') is a runtime query over the resolved feature set — must be documented to differ between `-p` and `--workspace` builds.

**T2 — execution context:** Runs ENTIRELY in the synchronous bootstrap/cold path, at the tail of the prepare-environment step, on the bootstrap thread before the context exists and before any eager singleton — so no `.await`, no hot path, no concurrency: the environment is still solely owned by the bootstrap driver (no `Arc` sharing yet). `FlavorSeeder::seed` MUST NOT block or do IO (it only computes/inserts default source descriptors; any IO-backed source, e.g. reading servlet config, must already be a lazily-resolved source, not eager IO here) — this keeps it off life-before-main and off the async runtime. Cancellation is trivial (whole bootstrap aborts → environment dropped). Errors propagate as a rich bootstrap-failure diagnostic (a flavor whose seeder collides on a reserved source name, or a deduced flavor with no registered seeder) — fail-fast at boot per charter #9, naming the flavor, the missing/colliding seeder, and the offending source name.

#### Typed flavor environments behind a thin façade + an explicit Transplant trait (state-extraction migration)
Keep the `environment` feature's read/mutate-segregated FAÇADE as the stable surface (`PropertyResolver` read view, `ConfigurableEnvironment` mutate view), but allow MULTIPLE concrete backing structs per flavor — `PlainEnvironment`, `ServletEnvironment`, `ReactiveEnvironment` — each a normal type that owns the shared `PropertySources` + profile sets PLUS its own flavor-specific fields. The façade holds the backing as `Box<dyn EnvironmentBacking>` (or an enum over the known flavors for the closed/core set). 'Conversion' is an explicit, lossless TRANSPLANT: `trait Transplant { fn into_parts(self) -> EnvironmentParts; }` where `EnvironmentParts { sources: PropertySources, active: ActiveProfiles, default: DefaultProfiles }` is the portable, flavor-agnostic core. The new flavor's constructor takes `EnvironmentParts` and re-seeds its own defaults around them: `ReactiveEnvironment::from_parts(parts)`. So conversion = `let parts = old.into_parts(); let new = make_flavor(final_flavor, parts);` then re-attach ConfigurationPropertySources on `new`. The shared core (`EnvironmentParts`) is what Spring transplants; the flavor wrapper is what differs. The `make_flavor` step consults an origin-agnostic `EnvironmentFactory` registry keyed by flavor (mirrors Spring's `ApplicationContextFactory` also supplying the matching environment type, doc line 254). API feel: closely mirrors Spring's `EnvironmentConverter.convertEnvironmentIfNecessary(existing, targetType)` but `into_parts`/`from_parts` makes the transplant EXPLICIT and total rather than reflective field-copying.

**Pros:** Cleanly supports genuinely flavor-specific TYPED state (a reactive scheduler handle, a servlet-context analogue) that realization 1 cannot — each flavor is a real type with its own fields, closest to Spring's intent of distinct environment subtypes.; The `into_parts`/`from_parts` transplant is provably lossless and total at COMPILE time: the portable core `EnvironmentParts` is a concrete struct the compiler forces every flavor to surrender and reconstruct, so 'preserve sources + profiles' is structurally guaranteed (no convention-tagging fragility of realization 1).; Maps almost 1:1 onto Spring's mental model (distinct environment types + a converter), so intent-parity is high and the escape hatch (`set_environment_factory`) mirrors `setApplicationContextFactory` exactly.; The flavor backing being origin-agnostic (`Box<dyn EnvironmentBacking>`) lets a dynamic WASM-host flavor slot in beside native ones without the core knowing.

**Cons:** `Box<dyn EnvironmentBacking>` behind the façade adds an indirection + heap allocation to the ONE environment and pushes toward dynamic dispatch on a structure the charter wants stack-friendly and minimally-`Arc`'d; the closed-enum alternative avoids the box but then the core can't accept a third-party/WASM flavor without a variant — tension between openness and the no-gratuitous-`dyn`/heap discipline (charter #5).; Two surfaces to keep in sync (the façade trait + N backing structs); every façade method must delegate, and any flavor-specific field is invisible through the least-privilege façade, so consumers needing it must downcast — re-introducing `Any`-style downcast the charter would rather avoid.; More machinery than realization 1: a backing trait, an `EnvironmentParts` carrier, a flavor-keyed factory registry, AND the transplant trait — heavier for a feature whose 99% case is 'plain, never converts'.; `into_parts(self)` consumes the old environment; any handle already handed out (e.g. to an EnvironmentPostProcessor that ran earlier) is invalidated — must guarantee conversion happens before ANY sharing, same window constraint as realization 1 but with a harder ownership edge.

**Mechanisms considered:** enum-over-known-flavors backing (closed core set, zero dyn) vs Box<dyn EnvironmentBacking> (open, third-party/WASM flavors); explicit Transplant/into_parts/from_parts trait pair as the lossless migration primitive; origin-agnostic EnvironmentFactory registry keyed by flavor (linkme link-time default + optional dynamic dir-scanned for WASM); trait upcasting (stable 1.86) to expose the read façade from a flavor backing, Any-downcast for flavor-specific fields

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Flavor BACKINGS + their `EnvironmentFactory`s are the cross-crate contributions, registered the same dual way as realization 1 (static linkme distributed slice of factory descriptors = default 'classpath'; optional dynamic directory-scanned WASM-host factories = loadable 'classpath'). The closed-enum variant keeps Plain in the kernel and would require servlet/reactive to be core variants (closes the set — bad for the charter's pluggable-everything #6); the `Box<dyn>` variant keeps the core open but inherits the full DCE/Layer-0 silent-vanish risk (a flavor backing crate not force-linked → its factory missing → deduced flavor unconstructible). The `EnvironmentBacking`/`EnvironmentFactory` ABI must be minimal + ultra-stable since registration code in sibling crates hard-codes its path. Trait-object self-registration of factories may favor `inventory` (runtime-computed) over `linkme` (const-only) here. Same `-p` vs `--workspace` deduction caveat.

**T2 — execution context:** Same cold synchronous bootstrap window as realization 1 — conversion happens once, pre-context, on the bootstrap thread, before any `Arc` sharing or `.await`. `from_parts` MUST be non-blocking and IO-free (flavor-specific handles that need IO must be lazily initialized, not eagerly built during transplant). The `into_parts(self)` move is the explicit cancellation/error seam: if `make_flavor` fails (no factory for deduced flavor, or a flavor ctor rejects the parts), the bootstrap fails fast with a rich diagnostic; because `into_parts` already consumed the old environment, the failure path must reconstruct or report rather than silently leave a half-migrated state — the total `EnvironmentParts` carrier makes 'what we have to put back' explicit. No hot-path or cancellation-across-await concern (all pre-runtime).

#### Deduce-flavor-first: pin the flavor before the environment exists, eliminating conversion (with a fallback rebind)
Challenge the premise that the environment must be built before the flavor is known. Spring builds it early ONLY because `EnvironmentPostProcessor`s / config-data must run against a live environment, and the FINAL flavor (`spring.main.web-application-type`) can be set from config that those processors load — a genuine chicken-and-egg. leaf can SPLIT this: do a CHEAP, two-phase flavor resolution. Phase A (pre-environment): deduce a PROVISIONAL flavor from the resolved feature set / registered seeders (the 'classpath' analogue) — this is a pure runtime query, no environment needed. Build the environment ALREADY seeded for the provisional flavor. Phase B (post-config, the bind-back): re-evaluate the flavor against `leaf.main.flavor` now that config is loaded; in the OVERWHELMING common case the provisional == final, so NO conversion happens at all. ONLY when the bind-back actually OVERRIDES the deduced flavor do we fall back to a transplant (delegating to realization 1's `reseed_flavor`, the minimal stack-splice). So this realization is a CONTROL-FLOW design that makes conversion the rare exception, not a per-boot step, and confines the transplant to the single override case. It frames the feature primarily as 'flavor resolution ordering' and only secondarily as 'transplant', with the transplant being whichever of realization 1/2 is chosen in Phase 3. API feel: `Flavor::deduce(&resolved_providers)` up front; `bind_back_flavor(&env)` after config; convert only if they differ, with a logged diagnostic explaining the override ('flavor Servlet deduced from registered seeders, overridden to Reactive by leaf.main.flavor in application.yaml:7').

**Pros:** Makes the COMMON case zero-cost: no transplant on a normal boot (provisional flavor is correct), directly serving charter #1/#2 (low overhead, intent-parity without paying for a rare migration every time) — Spring effectively does conversion every time the early default mismatches; leaf can avoid even that.; Confines the genuinely hard transplant to the single override path, so the expensive correctness burden (lossless source/profile migration) is paid only when a user explicitly overrides flavor via config — and that path is loud/diagnosable.; The override is exactly where a rich diagnostic adds the most value (charter #9): 'deduced X, you overrode to Y in <origin> — here is what re-seeded'; provenance from origin-tracking makes the explanation precise.; Separates concerns cleanly: this feature owns the WHEN/ordering of flavor pinning; the actual transplant mechanism is delegated to whichever of realization 1/2 Phase 3 picks — it composes with either rather than competing.

**Cons:** Does not by ITSELF solve the transplant — it still needs realization 1 or 2 underneath for the override case, so on its own it's an incomplete answer to 'how is the stack transplanted' (it answers 'how often').; Two-phase flavor resolution adds bootstrap-ordering complexity and a subtle invariant: the provisional flavor's seeding must be a SAFE superset/base so that, in the no-override case, nothing the provisional seeded is wrong — if provisional seeding can be actively harmful for the final flavor, the 'skip conversion' optimization is unsound.; If config-data loading itself behaves differently per flavor (e.g. a servlet flavor adds servlet-context as a config source that influences which profiles activate), then deducing provisional-then-overriding could load config against the wrong source set — a recursion Spring sidesteps by always using a plain environment for the early phase; leaf must prove flavor doesn't affect config LOADING, only later default-source precedence.; Relies on flavor being deducible from registered seeders/feature-set BEFORE the environment — fine for the static 'classpath' but the dynamic WASM-host 'classpath' is discovered at startup (a directory scan), so a WASM-contributed flavor may not be known until after the scan, pushing the deduction point later and shrinking the window where Phase A is truly pre-environment.

**Mechanisms considered:** two-phase flavor resolution (pre-env deduce + post-config bind-back) as control flow; runtime query over the resolved feature set / registered seeder descriptors for provisional deduction; linkme static seeder registry (for pre-env deduction) + optional dynamic dir-scanned WASM seeders (discovered later, complicating Phase A timing); delegation to realization 1 (stack-splice) or 2 (typed transplant) for the rare override case — composes, not competes

**T1 — cross-crate/tooling:** Provisional flavor deduction is a pure RUNTIME query over the link-time seeder/provider descriptor registry (the static 'classpath' — linkme distributed slice) resolved for THIS binary; it must be deterministic given the final resolved feature set and documented to differ between `-p` and `--workspace` (cross-crate doc). The DCE silent-vanish risk bites the deduction directly: if a flavor's seeder crate didn't link, the flavor is invisible to deduction and the binary silently runs Plain — so deduction MUST be paired with a self-check / expected-flavor assertion at boot (charter #9). The dynamic WASM-host 'classpath' (directory-scanned at startup per the wasm-modules exploration) is the timing wrinkle: a WASM-contributed flavor/seeder isn't in the link-time registry, so it surfaces only after the startup scan — this realization must define whether WASM flavors participate in provisional deduction (forcing the scan earlier, into Phase A) or only in the post-config re-evaluation (Phase B). Keep deduction origin-agnostic so static-linked and dynamically-scanned seeders are weighed uniformly.

**T2 — execution context:** Phase A deduction runs FIRST in the cold bootstrap path, synchronously, before the environment and before any async runtime — pure in-memory query over descriptors, no IO, no block, no `.await`. Phase B bind-back runs after config-data loading at the tail of prepare-environment, still synchronous/pre-context.
